# 通玄真经缵义

*The Comprehensive Annotations on the Scripture of Penetrating the Mysteries*

**Scripture Title:** *Comprehensive Annotations on the Scripture of Penetrating the Mysteries*
Compiled by Du Daojian of the Yuan dynasty, circa the third year of the Zhida era.
**Source Text:** *Daoist Canon of the Zhengtong Era*, Dongshen Section, Jade Instructions Category.
**Collated Edition:** *The Twenty-Two Masters* (photolithographic reprint of the 1877 re-engraved Ming Wuyingdian *Juzhen* edition, hereafter "Zhongzhen Edition").

*Pronunciation Guide for the Comprehensive Annotations on the Scripture of Penetrating the Mysteries*

**Wenzi**
Wenzi, surnamed Xin, given name Xing, also named Jiran, was a native of Puyang in Kuiqiu. He studied under Laozi. King Ping of Chu asked him, "I have heard that you received the Dao from Lao Dan. May I be permitted to hear of it?" Wenzi replied, "The Dao and virtue rectify the crooked to make it straight, and restore order from chaos. When pure virtue revives, the world attains peace, and the key lies in one man. Thus, the accumulation of virtue makes a king, while the accumulation of wrath leads to ruin. Yao and Shun flourished by this principle, while Jie and Zhou perished by it." The king said, "I respectfully receive your instruction."

Later, Wenzi traveled south to Wu and Yue, where Fan Li became his disciple. When Yue planned to attack Wu, Fan Li remonstrated, "Your servant has heard from his master: 'Weapons are instruments of ill omen. War is contrary to virtue. Strife is the least of affairs. To plot in secret and delight in instruments of ill omen, testing oneself in what is least important—this will not do.'" Goujian did not heed him and was defeated at Fujiao. Later, Wenzi was offered the position of Grand Minister but declined. He withdrew to Mount Yu in Wuxing. Tradition holds that he ascended on clouds. According to the *Universal Geography* and the *Gazetteer of Wuxing*, thirty *li* southeast of Yuying lies Jichou Mountain, where the Grand Minister of Yue, Jiran, once ascended to survey the terrain, hence its name. Today, the Tongxuan Abbey atop White Stone Peak on the mountain’s south face marks his former retreat. The Ziyun Pass and Shengyuan Abbey were once the ancient Changqing Abbey, renamed by imperial decree during the Qiandao era of the Song. Halfway up the mountain stands the "Cloud-Ascending Stone." Wenzi composed twelve chapters, titled *Wenzi*, and during the Tang dynasty, he was enfeoffed as the Perfected of Penetrating the Mysteries, while his book was titled *The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysteries*.

**Preface to the Comprehensive Annotations on the Scripture of Penetrating the Mysteries**
The *Wenzi* is a transmission of the *Scripture of the Dao and Its Virtue*. Laozi based his work on the *Book of Changes* to compose his text, while Wenzi modeled himself on Laozi to establish his teachings. Thus, he elucidated the Way of the Yellow Emperor and the Three Sovereigns. For those who would be rulers, he prescribed the governance of Fuxi and the Divine Farmer; for the people, he envisioned the era of Datian and Getian. His concern for the world was profound indeed. Yet later generations failed to fathom his intent, and some even slandered his work.

Now the Recluse of Nangu, Master Du, delved into the profundities of the *Book of Changes* and Laozi, harmonizing the teachings of Confucianism and Daoism, ever holding the composition of texts and the establishment of discourse as his heart’s intent. Among his works that circulated in the world was *The Original Purport of the Dao and Its Virtue* in several scrolls. Initially dwelling on Mount Jishou in Wuxing, he sought the extraordinary and inquired into antiquity, discovering the former abode of Wenzi. There, he founded the White Stone Abbey of Penetrating the Mysteries and recovered *The Complete Works of Wenzi*. Thus, he divided its chapters, parsed its sentences, compiled annotations with appended explications, enabling learners to perceive the Dao with a single glance.

I have often said that since the opening of Heaven and Earth, the celestial Way has flowed from north to south, and the sagely dynasty, tracing its origins, holds the primal destiny of the northern lands. Once the Six Directions are unified as one household, the age of primordial vastness is restored. Today, the Recluse of Nangu, responding to the mandate, composes texts to illuminate the imperial Dao, seeking to place this world within the realm of Huaxu. That a recluse of the mountains and forests does not forget the heart of aiding the ruler and benefiting the people is truly worthy of esteem. Our teaching has found its man, and thus I joyfully inscribe this preface.

In the third year of the Zhida era, on the first day of the sixth month, Wu Quanjie, the Lineage Master of the Mysterious Teaching, respectfully wrote.

In antiquity, when scholars served their families and states, they invariably had recluses beyond the world as their teachers, honing and tempering them, imparting their texts, and exhausting their teachings, so that their achievements were accomplished and their names established. Among them was the Grand General of Yue, Fan Li, whose teacher was Jiran. Jiran personally beheld the sages in the declining Zhou, yet harbored his supreme treasure without display. He thoroughly investigated the celestial Way, human affairs, the rise and fall of strength and weakness, and the principles of spontaneity, composing twelve scrolls. Fan Li applied these to pacify Wu and establish Yue’s hegemony, and later, with their remaining threads, preserved his life and enriched his household, accumulating and dispersing thrice, safeguarding his illustrious name. Observing Fan Li’s beginning and end confirms the truth of his teacher’s Way; examining Fan Li’s repeated remonstrances to Goujian reveals that they were all his teacher’s words. His work ranks among the Daoist masters.

Liu Zihou pruned its redundancies and contradictions, extracting its essence and subtleties to form his own text, which elucidated its meaning well, though regrettably it did not survive. Master Nangu, following maps, discovered the mountain of Jiran’s former residence, perched upon its towering peaks, gazing down upon the vast expanse of Lake Tai. Having built a chamber to emulate its likeness, he further took *Wenzi* to compile annotations, fusing and penetrating its depths, smoothing its ruggedness, and unfolding the hidden treasures of the Yellow Emperor and the hegemons. Alongside his *Original Purport of the Mysterious Scripture*, it now circulates in the world. The Master is a man of the Way—his purity, diligence, frugality, and non-contention yet mastery of victory deeply embody the essence of the Keeper of the Pillars. His words and deeds, as seen in the discourses of officials and commoners alike, have already been engraved in stone and printed on fine woodblocks.

Yet Sima Qian’s appraisal of Tao Zhu laments his toiling body and striving strength in deep counsel with Yue, and again notes his toiling body and striving strength to amass wealth of tens of millions, repeating himself without weariness. In this, the Master’s affairs differ, yet their merit is the same; his name is lofty, yet he does not enjoy its riches. Thus, what he has gained from Jiran’s work—how could it lie in the mere ends of written characters and sentences? Separated by a thousand years, they truly seem to align as if tallying tokens.

It is regrettable that while many eminent officials and great men know the Master, and not a few have come to his gate to inquire of the Way, they have yet to fully employ his teachings as the ancients did in statecraft. Is it that their faith in the Way is not yet sincere? Do the recluses of the mountains and forests not forget this world, while the meat-eating officials do? If not, then why do they revere our Laozi’s Way as they do?

Thus, I record the origins of Fan Li and his teacher at the head of this text, awaiting the era of Great Flourishing.

In the mid-summer of the Gengxu year, Huang Shiweng, a Daoist of Mount Lu residing at the Lodge of the Southern Perfected, inscribed this.

Those who ruled all under Heaven in antiquity: the highest practiced non-action, the next practiced action. Thus, the August One transformed through the Dao, the Emperor taught through virtue, the King exhorted through merit, and the Hegemon led through force. Their governance was like the four seasons. The celestial Way flows on its own—it is not something human effort can compel. Yet while times may be opportune for action, the Dao is never ultimately obstructed. Winter transforms, and spring endures within the year; the Hegemon transforms, and the August One endures within the ruler. This is why Wenzi composed his work, and the imperial Dao was illuminated.

Wenzi was a grandson of the state of Jin, surnamed Xin, named Xing, styled Jiran, and known by his epithet, Wenzi. His family resided in Kuiqiu of Sui, a region of Song, and he was also called Song Xing. He studied under Laozi and early attained the great Dao. He composed twelve scrolls, titled *Wenzi*, which returned to Laozi’s words, expounding the Way of Heaven and humanity, the appropriateness of temporal changes, and gathering the essence of all antiquity into a single compilation—truly the pivot and essentials of ordering the world. King Ping of Chu summoned him to inquire of the Way, and Fan Li followed him as his teacher, with Goujian appointing him as a grandee. Assisting Yue in pacifying Wu, he achieved merit without claiming it, retiring to the lands of Mount Yu, where he ascended the clouds and departed as an immortal. The region of Jishou in Wuxing was his former abode.

During the reign of Emperor Xuanzong of Tang, the scholar Xu Lingfu, who had secluded himself for cultivation on Mount Heng, presented Wenzi’s work to the throne. Thus, he was enfeoffed as the Perfected of Penetrating the Mysteries, and his book was titled *The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysteries*.

Born on the left bank of the Yangtze and aging in the land of Wu, I sought the remnants of Wenzi’s legacy and built the White Stone Abbey of Penetrating the Mysteries. There, I obtained Wenzi’s original compilation. In my leisure, I divided its chapters and compiled annotations, assisting in the flourishing of the mysterious wind. As for the distinctions between transformation and teaching, exhortation and leading, the merit of virtue and the efficacy of force—these are not without hope for the world’s great worthies.

The humble disciple, Du Daojian of Nangu in Dangtu, respectfully inscribed this preface.

**Comprehensive Annotations on the Scripture of Penetrating the Mysteries, Scroll One**
Compiled by Du Daojian, the Master of Nangu

At the beginning of each chapter, *Wenzi* frequently cites "Laozi says," out of reverence for his teacher. This likely records Laozi’s words as they were learned at the time, and thus he dared not claim authorship for himself. The work comprises twelve scrolls, totaling one hundred and eighty-eight chapters. Though I, Daojian, am of shallow and crude understanding, I have divided them according to their meanings, adding eighty-one chapters. Each chapter is distinguished by its purport and titled *Comprehensive Annotations* to facilitate perusal.

**Chapter on the Origin of the Dao**

Laozi says: There is a thing formed in chaos, born before Heaven and Earth. It has appearance but no form, profound and obscure, silent and serene. No sound is heard from it. I force a name upon it and call it the Dao.

Now, the Dao is lofty beyond reach, deep beyond measure. It embraces Heaven and Earth, bestowing without form. Its source flows ceaselessly, empty yet never full. When turbid, it slowly clarifies through stillness. Its giving is inexhaustible, unbound by time. Though it may be held in a single grasp, it contracts yet expands, dark yet luminous, soft yet firm. It harbors yin and exhales yang, manifesting the three lights.

Mountains rise by it, abysses deepen by it. Beasts run by it, birds fly by it. Scales glide by it, phoenixes soar by it. Stars and constellations move by it.

By loss it gains existence, by humility it attains honor, by retreat it advances first.

The Dao originates in Heaven, and the myriad things grow thereby.

The Dao is but a forced name—what name is not forced?

Thus, that which gives birth to Heaven and Earth, nurtures the myriad things, transforms being and non-being, and eludes comprehension of its mystery is the Dao. That which pacifies the world, comforts the multitudes, governs advance and retreat, life and death, without losing its correctness is the sage.

Only by knowing the Dao can loss become existence, humility become honor, and retreat become precedence.

In ancient times, the Three Sovereigns grasped the essence of the Dao. Standing at the center, their spirits roamed with transformation, governing the four directions. Thus, they could turn the heavens and move the earth, wheeling without cease, flowing like water without end. They began and ended with things, wind rising and clouds forming, thunder rumbling and rain falling, all responding without limit.

Carved and polished, they returned to simplicity.

Acting without action, they harmonized life and death. Speaking without words, they penetrated Dao and virtue. Serene and unassuming, they attained harmony. Though things differ in myriad ways, they all thrive thereby.

They harmonized yin and yang, regulated the four seasons, balanced the five phases, moistened grasses and trees, permeated metal and stone. Beasts grew large, fur and feathers lustrous. Bird eggs did not spoil, beast fetuses did not miscarry. Fathers knew no grief for lost sons, elder brothers no sorrow for dead younger brothers. Children were not orphaned, women not widowed. Rainbows did not appear, thieves did not arise—such were the fruits of embodying virtue.

The rulers of high antiquity took the Heavenly Dao as the foundation of governance, roaming in unity with creation.

Thus, when Dao was pure and virtue complete, the people prospered and things flourished, each fulfilling its nature.

When the Dao was not violated, nothing was harmed.

The great constant Dao gives life without possession, transforms without dominion. The myriad things depend on it to live, yet none know its virtue; depend on it to die, yet none can resent it.

It gathers and stores without growing richer, distributes and bestows without becoming poorer.

Vague and indistinct, it cannot be grasped as form. Elusive and fleeting, its use is inexhaustible. Profound and obscure, it responds without shape. Penetrating and unobstructed, it moves not in vain. It coils and unfurls with hardness and softness, rises and falls with yin and yang.

It gives life without being born, transforms without transforming—this is called the great constant Dao.

The sage follows it, ruling the world and cherishing the people as children.

When transformation flows and Dao aligns, it is an age of flourishing virtue.

Laozi says: The great man is tranquil and thoughtless, composed and carefree. He takes Heaven as his canopy, Earth as his chariot, the four seasons as his horses, and yin and yang as his drivers.

He travels where there are no roads, roams without weariness, departs without gates.

Taking Heaven as his canopy, nothing is left uncovered. Taking Earth as his chariot, nothing is left uncarried. Taking the four seasons as his horses, nothing is left uncommanded. Taking yin and yang as his drivers, nothing is left unguided.

Thus, he moves swiftly without swaying, travels far without fatigue. His limbs do not stir, his hearing and vision do not falter, yet he illuminates all under Heaven—this is the essence of holding the Dao and observing the boundless.

Therefore, the affairs of the world cannot be forced; one must follow their natural course and guide them. The changes of the myriad things cannot be fathomed; one must grasp their essentials and return to them.

Thus, the sage cultivates the root within and does not adorn the branches without. He sharpens his spirit and stills his knowledge and perceptions. Thus, he is effortlessly without action yet accomplishes all, governs without governing yet nothing is left ungoverned.

The so-called "non-action" is not acting before things do. The so-called "non-governance" is not altering the natural. The so-called "nothing left ungoverned" is following the mutual arising of things.

The Dao resides within me, uniting with Heaven as one.

A state’s ministers and aides are like Heaven’s seasons and times.

When the great man emerges to assist a wise ruler, governing the people’s fate, observing the celestial timing, understanding the principles of things, following the Dao of nature, and enacting the transformation of non-action, then his person is cultivated, and governance knows no disorder.

Laozi says: He who holds the Dao to govern the people responds to events as they arise, follows things as they move. The transformations of the myriad things all find their answer; the changes of the hundred affairs all find their match.

Thus, the Dao is emptiness and nothingness, levelness and ease, clarity and stillness, softness and weakness, purity and simplicity—these five are the forms and images of the Dao.

Emptiness and nothingness are the Dao’s abode. Levelness and ease are the Dao’s essence. Clarity and stillness are the Dao’s mirror. Softness and weakness are the Dao’s function. Reversion is the Dao’s constant. Softness is the Dao’s hardness. Weakness is the Dao’s strength. Purity and simplicity are the Dao’s trunk.

The great Dao has no form; great peace has no image—hence, emptiness and nothingness.

Levelness and ease, clarity and stillness, softness and weakness, purity and simplicity—these are the Dao.

As for forms and images, are they truly forms? Are they truly images? Only he who does not cling to forms and images and thereby attains the use of the profound and mysterious can be spoken to of the Dao of governing the people.

Emptiness means the center bears no burden. Levelness means the heart harbors no cares. Desires and cravings not borne—this is the perfection of emptiness. No preferences or aversions—this is the perfection of levelness. Unchanging unity—this is the perfection of stillness. Unmixed with things—this is the perfection of purity. Neither sorrow nor joy—this is the perfection of virtue.

The Supreme Ultimate is centered in emptiness; spirit and clarity abide with it. When a person’s heart is empty, the Dao naturally resides within. If even one thing is borne, desires and cravings will obstruct; likes and dislikes will arise, and spirit will depart. When spirit departs, the Dao is lost—how then can the body not perish? Only one of perfect virtue remains unmixed with things, unwavering in unity, the heart empty and qi level—what sorrow or joy could there be?

The governance of the perfected person discards cleverness and erases ornamentation, relying on the Dao and abandoning cunning, emerging with the people from impartiality. Narrowing what is guarded, reducing what is sought, casting aside allurements and desires, diminishing thoughts and cares—narrowing what is guarded brings clarity; reducing what is sought brings attainment. Thus, with the inner regulating the outer, no affair is neglected. If the inner attains, the outer can be shepherded. When the inner attains, the five viscera are at peace, thoughts are level, sinews and bones are strong, ears and eyes are keen.

The great Dao is smooth and broad, not far from the self—those who seek it afar go forth only to return. Ornament obscures substance; broad learning drowns the heart. When the external is heavy, the internal is light—thus the great man dwells in reality, not in its blossoms.

The governance of the perfected person has no other way—reverent silence and non-action alone. Turning inward to seek the Dao—how distant is it? Laozi said: The sage forgets governing others and attends to self-cultivation; esteems forgetting power and position and attends to self-attainment—self-attainment is when the world attains me. Joy forgets wealth and honor and attains harmony; knowing the great self and diminishing the world—this approaches the Dao.

Thus it is said: Attain utmost emptiness, guard stillness firmly. The myriad things arise together, and I observe their return. The sage is without fame, not forgetting their merit; the spirit person is without merit, not forgetting the self. The perfected person is without self—not that they lack a body, but that they enlarge the self and diminish the world.

Only those who act with something to rely upon—thus the sage’s great treasure is position. The Dao shapes and nurtures the ten thousand kinds, beginning and ending without form, still and unmoving, greatly penetrating and profoundly obscure, vast and boundless—it cannot be made external; splitting the finest hair or dividing the smallest point cannot make it internal. Without walls or roof, it gives birth to the general name of being and non-being.

The true person embodies it with emptiness and nothingness, levelness and ease, clarity and stillness, softness and weakness, purity and simplicity, remaining unmixed with things—thus attaining the Dao of heaven and earth, and so called a true person. The Dao has no form, thus it can shape and nurture the ten thousand things; the Dao has no name, thus it can encompass all existents. The true person embodies the Dao, emptying the heart and stilling the spirit—thus attaining the Dao of heaven and earth.

The true person enlarges the self and diminishes the world, esteems governing the body and disdains governing others, does not allow things to disturb harmony, does not let desires confuse the emotions, conceals their name and identity—when the Dao is present, they remain hidden; when the Dao is absent, they appear. They practice non-action, engage in non-affairs, know non-knowing.

Embracing the great Dao, encompassing the heart of heaven, exhaling and inhaling yin and yang, expelling the old and taking in the new, closing with yin, opening with yang, rolling and unfolding with hardness and softness, bowing and rising with yin and yang, sharing the heart of heaven, sharing the body of the Dao. Without joy, without suffering, without delight, without anger, the myriad things are profoundly the same—without right, without wrong.

When the body is harmed by the ravages of cold, heat, dryness, and dampness, the body is exhausted and spirit is blocked; when spirit is harmed by the afflictions of joy, anger, thought, and care, spirit is spent and the body remains in excess. Thus the true person employs the heart to restore nature, relying on spirit for mutual support, thereby attaining beginning and end. Therefore, their sleep is dreamless, and their waking is without sorrow.

Bending outwardly—this is the human Dao; straightening inwardly—this is the heavenly Dao. Inwardly straight and outwardly bending, heaven and humanity correspond—there has never been one who did not succeed. Thus the true person conceals their name and identity—when the Dao is present, they remain hidden, not usurping others’ merit; when the Dao is absent, they appear, to remedy the age’s ills.

Only one who shares the heart of heaven and the body of the Dao can restore nature and rely on spirit, mutually supporting to attain beginning and end. Confucius asked the Dao of Laozi, who said: Correct your form, unify your gaze, and heavenly harmony will arrive; gather your knowledge, rectify your measures, and spirit will come to dwell. Virtue will adorn you, the Dao will be your abode. Bright-eyed as a newborn calf, without seeking the old; form like withered wood, heart like dead ashes—truly knowing reality without crookedness, thus self-sustaining and expansive, no heart to scheme, luminous in all four directions—can one be without knowledge?

The Dao has substance and function; the sage has no dual heart. The mysterious sage and the unadorned king—substance and function are two, but the Dao is one. Confucius, a sage of heaven’s endowment—how could he not know the Dao? Yet he asked Laozi, surely to gain what lies beyond words. Thus there is the deception of the dragon.

Laozi said: One who attends to life responds to change and acts; change arises from the times—one who knows the times has no constant conduct. Thus, the Dao that can be spoken is not the constant Dao; the name that can be named is not the constant name. Writing arises from words; words emerge from knowledge—those with knowledge do not know, for it is not the constant Dao. The name that can be named is not one who hoards books.

Excessive words lead to exhaustion—better to guard the center. Cut off learning and be without sorrow; cut off sagacity and abandon knowledge, and the people benefit a hundredfold. Books carry words; words carry the Dao. Esteeming books is esteeming the Dao. Thus, one who knows the times responds to change when affairs arise; one who knows books—words emerge from knowledge, but those with knowledge do not know.

Only by not clinging to books nor stagnating in affairs, cutting off learning and abandoning knowledge, can one begin to speak of the art of responding to change.

Man is tranquil at birth—this is Heaven’s nature. Moved by things, he stirs—this is the desire of nature. When things arrive, he responds—this is the movement of knowledge. As knowledge engages with things, likes and dislikes arise; when likes and dislikes take form, knowledge turns outward, unable to return to the self, and Heaven’s principle is extinguished. Thus, the sage does not exchange Heaven for man, outwardly transforming with things while inwardly preserving true nature. Therefore, one who penetrates the Dao returns to clarity and stillness; one who exhausts things ends in non-action. Nurture knowledge with tranquility, unite the spirit with emptiness—thus entering the gateless gate.

Those who follow Heaven wander with the Dao; those who follow men mingle with the vulgar. Thus, the sage does not disturb Heaven with affairs, nor disorder true nature with desires. Without scheming, he is fitting; without speaking, he is trusted; without pondering, he attains; without acting, he accomplishes. Therefore, though he dwells above, the people do not find him burdensome; though he stands before them, none feel harmed. The world turns to him, the wicked fear him, for he contends with nothing—who then would dare contend with him?

Heaven’s nature is fundamentally still; worldly desires disturb it. When stillness moves, Heaven’s nature is defiled. Only the sage, outwardly transforming with things while his heart wanders with Heaven, merges self and other in profound unity—what contention could there be?

Laozi said: "When man follows desire, he loses his nature; his movements are never upright. With this, to govern a state is to bring chaos; to cultivate the self is to invite defilement." Thus, those who have not heard the Dao cannot return to their nature; those who do not understand things cannot attain clarity and stillness. Human nature is originally free from depravity—long immersed in things, it changes; changing, it forgets its root and merges with what seems natural.

Water’s nature desires clarity, yet sand and stone defile it; man’s nature desires equilibrium, yet cravings harm it. Only the sage can forsake things and return to the self. Thus, the sage does not enslave things with knowledge, nor disturb harmony with desire. In joy, he is not exuberant; in sorrow, he is not distressed. Therefore, though exalted, he is not imperiled; though secure, he does not topple. Hearing good words and expedient plans, even the foolish delight in them; praising sagely virtue and lofty conduct, even the unworthy admire them. Many delight in such words, but few employ them; many admire such conduct, but few practice it. The reason lies in being entangled by things and bound by custom.

Thus it is said: "I practice non-action, and the people transform themselves; I am without affairs, and the people prosper themselves; I love stillness, and the people rectify themselves; I am without desire, and the people simplify themselves." When the heart is still, it is bright; when water is still, it is clear—this is principle. If learning is not upright, habit shapes nature, and stillness becomes movement, clarity becomes obscurity. Encountering a worthy teacher and hearing good words, if the heart awakens, one may restore one’s nature.

Originally, Heaven united with the great Dao. Applying this to cultivate the self perfects the self; applying it to govern the state perfects the state. Thus, the sage’s Dao is this: above, he practices non-action, and the people transform themselves; above, he is without affairs, and the people prosper themselves; above, he loves stillness, and the people rectify themselves; above, he is without desire, and the people simplify themselves.

Clarity and stillness are the perfection of virtue; softness and weakness are the function of the Dao. Emptiness, tranquility, and joy are the ancestors of the myriad things. When these three are practiced, one merges with the formless—the formless is called the One. The One is the mindless union with all under Heaven.

Virtue is spread without irrigation; its use is not labored. It is seen yet unseen, heard yet unheard. From the formless, forms arise; from the soundless, the five notes resound; from the tasteless, the five flavors take shape; from the colorless, the five colors emerge. Thus, being arises from non-being, fullness from emptiness.

The number of notes does not exceed five, yet the variations of the five notes are inexhaustible to the ear; the number of flavors does not exceed five, yet the variations of the five flavors are inexhaustible to the palate; the number of colors does not exceed five, yet the variations of the five colors are inexhaustible to the eye. When the note *gong* is established, the five notes take form; when sweetness is established, the five flavors are determined; when whiteness is established, the five colors are complete; when the One is established, the myriad things are born.

Thus, the principle of the One extends throughout the four seas; the blessing of the One pervades Heaven and Earth. In its wholeness, it is simple as uncarved wood; in its dispersion, it is vast as turbid waters. Turbid, it gradually clears; empty, it gradually fills. Vast as the great sea, drifting like floating clouds—seemingly absent yet present, seemingly lost yet existing.

The Dao is formless yet gives birth to form, beginning in the beginningless, ending in the endless. The primordial qi arises from the Great Simplicity. Is the Great Simplicity not the ancestor of the myriad things? From the One come the Two, and yin and yang divide; the five qi spread, and the myriad transformations flourish.

The sage’s heart unites with the Great Simplicity—clear, still, empty, and void. Virtue covers the four seas, and the myriad things return to him, yet he does not act as their master. The Dao is great and formless, one with Heaven. As for the ear’s delight in sound, the eye’s delight in color, the mouth’s delight in flavor—these are what man shares with others. How could the sage take them to heart?

Laozi said: "The sum of the myriad things all passes through one opening; the root of a hundred affairs all emerges from one gate." Thus, the sage follows the single standard, does not alter the ancient ways, does not change the constant norms. He takes the level as his model, the plumb line as his guide, bending and conforming to the constant.

Joy and anger are the Dao’s perversities; sorrow and grief are virtue’s losses; likes and dislikes are the heart’s transgressions; cravings and desires are life’s burdens. Great anger shatters yin; great joy collapses yang; thin qi brings dimness; terror induces madness; sorrow and grief scorch the heart, and illness forms as accumulations. If one can eliminate these five, one unites with spirit brightness.

Spirit brightness is attained within. Those who attain it within find their five viscera at peace, thoughts and deliberations balanced, ears and eyes keen, sinews and bones strong and straight. Open and unobstructed, not perverse; firm and strong, not exhausted. Nothing in excess, nothing unattainable.

The Dao, does it not embrace all mysteries and encompass the myriad things? Through spirit it is made luminous, through resonance it is made penetrating—revealing the hidden, elucidating the subtle, present without exception. Thus, things obtain it and flourish; the people obtain it and thrive; the timely ruler obtains it and may embody the august standard to govern the four quarters.

Nothing in the world is more yielding than water, yet in water lies the Dao. Its breadth cannot be fathomed, its depth cannot be sounded; its length stretches without end, its reach extends without limit. Its ebb and flow, its gain and loss, surpass all reckoning. Above, it becomes heaven’s rain and dew; below, it becomes earth’s moisture and nourishment. Without it, the myriad things cannot live; without it, the hundred affairs cannot be accomplished. It embraces all life without partiality, bestows grace upon dragons and serpents without seeking repayment, enriches the world without exhaustion, extends virtue to the people without depletion. Its course cannot be exhausted, its subtlety cannot be grasped. Bind it and it is not constrained; pierce it and it is not wounded; sever it and it is not cut; scorch it and it is not scorched. Flowing and compliant, it cannot be scattered; its benefit penetrates metal and stone, pervades the world. Surplus or deficiency, it allows the world to take or give; it receives the myriad things without preference, without partiality or impartiality, vast and equal to heaven and earth—this is called utmost virtue.

That water can perfect its utmost virtue lies in its softness, compliance, and smoothness. Thus it is said: The softest in the world gallops through the hardest; nothing enters where there is no gap. The One generates water, which beneficially nourishes the myriad things—its merit is most vast. Heaven without water cannot revolve; earth without water cannot bear; things without water cannot live; the people without water cannot survive. Rain and dew in the four seasons moisten and nourish all species. Flowing and compliant, dwelling below without contention, Yu’s virtue resembled it—thus he could accord with water’s nature and bring the earth to order, heaven to completion.

The formless is the great ancestor of things; the soundless is the great progenitor of kinds. The perfected person communicates with the numinous treasury, becoming one with the creator, holding profound virtue in the heart, and transforming like a spirit. Thus, the Dao that is not a Dao—how vast and indistinct it is! Without issuing commands or decrees, it shifts customs and alters mores—this is the power of conduct in the heart.

The myriad things have their origins, yet only the Dao knows their roots; the hundred affairs have their sources, yet only the Dao guards their gates. Thus it can exhaust the inexhaustible, reach the unreachable, illuminate things without dazzling, respond without knowing. The Dao has no fixed form; it assumes form according to things—transformation is manifest. Virtue has no constant teacher; it takes goodness as its teacher—substance and function are attained.

Thus the perfected person harbors the Dao and virtue, communicates with spirit brightness. Things have their origins—only the Dao knows their roots. Affairs have their sources—only the Dao guards their gates. Inexhaustible and boundless, it becomes one with the creator. Laozi said: Those who attain the Dao have a will that is weak yet their affairs are strong; their hearts are empty yet their responses are fitting. A weak will is soft, delicate, tranquil, concealed in not daring, acting in not being able, serene and non-active, moving without missing the moment. Thus the noble must take the lowly as its foundation, the high must take the low as its base. Relying on the small to embrace the great, being within to control the external, acting with softness yet being firm, its strength is invincible, its enemies undefeatable. Responding to transformations and measuring the times, nothing can harm it.

Those who desire firmness must guard it with softness; those who desire strength must preserve it with weakness. Accumulated softness becomes firmness; accumulated weakness becomes strength. Observe what is accumulated to know survival or demise. Strength overcomes what is inferior to itself, yet when it encounters its equal, it is checked. Softness overcomes what surpasses itself—its power is immeasurable. Thus, when armies are strong, they perish; when trees are strong, they break; when leather is strong, it splits. The teeth are firmer than the tongue yet perish first. Thus softness and weakness are the stems of life; firmness and strength are the companions of death. When things turn against me, the Dao abides in the heart.

Knowing my heaven, knowing others’ heaven, and knowing things’ heaven—this is attainment. Water is utterly soft, yet when it bears a boat, it is firm; the people are utterly weak, yet when they support a ruler, they are strong. Those who skillfully employ the Dao can guard softness and weakness to overcome firmness and strength. The first to sing paves the path to exhaustion; the last to act reaches the source of success.

Those who hold the Dao to pair with change—whether first or last, they control the other. Why? They do not lose what controls others, and others cannot control them. The so-called "last" adjusts its calculations to align with the time. When time changes, the interval does not allow for pause. Acting first is excessive; acting last is insufficient. The sun revolves, the moon cycles—time does not wait for people. Thus the sage does not value a foot of jade but values an inch of time. Time is hard to obtain yet easy to lose, so the sage acts according to the time, establishes merit according to resources, upholds the pure Dao, adheres to the feminine principle, follows along and responds to change, always remaining behind yet never ahead. Soft and weak in stillness, tranquil and composed in stability, their achievements are vast and nothing is too firm—they cannot be contested.

When affairs arise, respond—the Dao values attaining the mean. Excess and deficiency both harm affairs. Only by not losing the proper time in first and last is the mean attained and all affairs accomplished. Thus the sage’s appearance and withdrawal are timely, their first and last have measure. If I do not lose control over others, then others cannot control me.

Laozi said: When the heart harbors scheming, purity is not unsullied. When spiritual virtue is not complete in the body, how can one embrace what is distant? When the heart forgets the desire to harm, even a starving tiger can be tailed—how much more so with people?

Those who embody the Dao are at ease yet not exhausted; those who rely on calculations are laborious yet without achievement. Ruling through laws, punishments, and executions is not the enterprise of emperors and kings; frequent use of the whip and rod is not the way to govern for the long term. Excessive likes and dislikes bring calamity in their wake. Thus the laws of the former kings were not what they created but what they followed; their prohibitions and punishments were not what they enacted but what they upheld.

Thus, to follow is great, to create is petty; to uphold is firm, to act is to fail. Grasping at schemes and pursuing a starving tiger—one barely escapes the tiger’s jaws. Only when I harbor no scheming heart does the tiger bring no harm.

Therefore, those who embody the Dao are at ease, while those who rely on calculations are laborious. The principles of the world are not difficult to perceive: what is easy is readily known, what is simple is readily followed. When laws lack the harshness of punishments, governance becomes effortless; when governance dispenses with the frequent use of the whip, it can endure for the long term. Thus, the laws of the former kings were not what they created but what they followed; their prohibitions were not to suppress evil but to uphold what was right. Only thus could they establish a lasting and great enterprise.

Those who rely on ears and eyes for hearing and seeing exhaust the heart yet remain unclear; those who govern through cunning and planning strain the heart yet achieve nothing. Relying on the talents of one person is insufficient to bring order; the ability of one person cannot even govern a three-acre homestead. Yet by following the principles of the Dao and aligning with the natural order of Heaven and Earth, the Six Directions are not too vast to harmonize.

To lose judgment in praise or blame, to be dazzled by gaudy colors, to rely on ritual alone is insufficient to restrain desire—only a sincere heart can embrace what is distant. Thus, no weapon is sharper than the will, yet fine blades are inferior; no enemy is greater than yin and yang, yet war drums are trivial. The greatest foe lies in unspoken restraints, the middling hides in the mountains, the petty lurks among the people.

Thus it is said: when the people abound in cleverness, strange things proliferate; when laws and decrees multiply, thieves and bandits flourish. To reject one and adopt the other is to invite no heavenly calamity. Therefore, to govern a state through cunning is to plunder it; to govern without cunning is to bless it. When seeing and hearing are labored, the heart is unclear; when schemes and plans are overburdened, affairs are ungoverned. To rely on the talents of one person to seek order is difficult indeed.

A sincere heart can embrace what is distant; collective strength can achieve success—even the Six Directions are not too vast to harmonize. What ails the body is crowned by yin and yang; what plunders the state is crowned by treachery. To govern without cunning is the fortune of the state.

What is formless is great, what has form is petty; what is formless is abundant, what has form is scant; what is formless is strong, what has form is weak; what is formless is solid, what has form is hollow. What has form is the completion of affairs, what is formless is their inception. The completion of affairs produces tools, their inception is uncarved simplicity. What has form has sound, what is formless has no sound; what has form arises from what is formless—thus what is formless is the beginning of what has form.

Vastness and thickness bear names, and what bears a name is prized for its wholeness; frugality and thinness are nameless, and what is nameless is base and light. Abundance bears a name, and what bears a name is honored and esteemed; poverty and scarcity are nameless, and what is nameless is lowly and despised. The male bears a name, and what bears a name manifests virtue; the female is nameless, and what is nameless remains hidden and restrained. Surplus bears a name, and what bears a name is exalted and wise; deficiency is nameless, and what is nameless is assigned to the lowly. Merit brings a name, lack of merit brings none. What bears a name arises from what is nameless—what is nameless is the mother of what bears a name.

The Dao gives rise to being and non-being, difficulty and ease complete one another. Thus the sage holds to the Dao’s emptiness, stillness, subtlety, and mystery to perfect their virtue. Therefore, where there is the Dao, there is virtue; where there is virtue, there is merit; where there is merit, there is a name; where there is a name, there is a return to the Dao. Merit and name endure, and one remains free from fault throughout life.

All things under Heaven arise from being, being arises from non-being—thus what is formless is the beginning of what has form. The Dao is nameless, things bear names, yet the nameless is the mother of what bears a name. Know which is closer: name or self? Which is weightier: self or wealth? Then name need not be exalted, wealth need not be abundant.

Thus the sage holds to the Dao to perfect their virtue. With merit accomplished, they withdraw—from ancient times to the present, their name does not fade. Kings and lords possess merit and name, the orphaned and widowed possess neither. Thus it is said: the sage calls themselves orphaned and widowed, returning to their root. With merit accomplished, they do not claim it—thus they use merit for benefit and namelessness for function.

In ancient times, the people were simple and unknowing, unaware of east or west. Their appearance did not depart from their nature, their words did not exceed their actions, their conduct required no adornment, their speech was unembellished. Their clothes provided warmth without ornament, their weapons were blunt and unsharpened. They walked with unsteady steps, gazed with unfocused eyes, dug wells to drink, tilled fields to eat. They neither bestowed nor sought virtue. High and low did not vie, long and short did not compare.

Customs were uniform and could be followed, tasks matched abilities and were easily performed. Those who flaunted deceit to delude the world or behaved erratically to mislead the masses—the sage did not make this the people’s way.

In ancient times, the people were simple and unknowing, unaware of east or west. Their speech was unadorned, their clothes uncolored. They tilled to eat, dug wells to drink, neither bestowed nor sought, each content with their own.

Thus kings and great lords called themselves orphaned and widowed, and those who possessed the Dao did not destroy themselves for name, did not kill the people with governance, did not exhaust descendants with wealth, did not harm the world with learning. Later generations did not burden their hearts with merit, name, profit, or emolument. Who would willingly flaunt deceit to delude the world or behave erratically to mislead the masses?

*Comprehensive Exegesis of the Scripture of Penetrating the Mysteries, Scroll One, Complete.*

**Comprehensive Exegesis of the Scripture of Penetrating the Mysteries, Scroll Two**
*South Valley Master Du Daojian’s Annotated Compilation*

**Chapter on Sincerity**

Laozi said: Heaven attains its height, earth attains its depth; the sun and moon shine, the stars in their ranks gleam; yin and yang harmonize—yet none of this is by deliberate action.

Align with the Dao, and things arise of themselves. Yin and yang, the four seasons, do not generate the myriad things; timely rain and dew do not nurture grasses and trees. Yet when numinous clarity meets, when yin and yang harmonize, the myriad things are born.

The Dao conceals essence within, shelters spirit in the heart, still and silent, serene and unperturbed, joyfully at peace in the breast. Vast and formless, silent and soundless—government offices as if empty, the court as if deserted. No recluses, no idle folk, no forced labor, no unjust punishments. All under heaven gaze upward in reverence of the ruler’s virtue, in emulation of the sovereign’s intent. Even distant lands of alien customs, none fail to arrive through successive interpreters—not because they journey to each household to meet the people, but because the ruler extends his sincere heart to all under heaven.

Thus, to reward the good and punish the violent is to issue commands; what enables their execution is sincerity. Commands, though clear, cannot act alone—they must await sincerity. Therefore, when the Dao is universally applied to the people yet they do not follow, it is because sincerity has not reached them.

The sages of antiquity governed heaven and earth, administered the myriad things, stored essence and preserved sincerity—formless and soundless. They aligned with the Dao and entrusted things to their own nature. In that age, the court had no favored ministers, the wilds no neglected recluses, the state no vagrant people. Weapons were not raised, labor was not imposed, and the four classes delighted in their work. Thus, without visiting each household to instruct the people, supreme peace was effortlessly achieved.

Laozi said: Heaven established the sun and moon, arrayed the stars, extended the four seasons, and regulated yin and yang. Day exposes, night rests; wind dries, rain and dew moisten. In generating things, none see its nurturance, yet the myriad things grow. In destroying things, none see its nurturance, yet the myriad things perish—this is called numinous clarity.

Thus, the sage emulates this: when blessings arise, none see their cause, yet blessings arise; when calamities are removed, none see their source, yet calamities vanish. Searched, they cannot be found; examined, they are not empty. Daily reckoning falls short, yearly reckoning exceeds. Silent and soundless, a single word stirs all under heaven—this is the movement of the celestial heart through transformation.

Thus, sincerity shapes qi within, moving heaven. Auspicious stars appear, yellow dragons descend, phoenixes arrive, sweet springs flow, fine grains grow. Rivers do not overflow, seas do not surge. The sun, moon, and stars are the spirits of heaven; water, fire, earth, and stone are the spirits of earth. Rain, wind, dew, thunder, heat, cold, day, and night—all are spirits. Human nature is most numinous, surpassing all things in spirit.

Heaven nurtures things without revealing its care, yet daily they grow. The sage nurtures the people, removing harm and promoting benefit, doing likewise. All arises from sincerity manifesting within, qi moving heaven, yin and yang aligning, numinous spirits aiding—thus auspicious omens arrive.

To defy heaven and ravage things is to invite the sun and moon’s eclipse, the five stars’ misalignment, the four seasons’ disorder. Day darkens, night brightens; mountains collapse, rivers run dry; winter thunders, summer frosts. Heaven and humanity communicate thus. When a state declines, celestial signs alter, customs decay, rainbows appear—all things interconnect, essence and qi clash.

Therefore, the workings of the numinous cannot be achieved by cunning or forced by strength. Thus, the great person joins virtue with heaven and earth, brightness with the sun and moon, numinosity with ghosts and spirits, and trustworthiness with the four seasons. Embracing the celestial heart, holding the earthly qi, grasping the mean and containing harmony, they do not leave their hall yet traverse the four seas, transforming customs. The people reform as if by their own will—this is the power of spiritual transformation.

Heaven and humanity share one qi, the hidden and manifest interpenetrate. Harmonious qi brings blessings; pestilential qi brings misfortune—none arise apart from the ruler. Thus, to defy heaven and ravage things, to violate the Dao and corrupt virtue, is to provoke the wrath of august heaven, and calamity follows.

As when King Cheng repented his errors, the grain stalks bent and the wind reversed; when the Lord of Song spoke a single word, the Fire Star retreated—these were signs of sincerity moving heaven, turning misfortune to fortune.

Laozi said: The human Dao is to preserve one’s nature and guard one’s authenticity, not to harm one’s body. When pressed by urgency or peril, essence connects with heaven. Those who have never departed from their origin—what could they not accomplish? Death and life share the same realm; they cannot be coerced.

Moreover, to govern heaven and earth, administer the myriad things, return to the creative process, and contain supreme harmony is to never die. Sincerity takes shape within and outwardly resonates with the human heart—this is the untransmitted Dao.

In life, all receive their mandate from heaven alike. Thus, nature is not without goodness. To preserve one’s nature and guard one’s authenticity, not to harm one’s body, to connect essence with heaven—what could not be achieved? To return to the creative process, contain supreme harmony, and never die—this is the true person.

When the sage rules above, embracing the Dao without speech, his beneficence extends to the myriad people. Thus, the teaching without words—how vast and boundless! When ruler and minister diverge in heart, deceit manifests in heaven, and the mutual resonance of spirit and qi is evident. This is the eloquence of the wordless, the Dao of the unspoken Dao.

To summon the distant, let there be no action; to draw close, let there be no speech. Only those who walk in darkness can attain this. Thus, horses are turned back to manure the fields, and chariot tracks do not reach beyond distant lands—this is called "sitting in stillness while galloping in obscurity."

The Dao of heaven is impartial in its approach, impartial in its departure—those who are capable have abundance, while the inept fall short. Those who follow it prosper; those who oppose it meet misfortune. Thus, governing through cunning makes it difficult to sustain a state; only by aligning with the Great Harmony and adhering to natural resonance can one attain this. The sage embraces the Dao, and his beneficence reaches the people—auspicious signs become visible. When ruler and minister diverge in heart, calamity manifests in heaven. The distant are summoned without action; the near are drawn close without speech. The mutual resonance of spirit and *qi* is evident—what need is there for summoning before they arrive?

Impartial in approach, impartial in departure, abundance and insufficiency—all align with the Great Harmony. The teaching without words is simply the natural course. Laozi said: The Dao and virtue are like leather and hide—distance makes them seem near, closeness makes them seem distant. Scrutiny yields no grasp, observation finds no emptiness. Thus, the sage is like a mirror: neither advancing nor retreating, responding without hoarding. The myriad things are unharmed—what he gains is but loss, what he loses is but gain.

Therefore, one who penetrates the Great Harmony is dark as if steeped in wine, sweetly reclining and roaming within it, as if never having departed from its source—this is called "great penetration." This is the use that arises from non-use. The Dao is revered, virtue is esteemed—they differ in name but emerge from the same source, residing within our hearts, not obtained from without. It gives life and nurtures, neither non-existent nor existent.

The heart of the sage is like a bright mirror: things arrive and it responds; things depart and it is still. Embracing sincerity, pure in virtue and power—what failure could there be? Without attachment, what loss could occur? As if never having departed from its source, even spirits and gods cannot discern it.

Laozi said: In ancient times, the Yellow Emperor ruled the world, harmonizing the movements of the sun and moon, regulating the *qi* of yin and yang, ordering the measures of the four seasons, rectifying the calculations of pitch and calendar. He distinguished male and female, clarified superior and inferior, ensuring the strong did not oppress the weak, the many did not tyrannize the few. The people preserved their lives without premature death; the years ripened without famine. The hundred officials were upright and impartial; superiors and inferiors were harmonious without blame. Laws and decrees were clear, not obscure; assistants were just, not sycophantic. Farmers yielded boundaries; roads were free of lost items; markets did not engage in preemptive pricing. Thus, in that time, the sun, moon, and stars did not deviate from their courses; wind and rain came in season; the five grains flourished; phoenixes soared in the courtyards, and qilin roamed the outskirts. Observing the Dao of heaven and grasping its workings, the Yellow Emperor attained governance of the world. Later, he relinquished the world, hastened to Kongtong, humbled himself to seek instruction, and asked Guangchengzi how to cultivate the body to achieve longevity.

Guangchengzi said: The essence of the supreme Dao is profound and obscure. Its ultimate is dim and silent. See nothing, hear nothing, embrace the spirit in stillness. The body will naturally rectify itself—be still, be pure. Do not weary your form, do not agitate your essence—only then can you attain long life.

When Fuxi ruled the world, he rested his head on stone and slept on rope, slaying in autumn and executing in winter. He bore the square earth and embraced the round heaven, enveloped by yin and yang. Those obstructed and stagnant—where *qi* and principle were perverse, harming the people and accumulating excess—he severed and halted them. His people were simple and unknowing, unaware of east or west, their sight dim, their steps faltering. Yet they were artless and content, unaware of their drifting, floating without knowing their origin, nurturing themselves without knowing their destination.

In that time, birds, beasts, insects, and snakes all sheathed their claws and fangs, concealed their stings and poisons—their achievements matched heaven and earth. Yet when the Yellow Emperor sought counsel beneath the Great Ancestor, he did not display his merits or proclaim his name, concealing the Dao of the true person to follow the inherent constancy of heaven and earth. Why? Because virtue and power ascend while cunning and artifice vanish.

When the Six Eras neared their end and the Two Sovereigns did not appear, then Taihao Fuxi emerged. At that time, the Great Simplicity dispersed, and human affairs began to sprout—the world first saw action. Observing images, he fashioned tools; knotting cords into nets, he tamed birds and beasts. This was called Fuxi. Smelting metal into cauldrons, transforming raw into cooked, he was also named Paoxi. In that time, birds, beasts, insects, and snakes sheathed their claws and fangs, no longer harming people. Later, when he created writing and rectified human relations, his achievements matched heaven and earth, yet he did not regard them as accomplishments. Thus, he was revered as Taihao—was this not fitting?

Laozi said: If heaven is unsettled, the sun and moon have no place to rest; if earth is unsettled, plants and trees have no place to stand; if the self is not at peace, right and wrong have no form. Thus, only with the true person does true knowledge arise. If what one holds is unclear, how can one know that what I call knowledge is not ignorance?

Accumulating kindness and valuing wealth, making the people joyful and delighting in their lives—this is benevolence. Establishing great deeds, displaying illustrious names, ordering ruler and minister, clarifying superior and inferior, distinguishing close and distant, preserving imperiled states, continuing severed lineages, and establishing heirs for the childless—this is righteousness. Closing the nine orifices, concealing will and intent, discarding wisdom and intelligence, returning to non-discernment, wandering blithely beyond the dust, roaming freely in the realm of no affairs, inhaling yin and exhaling yang to harmonize with the myriad things—this is virtue.

Thus, the Dao disperses into virtue, virtue overflows into benevolence and righteousness, and when benevolence and righteousness are established, the Dao and virtue are abandoned. The Dao and virtue in relation to the Five Constants are like yin and yang in relation to the Five Phases—they are one. Knowing that the sun and moon alternate in brightness, the four seasons proceed in succession, and only then is the year completed—this is understanding the human Dao. The Dao, virtue, and the Five Constants can coexist but cannot be without one another.

Thus, Laozi said: "Abandon sagacity, discard wisdom; abandon benevolence, discard righteousness." Why? Because he detested their names being usurped and enacted falsely. If one truly had the heart to abandon them, then the *Dao* and *De* chapters would not speak of sages or benevolence and righteousness.

Thus, only with the true person does true knowledge arise.

Laozi said: "When the spirit scatters, words become florid; when virtue dissipates, conduct turns false. When the utmost essence radiates within yet speech and action are observed without, this is inevitably to be enslaved by things." When essence is exhausted through sorrow and conduct knows no bounds, what one holds fast to is unstable, and one becomes externally swayed by worldly customs.

Therefore, the sage cultivates the Dao and virtue internally and does not adorn them externally with benevolence and righteousness. Knowing the proper use of the nine orifices and four limbs, they wander in the harmony of spirit—this is the sage’s wandering. The Dao and virtue are the ancestors of the Five Constants; to have ancestors without descendants is impossible. Yet to have descendants while forgetting the ancestors—is this permissible? The Five Constants are the five spirits. When the Dao and virtue reside within, the spirit does not scatter without. Once one loses what they guard, the spirit scatters and words become florid, virtue dissipates and conduct turns false—few escape enslavement by things.

Only the sage, knowing the proper use of the nine orifices and four limbs, wanders in the harmony of spirit—when the ancestor is preserved, how can the descendants not be preserved? Laozi said: "As for the sage’s wandering, they move in perfect emptiness, roam the mind in the Great Void, gallop beyond boundaries, act without gates, listen without sound, see without form, unbound by the world, unshackled by custom." Thus, what the sage uses to move the world is not surpassed by the true person; what the worthy use to correct worldly customs is not observed by the sage.

People bound by worldly customs inevitably have their forms constrained and spirits drained, and thus cannot escape entanglement. If what binds and restrains me must lie in something external, then when the body is not shackled by custom, others will not weary of me. When the mind is not bound by the world, I will not weary of others—this is called wandering with the Creator. Thus, what moves the world is not surpassed by the true person, and what corrects worldly customs is not observed by the sage.

When the will is enslaved by things, the form is constrained and the spirit drained—even the worthy cannot always escape this, let alone the common people. Laozi said: "The ruler’s thoughts do not manifest in the breast, nor does wisdom extend beyond the four quarters. Embracing a heart of benevolence and sincerity, timely sweet rains fall, the five grains flourish, spring births, summer grows, autumn harvests, winter stores. Monthly inspections and seasonal assessments are conducted, and tribute is offered at year’s end. Nurturing the people with impartiality, awe is instilled without admonition, laws are simple and not burdensome, transformation is as if divine, punishments are lenient and prisons empty. The world shares one custom, and none harbor treacherous hearts—this is the sage’s grace.

When superiors covet without measure, inferiors vie for merit without restraint. The people grow poor and bitter, disputes arise, toil yields no reward, cunning and deceit sprout, thieves and bandits multiply, superiors and inferiors resent one another, and decrees go unheeded. When carriages share the same tracks and writing the same script, the world shares one custom. When rewards are not excessive and punishments not cruel, the four seas unite in heart. If this is achieved, the ruler’s thoughts do not extend beyond the four quarters, and transformation is as if divine.

When superiors covet without limit, inferiors vie for merit without restraint. Cunning arises and the people’s strength is depleted; superiors and inferiors resent one another, heaven and earth do not interact, and the myriad things do not communicate. When water is turbid, fish gasp; when governance is harsh, the people rebel. When superiors are full of desires, inferiors grow deceitful; when superiors are restless, inferiors are unsettled; when superiors make many demands, inferiors contend with one another. To neglect the root and seek remedy in the branches is no different from digging channels to stop water or carrying firewood to extinguish fire.

The sage’s affairs are few, their demands for governance minimal yet sufficient. Without acting, they are benevolent; without speaking, they are trusted; without seeking, they obtain; without striving, they accomplish. Embracing naturalness, preserving utmost sincerity, holding to the Dao and extending sincerity, the world follows them as echo responds to sound, as shadow reflects form—what they cultivate is the root. Heaven displays omens to show good and ill fortune, and all see them. The ruler issues commands, clarifies rewards and punishments, and the people all trust them. Is the ruler not like heaven?

When water is turbid, fish gasp; when governance is harsh, the people rebel—this is the inevitable principle. Thus, when superiors are full of desires, the people grow deceitful; when superiors favor stillness, the people do not contend. The sage, holding to the Dao and extending sincerity, is followed by the world—this may be called knowing the root.

Laozi said: "When the spirit scatters without and wisdom dissipates within, governance cannot be achieved. When the use of form and spirit is distant, what is neglected is near. Thus, one who does not step beyond the door knows the world; one who does not peer through the window knows the Dao of heaven. The farther one goes, the less one knows—this speaks of essence and sincerity issuing from within, and spirit and qi moving heaven."

Heaven and earth interact, and the myriad things communicate; the sage arises, and the myriad things behold—this is the Dao of natural resonance. The human heart connects with heaven, for in receiving form and destiny, one’s origin is from heaven itself. Thus, when essence and sincerity issue from within, spirit and qi move heaven. When the human heart is empty and bright, the light of heaven shines forth, like a mirror reflecting form—beauty and ugliness appear of themselves. How can the heart not be guarded?

Laozi said: "The yang of winter’s sun, the yin of summer’s shade—all things return to them without limit. This is the resonance of utmost essence, coming unsummoned, departing without going, profound and obscure, accomplishing its work without one knowing how. To rely on the eyes to see or words to command makes governance difficult. Gaoyao was blind yet served as grand judge, and the world knew no cruel punishments—what need was there for words? Shi Kuang was blind yet served as grand minister, and the state of Jin knew no chaotic governance—what need was there for sight? The command that requires no words, the vision that requires no seeing—this is how the sage serves as teacher.

The burning mirror summons fire, yet without the sun, it does not flame; the square vessel summons water, yet without the moon, it does not flow. Thus, with the Dao but without position, affairs cannot be established."

With position but without the Dao, achievements cannot be realized. When the rulers and ministers of Tang and Yu aligned in the Dao, transformation was perfected and teaching spread; the hundred officials were upright, and the myriad people submitted—this is why the sage serves as teacher for a hundred generations. The people’s transformation by their superiors does not follow their words but follows their conduct.

Thus, when a ruler favors courage, though he does not incite strife, the state will face many calamities, and its gradual decline will inevitably lead to the chaos of robbery and murder. When a ruler favors beauty, though he does not encourage licentious talk, the state will fall into darkness and disorder, and its accumulation will reach the disaster of debauchery and dissipation. Therefore, the sage’s sincerity is distinguished within, and his likes and dislikes are clear without; his words match his feelings, and his commands clarify his intent. Thus, punishments are insufficient to shift customs, and executions are insufficient to suppress villainy—only spiritual transformation is precious. Utmost essence is spirit, and where essence moves, it is like the spring’s vitality or the autumn’s withering.

Thus, the noble person is like an archer: what is a hair’s breadth here is a fathom’s length there. Therefore, those who govern others must be cautious in how they influence them. When the standard is upright, its shadow is straight; when the source is clear, its flow is long—this is the Dao of mutual support between root and branch. Knowing the heart as the root of the body, one knows the ruler as the root of the people. Thus, a ruler’s preferences must not be unprincipled. Favoring courage gives rise to the chaos of robbery and murder; favoring beauty gives rise to the disaster of debauchery and dissipation. Only one who favors virtue distinguishes essence within and clarifies likes and dislikes without. Punishments go unused, yet villainy submits; with the root and foundation secure, the state naturally attains peace.

Laozi said: "When laws are displayed and rewards are set but cannot shift customs, it is because sincerity is not embraced." Thus, hearing its tone, one knows its customs; observing its music, one knows its mores; seeing its mores, one knows its transformation. Those who embrace truth and manifest sincerity move heaven and earth, and their spirit transcends all bounds. Commands are obeyed, and prohibitions are upheld; their Dao is penetrated, and their intent is conveyed. Though they utter not a word, the myriad people of the world, along with birds and beasts, ghosts and spirits, transform with them. Thus, the highest is spiritual transformation; the next is preventing wrongdoing; the lowest is rewarding the worthy and punishing the violent.

A mountain glows when it contains jade; a deep pool charms when it holds pearls—what is within manifests without. Water and stone are wordless, yet people trust them. When a state cherishes its benevolence and sincerity, extends its trustworthiness, punishes without resentment, and rewards without partiality, there is no need to display laws or set rewards, for the people will transform of their own accord. Thus, those who hear of Boyi’s virtue find the stubborn become incorruptible and the weak gain resolve—what words did Boyi speak? His person transformed them.

If words are spoken but not practiced, the people will not follow.

Laozi said: The Great Dao is non-action; non-action means non-possession, and non-possession means non-abiding. One who does not abide dwells in formlessness; one who is formless does not move; one who does not move does not speak. Without speech, there is stillness—soundless and formless. Soundless, it cannot be seen; formless, it cannot be heard. This is called the subtle and wondrous, the supremely spiritual. Vast and unceasing, it is called the root of Heaven and Earth.

The Dao is formless and soundless, so the sage forces it into form, naming the Dao of Heaven and Earth with a single word. The great takes the small as its foundation; the many takes the few as its beginning. The Son of Heaven regards Heaven and Earth as his standard and all things as his resources. His merit and virtue are supreme, his power and renown most noble. The beauty of these two virtues matches Heaven and Earth, so he must follow the Great Dao as the mother of all under Heaven.

Within the realm, there are four great ones: the Dao, Heaven, Earth, and the King. The Dao is non-action, thus enduring; Heaven is wordless, thus lofty and bright; Earth is soundless, thus vast and deep. To embody all three is to be a king. The king is the child of Heaven, the master of Earth, and the parent of the people. Only by lovingly nurturing all things without claiming it as favor does his merit and virtue become supreme, his power and renown most noble—none can surpass him.

Laozi said: Relieving the poor and aiding the distressed gives rise to fame and profit; eliminating harm brings merit to completion. If the world is free from disasters, even sages have no virtue to display; if superiors and inferiors are harmonious, even the worthy have no merit to establish. Thus, the governance of the perfected person embraces virtue and holds to the Dao, extending sincerity and delighting in giving. With boundless wisdom, he rests in silence and does not speak, yet none under Heaven fail to value his wordlessness.

Thus, "The Dao that can be spoken is not the constant Dao; the name that can be named is not the constant name." What is inscribed on bamboo and silk, carved into metal and stone, and transmitted to others—these are but its coarse aspects. The Three Sovereigns, Five Emperors, and Three Kings differed in their affairs but shared the same heart; they took different paths but reached the same destination. Scholars of later ages, not knowing the unity of the Dao’s embodiment or the essentials of virtue, seize upon the traces of accomplished deeds, kneel and sit to discourse upon them. Though learned and widely informed, they cannot avoid confusion.

Embracing the Dao and holding to virtue, extending sincerity and delighting in giving—this is the Way of those above. Relieving the poor, aiding the distressed, generating profit, and eliminating harm—this is the Way of those in the middle. Seeking victory for oneself without contending for it—this is the Way of those below. Adapting to the times, understanding change and contingency, not bound by office or reclusion, differing in affairs yet sharing the same merit—such a one need not rely on bamboo, silk, metal, or stone to stand as a peer of the ancients.

Laozi said: The essence of the heart can spiritually transform but cannot be spoken of. The sage governs all under Heaven without leaving his seat, his care more urgent than a cry for help. Thus, when words are the same, trust precedes them; when commands are the same, sincerity lies beyond them. When the sage is above, the people are transformed as if by spirit, for emotion precedes them. If he acts above but there is no response below, it is because emotion and command differ.

A three-month-old infant knows not benefit or harm, yet a loving mother’s care for it grows ever deeper—this is emotion. Thus, the function of speech brings change, but the change is small; the function of silence brings change, and the change is great. Trusting a noble person’s words and being loyal to his intent, loyalty and trust take shape within and move responses without—this is the transformation of the worthy and sage.

The *Book of the Yellow Emperor* says: Heaven’s nature is human; the human heart is mechanism. Is the ruler not the heart of Heaven and Earth? The heart is the dwelling of spirit and brightness; emotion stirs within, and words issue forth. If good, a response comes from a thousand *li* away; if not good, opposition arises from a thousand *li* away. Thus, when the sage is above, his transformation is like spirit—he governs all under Heaven without leaving his seat.

Laozi said: A son’s death for his father, a minister’s death for his lord—this is not seeking fame by dying, but because a heart of affection is hidden within and does not shirk difficulty. The sorrow of the noble person is not for outward show but arises from within; it is also observed in his conduct. The sage is not ashamed before his shadow; the noble person is cautious in solitude. To abandon the near and seek the distant is to block the way.

Thus, when the sage is above, the people delight in his governance—such were the Two Emperors and Three Kings. When the sage is below, the people long for his intent—such were the mysterious sages and unadorned kings. The people do not forget their desire to benefit others. When a scholar faces danger and offers his life, or stands firm in great integrity and cannot be swayed, it is loyalty and filial piety that make it so.

Like Chang Hong dying for his lord or Shen Sheng dying for his father, a heart of affection is hidden within and does not shirk difficulty—how could this be seeking fame by dying? When the sage is above, the people delight in his governance; when below, they long for his intent.

Laozi said: When a brave warrior cries out, the three armies all retreat—this comes from the sincerity of his utterance. If one sings and none respond, or speaks and none bear it, there must be discord within. To govern all under Heaven without leaving one’s seat is to seek it within oneself. Thus, where persuasion does not reach, bearing does; where bearing does not reach, subtle influence does. Influence arises from the heart and takes form; the utmost essence can be met in form but cannot be awaited in expectation.

Words issue from the mouth, actions arise from the heart—this is the movement of sincerity. Words come from near, actions extend afar—this is the response of sincerity. When a brave warrior cries out, does any in the three armies not retreat? It is the sincerity of his utterance. Like Sunzi’s teaching of warfare, courage arises from sincerity. When he beheaded the king’s beloved concubine, it was because the King of Wu’s words lacked sincerity, and disaster befell his subordinates. For those who hold a state, can their words and deeds not be sincere?

Laozi said: Words have their source, affairs have their root. Losing their source and root, even with many skills, is not as good as speaking little. To harm the multitude, have the master craftsman cut off his finger to show that great skill cannot be forced.

Thus, the wise craftsman does not rely on skill but on timely closure—yet if he does not know when to close, he must bar the door before opening it. Loquacity and busyness are what the sage warns against. Only by honoring the Dao’s root and virtue’s foundation, teaching through action rather than words, can one avoid failure. Handling affairs with non-action ensures no harm. Those swept along by vulgar trends, though skilled in many arts, are enslaved by their own cleverness—how can they not injure their hands? The great craftsman’s work does not depend on cunning; thus, no bolt or bar can keep it shut. Yet one who bars the door before opening it—though the lock be sturdy—will have it sprung when thieves arrive. Where, then, lies the root and foundation?

Laozi said: The sage’s undertakings, though pursued by different paths, converge in the same end. Survival and ruin, stability and collapse, are as one to him, for his resolve never wavers from benefiting others. Thus, the songs of Qin, Chu, Yan, and Wei differ in sound yet all express joy; the laments of the Nine Yi and Eight Di differ in sound yet all convey sorrow. Song is the manifestation of joy, weeping the expression of grief—what stirs within emerges without. It is the impulse that moves them. The sage’s heart, day and night, never forgets its desire to benefit others; the reach of his grace extends far indeed.

The sage is not without desire—he follows what benefits and thereby benefits; the sage is not without action—he acts when it is fitting to act. Different paths, same destination; survival and ruin as one. Diminishing oneself to profit others, never forgetting the world. Though lands differ and customs shift, though speech varies and winds change, human nature remains constant. What stirs within emerges without: joy finds voice in song, sorrow in tears, each response arising from its cause. All are our people—how can they be forgotten?
