Like the Duke of Zhou, who toiled day and night, sitting vigil until dawn, his resolve unwavering—his grace extended far.

Laozi said: When man governs through non-action, to act is to harm the governance of non-action. To practice non-action is to act without acting; one who cannot act without acting cannot act at all. When man is silent, the spirit speaks—yet to force silence harms the spirit’s silence. To bear silence is to injure the spirit’s numinosity. The body has form; the spirit is formless. Form speaks; the formless is silent. Knowing the mutual arising of being and non-being, the formless does not harm being, nor does being harm the formless. Thus, the sage governs through non-action, neither body harming spirit nor spirit harming body. For wisdom is not numinous—it is what makes the numinous numinous. Thus, the two do not harm each other.

Wenzi said: Reputation can be forcibly established, merit forcibly achieved. Once, Nanrong Chu felt ashamed that the sagely Dao had perished in himself alone. He journeyed south to see Laozi and received a single word of teaching. His spirit awakened, his obstructions dissolved, and though he endured hardship for ten days without eating, it was as though he feasted on the finest meats. Thereby, his clarity illuminated the world, his name endured through later ages, his wisdom spanned heaven and earth, his discernment pierced the finest detail. His praises resounded in elegant speech, ceaseless even now—this is what it means to forcibly establish a name.

Thus, the farmer who does not strive will not fill his granary; the official who does not exert himself will not refine his sincerity; the general and minister who do not press forward will not accomplish great deeds; the king and lord who grow lax will leave no name to posterity.

The Perfected One moves in concealment, like thunder hidden away. He acts in accord with the times, establishes merit through available resources, advances and retreats without difficulty, and penetrates all things without obstruction. Through persistent effort and resolute action, success is achieved as one—was Nanrong Zhao not also one who studied knowing the benefit of action?

Nanrong Chu felt shame that the sagely Way was absent within himself alone, so he entrusted his studies to the gate of Gengsang Chu. Later, he journeyed south to see Laozi and obtained the *Scripture of Nurturing Life*. His clarity illuminated the world, his name endured through later ages—thus, a name can be forcibly established, and merit can be forcibly accomplished.

The sage moves in concealment, acts in accord with the times, and establishes merit through available resources—how could advance and retreat pose any difficulty? The Perfected One refines sincerity within to manifest it outwardly, and his virtue flows to the four directions. Seeing benefit in the world, he rejoices yet does not forget harm; seeing harm, he is startled as if in mourning.

He who shares the people’s sorrows, the people will share his sorrows; he who delights in the people’s joy, the people will delight in his joy. Thus, to sorrow with the world and rejoice with the world—yet fail to become king—has never been known.

The sage’s method begins where it cannot be seen and ends where it cannot be attained. He abides in an unshakable position, accumulates in an inexhaustible storehouse, and bears his charge in an unfailing treasury. His decrees issue forth like the source of flowing water, guiding the people to offices free of contention and opening gates that must be entered.

He does not attempt what cannot be accomplished, does not seek what cannot be obtained, does not dwell where he cannot remain, and does not act where he cannot return. The great man enacts governance that brings delight, and none fail to follow his commands. When commands are followed, one progresses from the small to the great; when commands are defied, good becomes harm, and success becomes failure.

The Perfected One, the sage, and the great man should be distinguished. The Perfected One regards the people as himself, sharing in their benefit and harm. When the people rejoice, he rejoices; when the people sorrow, he sorrows—there has never been one who did not become king.

The sage’s method begins where it cannot be seen and ends where it cannot be attained; now it issues forth like a flowing stream. The great man enacts governance that brings delight, issuing commands in accord with the times. When commands align, the people follow; when they oppose, the people suffer harm.

The so-called great man is inwardly strong and outwardly luminous—his inner strength like heaven and earth, his outer radiance like the sun and moon. Heaven and earth cover and bear all without exception; the sun and moon illuminate all without fail. The great man reveals goodness to others without altering his fundamental nature or changing his constant principles. The world obeys his commands as grass bends to the wind.

When governance falters in spring, Jupiter’s movements expand or contract, no longer abiding in its proper course. When governance falters in summer, Mars retrogrades. When governance falters in autumn, Venus does not appear where it should, its rising and setting erratic. When governance falters in winter, Mercury fails to manifest in its proper region. When governance falters in all four seasons, Saturn sways and trembles, the sun and moon display omens, the five planets move in rebellion, and comets appear.

When spring governance is not lost, millet and grain flourish; when summer governance is not lost, rain falls in season; when autumn governance is not lost, the people prosper; when winter governance is not lost, the state enjoys peace and security.

Humans receive the numinous essence of heaven and earth, and the heart is the abode of spirit and illumination. The great man, however, possesses a spirit even more numinous than others. The slightest movement of his mind—whether toward good or evil—is mirrored by the celestial bell. Thus, the success or failure of governance manifests in portents and omens, responding to affairs without error.

The *Book of Documents* states: “To slight the spirits and oppress the people—August Heaven will not protect such.” Those who hold office—can they not be cautious?

*Comprehensive Meaning of the* Scripture of Penetrating the Mysteries, *Scroll Two* (concluded)

*Comprehensive Meaning of the* Scripture of Penetrating the Mysteries, *Scroll Three*
Compiled by Du Daojian of Nangu Valley

**The Nine Guardians**

Laozi said: Before heaven and earth took form, all was dark and indistinct, merged as one, silent and limpid. The heavy and turbid became earth; the refined and subtle became heaven. They separated into the four seasons, divided into yin and yang. Pure qi formed humans; coarse qi formed creatures. Through the interplay of hardness and softness, the myriad things were born.

Spirit originates from heaven; bones and flesh are rooted in earth. When spirit enters its gate, bones and flesh return to their root—what then remains of me? Thus, the sage models himself on heaven and follows earth, unbound by custom, unenticed by others. He takes heaven as father, earth as mother, yin and yang as his warp, the four seasons as his weft. Heaven is still and clear; earth is settled and tranquil. All things that oppose them perish; those that accord with them live.

Thus, stillness and silence are the abode of spirit and illumination; emptiness and nothingness are where the Dao resides. Spirit is what one receives from heaven and earth; bones and flesh are what one is endowed with from earth.

The Dao gives birth to the One; the One gives birth to the Two; the Two give birth to the Three; the Three give birth to the myriad things. The myriad things bear yin and embrace yang, blending qi to achieve harmony.

The One is the beginning of form; nine is the completion of numbers. Nine is the ultimate. The sage investigates the nine yet guards the One—then the Dao is within me. The One is the heart; the heart is heaven; heaven is man; man is things; things are the Dao; the Dao is I; I am the beginning. To know the ancient beginning—this is called the discipline of the Dao.

Laozi said: Humans are born through the transformations of heaven and earth. In the first month, the essence congeals; in the second, blood and vessels form; in the third, the embryo takes shape; in the fourth, the fetus develops; in the fifth, the sinews form; in the sixth, the bones harden; in the seventh, the body is complete; in the eighth, it stirs; in the ninth, it becomes restless; in the tenth, it is born. Once the physical form is complete, the five viscera become distinct. The liver governs the eyes; the lungs govern the nose; the spleen governs the tongue; the kidneys govern the ears; the gallbladder governs the mouth. The exterior is the surface; the interior is the core. The head is round, following heaven; the feet are square, resembling earth.

Heaven has the four seasons, five phases, nine luminaries, and three hundred sixty days; humans have four limbs, five viscera, nine orifices, and three hundred sixty joints. Heaven has wind, rain, cold, and heat; humans have taking and giving, joy and anger. The gallbladder corresponds to clouds; the lungs to qi; the spleen to wind; the kidneys to rain; the liver to thunder. Humans resemble heaven and earth, and the heart governs them all. The ears and eyes are the sun and moon; blood and qi are wind and rain. When the sun and moon lose their course, they dim and eclipse; when wind and rain come unseasonably, they destroy and bring disaster. When the five stars lose their course, states and lands suffer calamity.

The beginning of things lies in the One, from which all arises. Through transformation, the myriad things are born. Father heaven and mother earth, yin and yang unite, conceiving and nurturing life. Impure qi forms creatures; pure qi forms humans. Thus, heaven, earth, and the myriad things are all contained within my body.

Opening and closing, giving and taking—such is the Way of heaven. To oppose it is to die; to follow it is to live. The Way of heaven and earth is vast and great, yet it still moderates its brilliance and cherishes its spirit and illumination. How then can human ears and eyes endure constant stimulation without rest? How can spirit gallop without exhaustion? Thus, the sage guards the inner and does not lose the outer.

Blood and qi are the glory of humans; the five viscera are their essence. When blood and qi concentrate within and do not stray outward, the chest and abdomen are full, and desires are few. With few desires, the ears and eyes are clear, hearing and vision sharp—this is called illumination. When the five viscera are aligned with the heart and do not depart, qi and intent prevail, conduct is upright, spirit flourishes, and qi does not scatter. Thus, one hears all, sees all, accomplishes all, and no misfortune or calamity can enter, nor can evil qi assail.

Therefore, those who seek much gain little; those who see broadly know little. Heaven’s creation of things: what does not move grows like a plant; what moves crawls horizontally; what is planted stands upright. Humans combine the functions of both movement and stillness—thus they are more numinous than the myriad things. The four limbs, the hundred parts, spirit, blood, and qi—how can one not cherish them? How can one seek excessively?

Kings, dukes, and great officials, entrusted with the world, must also encompass humanity and all things, loving and nurturing them as they do their own bodies—only then is the Dao fulfilled. The orifices are the doors and windows of spirit; blood and qi are the messengers and sentinels of the five viscera. Thus, when the ears and eyes indulge in sounds and sights, the five viscera become agitated and unsettled, blood and qi surge and do not rest, spirit gallops and does not guard. Though fortune and misfortune come like mountains, one cannot recognize them.

Thus, the sage cherishes without excess. If the sage’s ears, eyes, and spirit are profoundly penetrating, unenticed by external allure, and if his intent and qi do not lose their clarity, with few desires, the five viscera are at peace. When spirit guards within and does not stray from the body, one can observe beyond past ages, within future affairs, and between fortune and misfortune—all becomes clearly visible. Therefore, the farther one strays, the less one knows. This is to say that spirit must not be allowed to indulge externally.

Thus, the five colors confuse the eyes, making them unclear; the five sounds enter the ears, making them dull; the five flavors disorder the mouth, causing sores; preferences and aversions disturb the heart, making conduct reckless. Desires make human qi excessive; likes and dislikes exhaust human essence. If one does not swiftly cast them aside, intent and qi will daily diminish. Though the Yellow River is vast, wind and sun deplete it. Though spirit may be sovereign, worldly desires disturb it.

Nothing escapes this diminishing. The sage, profoundly penetrating, is free from allure and longing. Spirit is firmly secured within, while the body remains at ease externally. The chamber of the heart is empty and void, and the numinous comes to dwell therein. Beyond past ages and before future affairs, nothing is not illuminated—all due to the heart’s emptiness.

The Way of nurturing life is no other than this: its method is like tending a horse—simply remove what harms it. The reason people cannot fulfill their natural lifespan is their excessive indulgence in the pursuit of life. Only one who takes no thought for life thereby attains long life.

Heaven and earth move in mutual resonance; the myriad things are gathered into one. To know the One is to know all things; to fail to know the One is to know nothing. I dwell in the world as but one thing among things—yet things are also things. How can one thing assess another?

The desire for life cannot be served; the aversion to death cannot be refused. To despise lowliness cannot be hated; to delight in honor cannot be rejoiced. Following one’s endowment and settling into stillness, daring not to go to extremes—daring not to go to extremes, one reaches the utmost joy.

Some things arise without being made, sought, or nurtured—this is Heaven. Those that must be made, sought, and nurtured belong to man. If man errs by harboring the intent to make or seek, it is like forcing the sprouts to grow, only to bring harm, injuring life and losing nurture, failing to fulfill the natural lifespan.

The sage, resonant with Heaven and earth, is one with things. He does not augment life nor reject death; he scorns lowliness yet does not hate it, honors nobility yet does not rejoice. Following his endowment and settling into stillness, daring not to go to extremes, he reaches the utmost joy.

**Guarding Emptiness**
Laozi said: The so-called sage adapts to the times and rests content in his place, engaging with the world and delighting in his work. Grief and joy are deviations of virtue; likes and dislikes are burdens of the heart; delight and anger are excesses of the Dao. In life, he moves with Heaven’s course; in death, he transforms with things. At rest, he harmonizes with yin’s power; in motion, he flows with yang’s current. Thus, the heart is the master of the body, and spirit is the heart’s treasure. If the body labors without rest, it withers; if essence is expended without cease, it is exhausted. Therefore, the sage follows this without daring to transgress.

Responding to being with non-being, he exhausts its principles; receiving the full with the empty, he fathoms its limits. Serene, joyful, empty, and still, he fulfills his destiny. Neither distant nor close, he embraces virtue and kindles harmony, aligning with Heaven. He borders the Dao and neighbors virtue, neither initiating fortune nor preceding misfortune. Death and life bring no change to him—this is called utmost spirit. With spirit, one seeks and obtains all, accomplishes all. The sage empties himself to wander the world, moves with Heaven, and acts in accord with the times. Neither profit nor harm sways his heart; neither death nor life alters him. Receiving the full with the empty, he embraces virtue and kindles harmony. Misfortune does not precede him; fortune does not initiate with him. When affairs arise, he responds like an echo—divinely. When the response ceases, he is still. This is called guarding emptiness.

**Guarding the Undivided**
Laozi said: To regard the world as light is to keep spirit unburdened; to see the myriad things as minute is to keep the heart unconfused. To equalize life and death is to keep the will unafraid; to harmonize with change is to keep names from dazzling. The perfected man leans on an unshakable pillar, walks an unobstructed path, draws from an inexhaustible store, and learns from an undying teacher. Wherever he goes, he succeeds; wherever he turns, he penetrates. Whether bending or stretching, bowing or rising, he holds fast to destiny without confusion, turning through fortune and misfortune. Profit and harm cannot trouble his heart.

One who acts with righteousness may be pressed with benevolence but cannot be coerced with arms; he may be guided with righteousness but cannot be lured with profit. The noble man dies for righteousness—he cannot be detained by wealth or honor. He acts for righteousness and cannot be frightened by death—how much more so for one who practices non-action? One who practices non-action is without burden. The unburdened man takes the world as his shadow and pillar. Looking up to the ranks of the perfected, deeply probing the intent of the Dao and virtue, and examining the conduct of the vulgar below—this is truly shameful.

One who takes no thought for the world is like striking a drum to summon learning. Non-being gives rise to being, yet being is not without non-being. When being perishes, it returns to non-being. Being cannot exist without non-being; non-being is the root of being, yet being cannot sustain itself. To know the benefit of non-being and the use of being is to know that non-being is not nothingness—where nothingness is not nothing, there is the Dao. Being does not endure forever; what does not endure forever is a thing. Thus, the sage is rich without possessing, poor without lacking. Equalizing self and others, unifying life and death, he is not burdened in spirit. What does not perish in the self resides here—this is called guarding the undivided.

**Guarding Equanimity**
Laozi said: Esteem and wealth are what men covet, yet compared to the body, they are base. Therefore, the sage eats enough to fill the belly and replenish qi, wears enough to cover the body and ward off cold. He satisfies his nature and rejects excess, neither coveting gain nor hoarding abundance. His eyes do not gaze, his ears do not listen, his mouth does not speak, his heart does not scheme. He discards cleverness and returns to simplicity, rests his spirit, and abandons cunning. Without preferences or aversions, this is called great penetration.

To remove filth and cast off burdens, nothing surpasses never having departed from the source. What could then not be accomplished? One who knows the harmony of nurturing life cannot be lured by profit; one who unites inner and outer signs cannot be tempted by power. The outer limit of the outer is vast; the inner limit of the inner is most noble. To know the great and noble—where could one not succeed?

The Dao values guarding; with guarding, there is accomplishment. The heart values equanimity; with equanimity, there is no tilting. The world’s esteem and wealth are what all men covet. Covetousness gives rise to an unbalanced heart—this is not the Way of guarding. Thus, the sage eats to fill his belly, wears enough to cover his body, and harbors no greed for excess accumulation.

Thus, with a heart at peace and qi settled, the spirit does not stray outward—this accords with the great constancy of the Dao and is called "guarding equilibrium."

**Guarding the Easy (4)**
Laozi said: Those of old who embodied the Dao ordered their emotions and nature, cultivated their heart’s arts, nurtured them with harmony, and maintained them with moderation. They delighted in the Dao and forgot lowliness; their inner power was secure, and they forgot poverty. If nature has no desires, there is nothing unattainable through desirelessness; if the heart has no joys, there is nothing unachievable through joylessness. What harms nature is not allowed to burden virtue; what disrupts life is not permitted to disturb harmony. They did not indulge the body or follow whim, yet their institutions could serve as models for the world. They ate according to their stomach’s measure, clothed themselves according to their form’s needs, dwelled where their body fit, and acted according to their nature’s inclination. They left the world’s surplus without claiming it and entrusted the myriad things without seeking profit—how could poverty or wealth, nobility or baseness cause them to lose their nature and life? Those who do so may be said to have embodied the Dao.

"Much ease brings many difficulties"—this speaks of affairs. But in terms of the Dao, it is not so. Those of old who embodied the Dao cultivated their heart and nature, made their bodies at ease before acting, settled their intentions before moving, delighted in the Dao and rested in constancy, and did not attempt what was difficult or extraordinary. Thus, their institutions had laws, their bearing was observable, and they acted with composure—this is called "guarding the easy."

**Guarding Purity (5)**
Laozi said: Humans receive qi from Heaven, and their ears and eyes respond to sounds and sights, their nose and mouth to fragrances and odors, their hungry skin to cold and warmth—all share the same nature. Yet some die, some live; some become noble, some base. The difference lies in how they are governed.

The spirit is the wellspring of wisdom; when the spirit is clear, wisdom is bright. Wisdom is the storehouse of the heart; when wisdom is impartial, the heart is at peace. No one mirrors themselves in flowing water but in still water, for it is clear and tranquil. Thus, when the spirit is clear and the intent is calm, one can perceive the true nature of things. Those who employ this must borrow from non-employment. A bright mirror is not sullied by dust and grime; a clear spirit is not misled by cravings. Wherever the heart reaches, the spirit is luminously present; returning to emptiness, agitation dissipates and rest is stored—this is the wandering of the sage.

Thus, those who govern the world must first comprehend the nature of life and destiny. Water is clear and mirrors things; the spirit is clear and perceives the Dao. Humans receive qi from Heaven in the same way, so why should my perception of the Dao in the heart be uniquely different? Because the heart is clear. The sage’s heart is bright as still water: when things come, they are seen; when they depart, it returns to stillness—how could anything linger in my heart? A clear mirror is unperturbed—this is called "guarding purity."

**Guarding Authenticity (6)**
Laozi said: The so-called sage simply accords with nature: eating according to the stomach’s measure, clothing according to the body’s form, restraining the self so that greedy and corrupt thoughts have no way to arise. Thus, those who can possess the world must not treat the world as their possession; those who can attain fame and reputation must not seek it through transgressive conduct. Truly comprehending the nature of life and destiny, benevolence and righteousness naturally adhere.

If the spirit is unobscured and the heart unburdened, all is penetrating and unimpeded, serene and untroubled—power and profit cannot lure, sounds and sights cannot seduce, eloquence cannot persuade, cunning cannot move, and bravery cannot frighten. This is the wandering of the true person. Those who give life do not live; those who transform do not change. Those who do not grasp this Dao, though their wisdom governs heaven and earth, their brilliance illuminates the sun and moon, their eloquence unravels linked rings, and their words moisten metal and stone, are still of no benefit in governing the world. Thus, the sage does not lose what they guard.

The sage follows nature, guards utmost authenticity, aligns with the times, and understands the hearts of the people. Only by not opposing the nature of the myriad things can the heart congeal and the form dissolve, becoming pure and unceasing—this is called "guarding authenticity."

**Guarding Stillness (7)**
Laozi said: Stillness, silence, tranquility, and simplicity nurture life; harmony, joy, emptiness, and nothingness uphold virtue. When the outer does not disturb the inner, nature attains its proper place; when stillness is unmoving and harmony is settled, virtue rests in its position. Nurturing life to order the world and embracing virtue to live out one’s years—this may be called embodying the Dao.

Those who do so have unobstructed blood and qi, no stagnant qi in the five viscera, and are neither warped by fortune nor sullied by fame or blame. Without possessing the world, who could aid them? Having talent but not meeting the times, one cannot even free oneself—how much less without the Dao?

Those who see the tip of an autumn hair cannot hear the sound of thunder; those who tune their ears to the sounds of gold and jade cannot see the shape of Mount Tai. Thus, a small focus causes one to forget the great. Now, when the myriad things come, they snatch at our nature and seize our essence like a spring’s source—though one may wish not to receive them, how is it possible?

If a basin of water is clear, one can see their eyebrows after a day; if it is muddied by a single stir, one cannot discern its shape. The human spirit is difficult to keep clear and easy to muddy, like a basin of water. The Dao of heaven and earth is still, so things grow without being forced; the sage’s governance is still, so the people are naturally capable. If one harbors a mind to seek merit and stir up affairs, disturbing nature and disordering heaven’s constancy, stillness is lost and movement is confused.

Only by not pursuing strange changes is this called "guarding stillness."

**Guarding the Law (8)**
Laozi said: The highest sage models Heaven; the next esteems worthies; the lowest employs ministers. Employing ministers is the path to peril and ruin; esteeming worthies is the source of delusion and confusion; modeling Heaven is the Dao of governing heaven and earth.

Emptiness and stillness are the foundation: emptiness receives all, stillness sustains all. Knowing the Dao of emptiness and stillness, one can endure from beginning to end. Thus, the sage takes stillness as governance and movement as disorder. Therefore it is said: Do not disturb or agitate, and the myriad things will clarify themselves; do not startle or alarm, and the myriad things will order themselves—this is the Dao of Heaven.

The highest sage models Heaven, and the hundred bones are ordered, the myriad transformations at peace. The next esteems worthies, and the law proceeds from the self—this is the source of delusion. The lowest employs ministers, and the law emerges from the mouths of the many—this is the omen of peril and ruin. Cultivating the self without the Dao is to be deluded in affairs and lose one’s spirit; governing the state without the Dao is to disorder policy and harm the people. Only taking the embodiment of the Dao as the foundation is this called "guarding the Law."

**Guarding Weakness (9)**
Laozi said: The Son of Heaven and the feudal lords take the world or the state as their household, regard the myriad things as their livestock, embrace the vastness of all under Heaven, and possess the abundance of the ten thousand things. Yet when their qi is replete and their ambitions arrogant, the great use troops to invade the small, the small grow insolent and overbear their superiors, and their hearts indulge in extravagance and excess—this is like whirlwinds and violent rains, which cannot endure for long. Therefore the sage governs with the Dao, holds to the One and practices non-action, without harming the primal qi. Perceiving the small and guarding softness, withdrawing and possessing nothing, modeling the rivers and seas, the rivers and seas do not act—thus merit and fame transform themselves. Not forcing, one can complete one’s kingship; acting as the female of all under Heaven, one’s spirit does not die. Self-cherishing completes one’s nobility. With the power of ten thousand chariots and the ten thousand things as one’s merit and fame, the burden of authority is most weighty—one cannot treat oneself lightly. Treating oneself lightly, merit and fame will not be achieved.

The Dao is great yet accomplished through the small, abundant yet ruled by the few. Thus the sage governs all under Heaven with the Dao. That which is soft, weak, subtle, and mysterious perceives the small; that which is frugal, sparing, diminished, and deficient perceives the few. Perceiving the small, one can complete the great; perceiving the few, one can complete the beautiful. Weakness is the function of the Dao, not cowardice—it is but guarding the harmonious and balanced. The Son of Heaven takes all under Heaven as his household; the feudal lords take their states as their households. Regarding the people as oneself, not relying on power and position to assert strength, not using arms to oppress the multitude—if distant peoples do not submit, then cultivate civil virtue to attract them. Great resources complete the small and become abundant; supporting the few as the foundation, advancing without harm, one attains peace and tranquility—this is called "guarding weakness."

The Way of Heaven suppresses the high and elevates the low, diminishes excess and supplements insufficiency. The rivers and seas occupy the lowest places, thus all under Heaven returns to and serves them. Therefore the sage, humble, modest, pure, and yielding, perceives lowness; emptying the heart of all, perceives insufficiency. Perceiving lowness, one can attain loftiness; perceiving insufficiency, one can complete one’s worthiness. Those who are haughty do not stand; those who are extravagant do not endure; the strong and overbearing perish; the overflowing and full are ruined. Whirlwinds and violent rains do not last the day; small valleys cannot remain full for even a moment. Whirlwinds and torrential rains carry the qi of strength and overbearing, thus they cannot last and perish; small valleys occupy the place of strength and overbearing, thus they cannot but be seized.

Therefore the sage holds to the female and the yielding, abandons arrogance and extravagance, and dares not act with the qi of strength and overbearing. Holding to the female and the yielding, one can establish one’s maleness and virility; not daring arrogance and extravagance, one can endure long. The Way of Heaven diminishes excess and supplements insufficiency, detests fullness and favors modesty. Those who rely on force become hegemons; those who rely on virtue become kings. The weak overcomes the strong.

When people impose harsh governance and oppress the people, it is like Heaven’s violent winds breaking trees—primordial qi vents its wrath, and strength cannot endure long. Thus the sage is humble and lowly, guarding only weakness.

Laozi said: The Way of Heaven reaches its extreme and then reverses; when full, it diminishes—this is seen in the sun and moon. Therefore the sage daily diminishes, and the primal qi does not dare become full; daily advancing with the female, his merit and power do not decline—this is the Way of Heaven.

Human nature and emotions all favor the high and detest the low, favor gain and detest loss, favor profit and detest harm, favor honor and detest humility, favor nobility and detest baseness. The multitude acts accordingly, thus cannot succeed; clinging to these, thus cannot attain. Therefore the sage models Heaven: not acting yet accomplishing, not grasping yet attaining. Sharing human emotions yet following a different path, he can endure long.

Thus the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors had vessels of warning, called the *yòuzhī*. When empty, it stands upright; when full, it overturns. Things flourish and then decline; the sun at its zenith moves on; the moon at its full wanes; joy ends in sorrow. Therefore, those with keen hearing and broad wisdom guard with foolishness; those with extensive learning and eloquence guard with restraint; those with martial strength and courage guard with awe; those with wealth and vastness guard with narrowness; those whose virtue extends to all under Heaven guard with yielding. These five are how the former kings guarded all under Heaven. Those who follow this Dao do not desire fullness, thus can wear out without renewal.

The Dao reaches its extreme and then reverses; things flourish and then decline—this is the constant principle. The *zhī* vessel full then overturns; the blade hard then snaps—this is the constant of things. If one can observe the constant principles of things and not rely on power to oppress the multitude and assert strength, then one avoids the harm of stumbling and falling. Preserving softness and weakness, one can endure long.

Laozi said: The sage closes with yin and opens with yang, able to reach the state of no joy—thus there is nothing not joyful. Being without anything not joyful is to reach ultimate joy. Therefore, inner joy is external, not external joy within. Thus there is self-joy, and having this attainment, one is honored throughout all under Heaven. The reason for this is taking all under Heaven as the essential for all under Heaven, not residing in that, but in the self; not in others, but in one’s own person. When the person attains, the ten thousand things are complete.

Thus one who has mastered the discourse on the arts of the heart is external to desires, likes, and aversions. Therefore, there is nothing to delight in, nothing to anger, nothing to rejoice in, nothing to suffer. The myriad things are profoundly united, without right or wrong. Thus the scholar has a constant discourse; the woman has unchanging conduct. Not relying on power to be honored, not needing wealth to be rich, not requiring force to be strong, not valuing goods and wealth, not coveting worldly fame, not taking nobility as security, not taking baseness as peril—form, spirit, qi, and will each reside in their proper place.

When Heaven is about to rain, the pillars’ bases first moisten...

When spring is near, the ice thaws first; when a man is about to die, his tongue stiffens first—qi effects this. The soft and weak belong to the realm of life; the hard and strong belong to the realm of death. Those who are violent and unyielding yet do not meet their end are the most extreme of the violent and unyielding. Only when qi and form are harmonious and weak, merging with things in profound unity and growing together without harm, does one approach the Way.

The form is the dwelling of life; qi is the origin of life; spirit is the regulator of life. If any one of these loses its proper place, all three are injured. Thus, when spirit presides, form follows and benefits; when form presides, spirit follows and suffers. The greedy and covetous, blinded by power and profit, lured by fame and rank, strive with excessive cunning to rise above others in the world. Their essence and spirit daily wane and drift far away; long immersed and unable to return, their form becomes closed and resistant, leaving no path for entry. Thus they often suffer blindness, forgetfulness, and self-loss.

Essence, spirit, will, and qi—when still, they daily grow and strengthen; when restless, they daily deplete and age. Therefore, the sage nurtures their spirit, harmonizes and softens their qi, levels their form, and drifts with the Dao. In this way, the transformations of the myriad things all find their counterpart, and the changes of a hundred affairs all meet their response.

Spirit depends on form to live; essence depends on qi to flourish. Nurturing one another without losing harmony—this is the essence of cultivating life. But those who are empty and arrogant, relying on qi to contend with things, will deplete their essence and exhaust their spirit, often suffering blindness and forgetfulness—signs of approaching death.

Thus the sage weakens their form, harmonizes their qi, and conceals their spirit, attaining the Way of the Nine Guardians. Does the sage deceive us? To aspire to sagehood is to become a sage; to aspire to worthiness is to become worthy.

In guarding simplicity, Laozi said: The so-called True Person is one whose nature aligns with the Dao. Thus, they have yet seem without; are full yet seem empty. They govern their inner self, not their outer self, clarifying the Great Simplicity. They act without contrivance and return to simplicity, embracing their origin and holding fast to spirit, roaming the root of Heaven and Earth. Vague and drifting beyond the dust and grime, carefree in the work of non-action, they carry no schemes or cunning in their hearts. Discerning the unborrowed, they do not shift with things. Perceiving the transformations of affairs, they hold to their source. Their mind and intent focus inward, comprehending fortune and misfortune as one. Abiding without knowing what they do, moving without knowing where they go, they know without learning, see without looking, accomplish without acting, and distinguish without governing.

They respond to stimuli, move under compulsion, and go when there is no choice—like light fearing darkness, like a shadow following form. Following the Dao as their guide, they act when conditions are met. Vast and empty, clear and still, they take the thousand lives as one transformation, the ten thousand differences as one source. They possess essence yet do not employ it; possess spirit yet do not use it. They guard the simplicity of the Great Undifferentiated, standing at the center of utmost purity. Their sleep is dreamless, their wisdom unaroused. In movement, they leave no form; in stillness, no substance. Existing yet seeming absent, living yet seeming dead, they enter and exit without gap, commanding ghosts and spirits.

This is how essence and spirit ascend to the Dao: by ensuring their free flow without losing their origin, day and night without interruption, harmonizing with things like spring. This is to unite and give birth to the seasons within the heart. Thus, though the form may decay, the spirit never transforms. Responding to change without changing, through a thousand shifts and ten thousand turns, it remains without limit. The changing return to formlessness; the unchanging are born with Heaven and Earth. Therefore, that which gives life has never truly lived; what it gives life to is but life’s transformation. The transforming has never truly transformed; what it transforms is but transformation.

This is the wandering of the True Person, the pure Dao. The True Person guards the simplicity of the Great Undifferentiated, roams the root of Heaven and Earth, and merges with the Great Thoroughfare—vast and without trace. Only by not possessing a self do they not lack things. They laugh when others laugh, weep when others weep, yet through a thousand changes and ten thousand transformations, they never reach an end. The changing return to formlessness, yet there is change without change. The unchanging are born with Heaven and Earth, yet there is life without life.

The Nine Guardians of before and the guarding of simplicity that follows—these are one becoming nine, and ten returning to one. This is called roaming with the Creator. *The Thoroughly Illuminated True Classic, with Collected Explanations, Scroll Three* ends here.

**Scroll Four of *The Thoroughly Illuminated True Classic, with Collected Explanations*
Compiled by Nan’guzi Du Daojian**

**The Chapter of Tally-Words**

Laozi said: The Dao is supremely high, without above; supremely deep, without below. Level as the water-level, straight as the marking-line, round as the compass, square as the carpenter’s square. It enfolds Heaven and Earth yet has no inside or outside; it penetrates and covers all yet encounters no obstruction. Thus, one who embodies the Dao is neither angered nor delighted. Seated, they are without care; asleep, they dream not. They name things as they appear and respond to affairs as they arise.

Tallies manifest trust; words convey sincerity. The world speaks of tally-mandates, tally-seals, golden tallies, and jade tallies—all serve to manifest trust and convey sincerity, so that this evokes and that responds. Hence, they are called *tally-words*.

The Dao is without limit in height or depth, connecting far and near. It is formless and nameless, yet possesses sentiment and trust.

The sage embodies the Dao, harboring no private joy or anger. They name things as they appear and respond to affairs as they arise. Unspoken words may yet serve as tally and trust.

Laozi said: Those who covet fame must create affairs. Once affairs arise, they abandon the public and pursue the private, forsake the Dao and rely on themselves. They perform good deeds for praise and establish virtue for renown. Thus, governance does not follow principle, and affairs do not accord with the times. Governance that does not follow principle invites much blame; affairs that do not accord with the times yield no merit. Acting recklessly to meet expectations, success cannot absolve their blame, while failure is enough to destroy them. Fame is the instrument of self-destruction—what the sage guards against.

Those who covet fame must violate Heaven and defy the Dao, abandon the public and pursue the private, seek praise and establish renown, creating affairs to harm the multitude. Thus, governance does not follow principle, merit cannot outweigh blame, affairs fail and destroy the self—this is no falsehood.

Laozi said: Be not the corpse of fame, the storehouse of schemes, the bearer of tasks, or the master of cunning. Conceal yourself in formlessness, act without negligence. Be not the first to seek fortune, nor the initiator of misfortune. Begin in formlessness, move only when unavoidable. Desire fortune? First distance misfortune. Desire benefit? First avoid harm.

Thus, those who act yet seek tranquility—if they lose their tranquility, they court danger. Those who govern—if they lose their governance, they invite chaos. Therefore, desire not to be as jade, restless and polished; nor as stone, solitary and unyielding.

The finer the grain, the sooner the skin is flayed; the finer the antlers, the sooner the body is slain. Sweet springs must run dry; straight trees must be felled. Florid words later become transgressions; jade within stone harms the mountain. The calamities of the black-headed people surely precede their words.

The retribution of good and evil follows like shadow after form. Good deeds summon blessings; evil deeds summon misfortune. Evil must not be done—how then can fame be coveted?

Those who seek tranquility—if tranquility is lost, danger follows. Those who seek governance—if governance is lost, chaos ensues. Fine grain invites flaying; fine antlers invite slaughter. Never has harm come that was not self-invited.

Laozi said: When the times move, act in accord. Those who do not know the Dao mistake fortune for misfortune. Heaven is the canopy; Earth, the carriage-pole. Those who skillfully employ the Dao never meet their end. Earth is the carriage-wheel; Heaven, the canopy. Those who skillfully employ the Dao never suffer harm.

When the five phases are arrayed, there must be conquest. All beneath Heaven’s cover is fitting. Thus, to know yet claim ignorance is sickness; to be ignorant yet claim knowledge is also sickness.

Heaven as canopy covers all; Earth as carriage bears all. Heaven and Earth’s grace toward humanity is boundlessly great. The sage emulates this: acting in accord with the times, every movement follows. Like the five phases, they mutually generate and mutually conquer.

Those who skillfully employ the Dao know without speaking of knowledge—this is superior. To be ignorant yet speak of knowledge is sickness.

Laozi said: Mountains give birth to metal; stone gives birth to jade. They turn and flay one another. Trees give birth to insects, which return to devour them. Humans create affairs, which return to harm them.

Those who love affairs are never without misfortune; those who contend for profit are never without exhaustion. Skilled swimmers drown; skilled riders fall. Each suffers calamity through what they love.

Success lies in timing, not in contention; governance lies in the Dao, not in sagacity. Earth abides below and does not contend for height—thus it is secure and never imperiled. Water flows downward and does not contend for speed—thus it departs yet is never late.

Therefore, the sage, being without attachment, suffers no loss; being without action, meets no defeat. Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things mutually steal to nourish one another. When theft is fitting, there is peace; when theft is unfitting, there is harm.

Thus, food can nourish humans, yet also harm them. The people can support their lord, yet also rebel against him. When scholars establish laws and initiate affairs to exploit the people’s strength, it may seem beneficial at first, but in the end, none do not harm themselves.

Know that success lies in timing, not in contention; governance lies in the Dao, not in sagacity—then there will be no loss from mutual theft.

Laozi said: One word cannot be exhausted; two words are the ancestral teaching of the world. Three words make the lords of states mighty; four words make the world balanced.

Integrity and trust cannot be exhausted. Virtue and the Dao are the world’s ancestral teaching. Exalting the worthy and virtuous makes the lords of states mighty. Loathing the few and loving the many makes the world balanced.

Few faults in speech, few regrets in conduct—this lies in action, not in words. Holding the great image, the world comes to you—this lies in virtue, not in peril.

The mouth alone gives rise to good and incites strife—how can words not be cautious?

Laozi said: Humans face three deaths, none of which are fated. Immoderate eating and drinking, despising and degrading the body—illness kills them together. Insatiable in pleasure, unceasing in desire—punishment kills them together.

With few to confront the many, with weak to oppress the strong—warfare kills them together.

Life must end in death; who can escape it? To die without fate is truly pitiable.

Gluttony and lust bring death by illness,
Greed for gain brings death by punishment,
Tyranny brings death by war.

Though these three deaths differ in cause, their lack of fate is one.

Laozi said:
To those who give generously, their reward is splendid;
To those who harbor great grudges, their calamity runs deep.
To give little yet expect much,
To hoard resentment yet claim no misfortune—
Such things have never existed.

Observe how they go, and you shall know how they came.
The principle of giving and receiving is like planting melons and gathering melons,
Planting fruit and gathering fruit.
The law of gratitude and resentment is the same.

Laozi said:
To fathom Heaven’s decree,
To cultivate the mind’s method,
To regulate affection and aversion,
To harmonize nature and disposition—
Then the Way of governance is complete.

To fathom Heaven’s decree is to not be perplexed by fortune and misfortune;
To cultivate the mind’s method is to not indulge in false joy or anger;
To regulate affection and aversion is to not covet what is useless;
To harmonize nature and disposition is to let desire not exceed proper measure.
Not to be perplexed by fortune and misfortune means action and stillness follow natural order;
Not to indulge in false joy or anger means rewards and punishments are impartially given;
Not to covet what is useless means desire does not harm innate nature;
To let desire not exceed proper measure means nurturing life through contentment.

These four principles require no external seeking, no reliance on others—
They are attained by turning inward.

Heaven’s decree, the mind’s method, affection and aversion, nature and disposition—
These four are interlinked, like the moon reflected in water,
Its waxing and waning, roundness and absence, mirroring its form.

The rectitude or perversity of the mind’s method determines fortune and misfortune.

Laozi said:
Do not seek actions that invite censure,
Do not resent others’ disapproval of you.
Cultivate virtue that earns praise,
Do not seek praise for yourself.
You cannot ensure misfortune will not come—trust that you do not court it,
Nor can you ensure fortune will arrive—trust that you do not reject it.
When misfortune comes, it is not of your making, so even in adversity, you need not grieve;
When fortune arrives, it is not of your doing, so even in prosperity, you need not boast.

Thus, in quietude you find joy,
In non-action you govern.
Trust yourself—what need for seeking?
Resent not others—what need for aversion?
Virtue and conduct stem from yourself; praise and blame come from others.
Thus, praise does not spur you to greater effort,
Nor does criticism deter you.
You dwell in quietude and take joy,
Governance is not a burden to you.

Laozi said:
The Dao is to hold fast to what you already possess,
Not to seek what you do not yet have.
To seek what you do not yet have is to lose what you already possess;
To follow what you already possess is to attain what you desire.

Governance not yet secured against disorder will inevitably falter;
Actions not yet free of fault will inevitably meet setback.
Thus, no greater fortune than avoiding misfortune,
No greater benefit than avoiding loss.

The Dao cannot be pursued to gain advantage,
But it can bring tranquility and avert harm.
Thus, one may always avoid misfortune but not always attain fortune,
Always avoid guilt but not always earn merit.

The Dao is vast and obscure,
Following Heaven’s might,
Aligning with Heaven’s qi,
Without scheming or planning,
Without storing or hoarding.
To welcome without inviting,
To send without bidding farewell—
Though people move east, west, south, or north,
They stand firm at the center.
Thus, amid crookedness, you remain straight,
Flowing with the world yet never straying from your path.
You neither seek goodness nor shun ugliness,
Following Heaven’s Way without initiation,
Without self-aggrandizement,
Following Heaven’s principle without premeditation,
Without neglecting the times,
Aligning with Heaven’s timing,
Without seeking gain,
Without rejecting fortune,
Conforming to Heaven’s decree.

Within, no extraordinary fortune;
Without, no extraordinary misfortune.
Thus, misfortune and fortune do not arise,
And who would dare harm others?

Therefore, supreme virtue speaks of shared paths,
Acts in shared fortune,
Unites hearts above and below,
With no divergent ways.
Those with peripheral visions are dismissed from error,
The path to goodness is opened,
And the people turn toward rectitude.

Greed for gain and forgetfulness of loss—
A common affliction among all.
Without desire, there is tranquility—
This is called the Perfect Person.

In the world, there are things that cannot be guaranteed.
To hold fast to what you possess may bring what you lack;
To seek what you lack may lose what you possess.
Thus, those who follow the Dao
Have no extraordinary fortune within,
No extraordinary misfortune without,
And align with Heaven—
Who could harm them?

Laozi said:
To do good is to encourage,
To do evil is to invite observation.
Encouragement brings nobility,
Observation brings calamity.
Thus, the Dao cannot be pursued to seek fame,
But it can be cultivated in retreat to refine oneself.

Therefore, the sage does not seek fame through action,
Nor reputation through knowledge.
Governance follows naturally,
And the self remains unburdened.

What is done may fail,
What is sought may not be attained,
And people may falter while the Dao remains unobstructed.

To have wisdom yet do nothing is the same as having no wisdom;
To have ability yet act not is the same as having no ability.
To have wisdom as though without wisdom,
To have ability as though without ability—
When the Dao is fully understood, human talent is extinguished.

Human understanding and the Dao cannot both flourish;
If people love fame, they will not use the Dao.
When the Dao surpasses humanity, fame fades,
And when fame flourishes, the Dao is obscured—
Then peril and ruin follow.

To do good without seeking fortune yet fortune comes,
To do evil without inviting misfortune yet misfortune arises.
To leave a stench for ten thousand generations,
Or a fragrant name for a thousand years—
The difference is clear.

Thus, if one values fame excessively,
The mind will not adhere to the Dao;
If one delves deeply into the Dao,
The body will not seek fame.
This is the distinction between Heaven and humanity.

Laozi said:
To let honest men divide wealth is not as fair as
Setting fixed portions and casting lots—
For those with intent in division
Are not as impartial as those without intent.

To let virtuous men guard wealth is not as secure as
Sealing the doors and keeping it sealed—
For those with desire in guarding
Are not as tranquil as those without desire.

When others point out your flaws, resentment arises;
When overseers see your ugliness, you mend your ways.
If one can interact with others without entangling oneself,
They are free from burdens.

To divide wealth by casting lots,
Intent is less fair than disinterest;
To guard wealth by sealing doors,
Desire is less secure than contentment.

「To point out another’s flaws brings resentment,
Yet to love one’s own faults is unnatural.
To see another’s ugliness prompts self-improvement,
Yet to cherish one’s own flaws is self-deception.
Only those who interact with others without entangling themselves in their affairs
Have no such faults, and others find none in them.

Laozi said:
When dealing with others,
If one does not offer treasures or wealth,
They will use humble words,
Yet their greed will never be sated.
With a humble demeanor, they avoid flattery,
And their discussions and dealings remain uncorrupted.

To bind with oaths and alliances,
Yet break them before the appointed day—
This is why the virtuous do not outwardly display benevolence and righteousness
While inwardly cultivating virtue and moral power.
They govern their own lands,
Expand their territories,
Encourage the people to defend their lives,
Strengthen their walls and fortresses,
And unite the people in heart,
So that together they may guard the ancestral altars and the state.
Thus, they do not punish the innocent,
Nor exploit the scarce for private gain—
This is the Dao of preserving completeness,
The principle of ensuring benefit.

To deal with others by offering treasures and wealth,
Their greed will never be sated.
To bind with humble words,
Their agreements will falter before the appointed day.

Only when one inwardly cultivates virtue and moral power,
And unites the people in heart,
Can they guard the ancestral altars and the state,
And protect the people.
Then their Dao is complete.

Laozi said:
The Sage does not conquer their own heart,
While the common people cannot conquer their desires.
The virtuous follow the upright *qi*,
The petty follow the perverse *qi*.
What aligns with nature within,
And conforms to righteousness without,
Moving according to principle without clashing with things—
This is the upright *qi*.
What indulges in flavors and luxuries,
Revels in sound and color,
Acts on whims and anger,
Without regard for future consequences—
This is the perverse *qi*.
The perverse and the upright harm each other,
Desire and nature conflict,
They cannot coexist—
One flourishes while the other declines.
Thus, the Sage diminishes desire to follow nature.

The eyes love color,
The ears love sound,
The nose loves fragrance,
The mouth loves flavor—
When all are indulged,
They cannot escape the harm of craving and desire.
The ears, eyes, nose, and mouth,
If they do not know what to desire,
Are governed by the heart,
Each finding its proper place.
From this, it is clear that desire cannot be conquered.

The Dao-heart and the human-heart,
The distinction between Heaven’s principle and human desire.
When principle prevails,
All actions are Heavenly.
When desire prevails,
All actions are human—
This is the distinction between the virtuous and the petty.

Principle and desire conquer each other,
The perverse and the upright harm each other—
The virtuous do not indulge in such,
How much less would the Sage?

Laozi said:
To cultivate the body and nurture nature,
One must regulate sleep and rest,
Moderate food and drink,
Harmonize joy and anger,
Balance movement and stillness.
What is gained within,
The perverse *qi* cannot enter.

To adorn the exterior while harming the interior,
To indulge emotions while damaging the spirit—
Those who see only outward appearances
Are blind to their true nature.
He who forgets his virtue for even a moment
Will be constrained in his nature,
He who forgets his true nature within a hundred paces
Will burden his form.
Thus, those with beautiful feathers
Are wounded in their bones,
Those with lush branches
Are harmed at their roots.
None in the world can possess both beauties.
The true Dao nurtures the spirit,
The human Dao nurtures the form.
What is gained within is lasting,
What is gained without is fleeting.
To distance oneself from sound and color,
To moderate flavors and tastes—
This is the human Dao of nurturing the form.
To sever thought and contemplation,
To preserve the vital *qi*—
This is the true Dao of nurturing the spirit.
To cultivate the body and nurture nature,
Is to gain both within and without—
How can one seek this through appearance, voice, or countenance?

Laozi said:
Heaven has its light,
Yet it does not fear the people’s darkness.
Earth has its wealth,
Yet it does not fear the people’s poverty.
The truly Daoist, like a mountain,
Remain unmoved and steady.
The traveler takes them as a guide,
Straightforward yet sufficient in their offerings,
Not seeking to bestow,
Nor receiving gratitude in return.
Thus, they are tranquil and endure.
Heaven and Earth have no part in it,
So nothing is taken from them—
No virtue is claimed,
So no resentment arises.
Those who are skilled at anger
Will invite many grievances,
Those who are skilled at giving
Will invite many takings—
Only by following Heaven and Earth’s natural course
Can one conquer principle.
Thus, praise leads to blame,
Goodness leads to evil,
Harm begins with benefit,
Calamity follows fortune—
To not seek benefit is to avoid harm,
To not seek fortune is to avoid calamity.
Preserving completeness is the norm for the body,
Wealth and honor are but temporary lodgings.

The Sage illuminates the land,
Yet the people do not grow dim,
The Sage stores riches in the world,
Yet the people do not grow poor.
To follow Heaven and Earth’s natural course,
To allow all things to grow of their own accord—
Not to hoard for oneself,
Thus no need for public seizure,
Not to bestow lightly,
Thus no need for harsh punishment.
To regard wealth and honor as passing clouds,
Is to preserve one’s true nature.

Laozi said:
The Sage wears no extravagant garments,
Nor practices deceptive conduct.
Their clothing is simple,
Their actions unobserved,
Their way is open yet not ostentatious,
Their poverty is unshaken,
Their honor is not flaunted,
Their obscurity is not humiliating,
Their strangeness is not bizarre—
Their use is the same as all others,
Yet cannot be named—
This is called *Da Tong* (Great Harmony).

Plain yet hidden,
Strange yet not bizarre—
The virtuous do not indulge in such.

Extravagant garments,
Deceptive conduct—
How could these be the Sage’s affairs?
Their brilliance does not dazzle,
Their integrity is not ostentatious—
They simply share in the people’s common use.
This is called *Da Tong* (Great Harmony).

Laozi said:
The Daoist lives straightforward,
Waiting for their fate.
When time arrives,
It cannot be hastened or repelled.
When time departs,
It cannot be pursued or retained.
Thus, the Sage does not advance to seek,
Nor retreat to yield.
Three years of following time,
Time departs and they move on.
Three years of waiting for time,
Time comes after them—
Neither departing nor arriving,
They remain steadfast in their place.

Heaven’s Dao knows no favoritism,
Only virtue is bestowed.
When fortune comes,
It is not sought by oneself,
So they do not claim its merit.
When calamity arrives,
It is not generated by oneself,
So they do not regret their actions.
Their center is serene,
Not burdened by virtue,
The barking of dogs does not startle them,
They trust their own nature—
Their sincerity knows no division.

Thus, those who understand the Dao are not perplexed,
Those who know fate are not anxious.
When the Emperor dies,
Their remains are buried in the wild,
Yet their spirit is enshrined in the Bright Hall,
For the spirit is nobler than the form.
Thus, if the spirit governs the form,
They prosper.
If the form conquers the spirit,
They decline.
Though cleverness may be employed,
It must ultimately return to the spirit—
This is called *Da Tong* (Great Harmony).」

「The Dao is the shared path of all under Heaven. The Sage aligns with their true nature and awaits fate, neither grasping nor rejecting as fortune comes and goes. They stand at the center, governing the four directions—
Not claiming merit when prosperity arrives,
Nor regretting actions when calamity strikes.
Their mind remains tranquil,
Understanding the Dao and knowing fate,
Though cleverness is employed,
It must ultimately return to the spirit—

Laozi says: Those of old who preserved themselves
Found joy in virtue and forgot lowliness,
Thus their resolve was steadfast—*unmovable by ambition*.
They delighted in the Dao and forgot poverty,
Thus their gain was *unshaken by desire*.
Hence, they could be humble yet joyful,
Quiet yet serene.

Measuring life by years,
They fretted over the world’s chaos,
As one might fear the drying of the Yellow River,
Weeping to nourish it instead.
Thus, those who do not fret over the world’s chaos
But rejoice in their own order,
May be spoken to of the Dao.

The Dao reveres virtue and values power,
Giving delight to all hearts and preserving all within.
Thus, it does not stir their mind with poverty or lowliness,
For when their own nature is ordered,
The world’s turmoil is no longer a cause for worry.
Would they then burden themselves with concerns
For a life not even a hundred years long?
Laozi says: There are three sources of resentment in people—
Those with high rank are envied by others,
Those with great office are distrusted by their lord,
Those with abundant wealth are resented by the people.

The higher the rank, the lower the intent,
The greater the office, the smaller the heart,
The richer the wealth, the broader the giving.
Cultivating these three,
Resentment does not arise.

Thus, nobility finds its root in humility,
Elevation in submission as its foundation.
High rank breeds arrogance,
Others will envy it.
Great office fosters pride,
The lord will distrust it.
Abundant wealth without generosity,
Others will resent it.
This is the Dao of *mutual undermining*—

Only through humility and generosity,
Without forgetting lowliness or submission,
Is resentment avoided.

Laozi says: Speech is the means by which one conveys their own nature to others,
Hearing is the means by which others convey their nature to oneself.
If one is both deaf and blind,
The Dao cannot pass through them.
Thus, those afflicted with deafness and blindness,
Do not know how to convey or receive the Dao—
And is it only the body that can be deaf and blind?
The heart too can be sealed,
Unknowing how to convey or receive.
This is the kind of deafness and blindness.

The Dao as the ultimate principle,
All things with form arise from it—
As its kin, they are also close to it.
Those who nourish on grains and *qi*,
All attain longevity—
As its ruler, they are also blessed.
All who are wise learn from it—
As its teacher, they are also enlightened.

Most people harm what is useful by clinging to what is useless,
Thus they know not enough and their days are insufficient.
They seek the Dao in the midst of games of chance,
Their knowledge and insight deepen.
Without hearing or inquiry,
They are like the deaf and blind among people.

To receive form and live,
Is the kin’s favor.
To nourish on grains and attain longevity,
Is the ruler’s blessing.
Through learning, they become enlightened,
Is the teacher’s virtue.
To grow and become an adult,
Is also a kind of nobility.

Speech is the voice of the heart—
To convey oneself to others,
Without words, one cannot be heard;
To receive others into oneself,
Without words, one cannot be understood.
Thus, speech is the expression of the spirit.

If one does not let private words obstruct public words,
And does not harm what is useful by clinging to what is useless,
Then they hear the Dao deeply
And do not stand alone against their superiors.

Laozi says: Human nature,
The heart submits to virtue,
Not to force.
Virtue lies in giving, not in seeking.

Thus, the Sage who wishes to be noble among others,
First makes others noble.
Who wishes to be revered among others,
First reveres others.
Who wishes to conquer others,
First conquers themselves.
Who wishes to humble others,
First humbles themselves.
Hence, nobility and humility,
Reverence and lowliness,
The Dao governs them.

The ancient Sages,
With their words, they led the people,
With their bodies, they followed behind.
Thus, the world gladly pushed them forward,
Yet they did not weary of it,
And gladly bore them up,
Yet they did not grow heavy—
This was virtue overflowing and *qi* in harmony.
Hence, they knew that giving was taking,
And following was leading.
They nearly reached the Dao.

Ritual values precedence,
Speech values humility—
This is human nature,
Thus they can be swayed by virtue,
Not by force.
Hence, the Sage moves with measured steps,
And acts with appropriate timing,
Thus the world gladly pushes them forward
And gladly bears them up.

Laozi says: Those with little virtue but much favor are ridiculed,
Those with inferior talent but high position are in danger,
Those with no great deeds but abundant wealth are despised.
Thus, some things benefit by being increased,
While others are harmed by it.
Some things are harmed by being decreased,
While others benefit by it.

The common people know only to seek what is advantageous,
Yet they do not know to avoid what is harmful.
Only the Sage knows that harm can be advantageous,
And advantage can be harmful.

Thus, the twice-hewn tree,
Its roots must be wounded.
The household that hoards much,
Its future must be cursed.
The greatest advantage,
Becomes a source of harm—
This is the Dao of Heaven.

Virtue is never sated by abundance,
But one’s portion must not be exceeded.
Shallow waters cannot bear a great vessel,
Shallow roots cannot sustain a tall tree.
If a person’s virtue is thin and their talent shallow,
Yet they hold high office without benevolence,
They will rarely avoid ruin.
Only if one knows the reversals of advantage and harm,
And does not cling to excessive hoarding,
Attaining harmony between righteousness and gain,
Then what harm remains?

Laozi says: The petty person pursues gain with *guǒdé* (merely obtain),
The noble person pursues righteousness with *guǒyì* (merely uphold).
Those who do good are not seeking fame,
Yet fame follows them.
Fame does not wait for advantage,
Yet advantage returns to them.
What they seek is the same,
What they strive for is different.

Thus, gain brings harm in its wake.
Those whose words lack truth,
Whose actions lack propriety,
Are petty people.
Those who excel in one matter,
And are skilled in one ability,
Are mediocre.
Those who encompass all and possess all,
Whose abilities are matched by their talent,
Are the Sage.

Round heads and square feet,
Teeth clenched and hair worn,
All are human.
Yet the *qi* they inherit differs,
And wisdom and folly divide them.

The petty person merely obtains,
Their talent outweighs their virtue;
The noble person merely upholds righteousness,
Their virtue outweighs their talent.
If one can ascend or descend,
They are mediocre.
If Heaven grants them many talents,
Then they are the Sage.

Laozi says: Life is a borrowed state,
Death is the return to it.
Thus, in times of order,
They defend righteousness with their life,
In times of chaos,
They defend their life with righteousness.
On the day of death,
Their actions reach their end.

Thus, the noble person carefully attends to this single use alone.»

The life one receives is from Heaven,
The fate one encounters is from the times.
To possess talent yet not meet the age—this is Heaven’s will.
To seek it must follow the Way,
To obtain it lies in fate.

The noble person may act virtuously,
Yet cannot guarantee receiving blessings,
May refrain from wrongdoing,
Yet cannot be certain of avoiding calamity.

Thus, when the time is right, they advance with righteousness—what greater fortune?
When the time is not, they withdraw with propriety—what greater misfortune?
Yet even in poverty and lowliness, they regret nothing,
For they have found what is truly precious.

Born by Heaven’s command,
Shaped into human form—
Life is a borrowed state,
Death is the return to it.
What one encounters in the times
May bring hardship or prosperity—
What is not fate?

To have talent yet not meet the age—this is Heaven’s will.
Thus, the noble person acts virtuously,
Yet blessings are not guaranteed;
They refrain from wrongdoing,
Yet calamity is not always avoided.

When the time is right, they ride the tide;
When it is not, they travel with humility—
Nobility lies within.

Laozi says:
Within the heart, there is the *qi* of harmony and discord.
When the heart is ordered, the *qi* flows smoothly;
When the heart is disordered, the *qi* becomes inverted.
The order or disorder of the heart lies in the Dao.

To attain the Dao is to order the heart;
To lose the Dao is to disorder it.
An ordered heart brings mutual yielding;
A disordered heart brings mutual contention.
Yielding brings virtue;
Contention breeds harm.
Virtue brings smooth *qi*;
Harm brings inverted *qi*.
Smooth *qi* means self-sacrifice to benefit others;
Inverted *qi* means harming others for self-gain.
The *qi* can be regulated by the Dao.

Heaven’s Way is like the echo answering sound—
Virtue accumulated brings blessings;
Calamity accumulated brings resentment.
Learning is undermined by official favor;
Filial piety wanes through wife and children;
Trouble arises from neglecting worries;
Illness worsens when near recovery.
Thus, to attend to the end as to the beginning,
Is to have no failed endeavors.

*The Yinshu* says:
Five harms dwell in the heart,
Manifesting in Heaven’s realm—
Smooth *qi* brings order,
Inverted *qi* brings chaos.
Order brings mutual yielding;
Chaos brings mutual contention.
When *qi* inverts in the heart,
It harms the self.
When *qi* inverts with the times,
It harms the state.
Only by the Dao can *qi* be regulated,
Thus one can sacrifice self to benefit others.
Though officials may flourish, they cannot undermine my learning;
Though wife and children may weaken my filial piety,
What harm from neglected worries?
What illness from near recovery?
To attend to the end with care,
Is to lose nothing.

Laozi says:
To raise the crooked with the straight—
How could this not succeed?
To raise the straight with the crooked—
Do not let them proceed.
This is called "same filth, different mud."
Why not say "raise the crooked and set the straight"?
But to raise the crooked with the straight—
This is to elevate the petty and return them to the noble—
How could this not succeed?
To raise the straight with the crooked—
This is to push the noble into the petty’s domain—
The noble will not go.
Same filth, different mud—
To dwell in the petty’s Way,
Harmonious yet unmoved.

Laozi says:
The Sage treats death and life alike,
The fool also treats death and life alike.
The Sage’s equality of death and life
Lies in understanding the divisions of principle;
The fool’s equality of death and life
Lacks knowledge of where harm and benefit lie.

The Dao is the countenance of Heaven,
Things are the spread of Earth,
Harmony belongs to humanity.
When the ruler is not harmonious,
Heaven’s *qi* does not descend,
Earth’s *qi* does not ascend,
Yin and Yang are unbalanced,
Winds and rains are untimely,
And the people suffer hunger and sickness.
Harmony’s use is supreme.

Heaven gains clarity in all phenomena,
Earth gains life in all things,
Humanity gains success in all endeavors.
How could the ruler’s heart lose harmony?
To lose it means Heaven and Earth do not interact,
And the people suffer hunger and sickness.

Though the Sage and the fool share death and life,
Their difference lies between righteousness and self-interest.
Self-interest is the harmony of righteousness—
The Sage understands principle,
And even the division of benefits is righteousness.
The fool knows not where harm and benefit lie,
And righteousness becomes self-interest.

Laozi says:
To command ten thousand soldiers
Is not as valuable as hearing a single appropriate word;
To possess the Random Marquis’ pearl
Is not as valuable as knowing the origin of things;
To possess the He Shi’s jade
Is not as valuable as knowing the proper course of things.

Though the world be vast,
Those who delight in war perish;
Though a state be secure,
Those who love war are in danger.
Thus, even small states with tenfold weapons
Should not use them.

Yu bowed to Chang Yan:
"Return the troops, and the Miao people will submit."
To hear a single appropriate word
Is to triumph over ten thousand armies.
To possess the Random pearl and He Shi’s jade
Is not as valuable as knowing the origin and proper course of things.

Thus, the Sage values the Dao over treasures,
And virtue over achievements.
Would they willingly endanger themselves with arms?

Laozi says:
Those who can achieve hegemony
Must first excel in virtue;
Those who can defeat enemies
Must first be strong;
Those who can be strong
Must first employ the people’s strength;
Those who can employ the people’s strength
Must first win the people’s hearts;
Those who can win the people’s hearts
Must first attain self-mastery;
Those who attain self-mastery
Must first be gentle and yielding;
Those who can defeat those not their equal
Yet submit to those their equal
And yield to those superior—
Gentle strength emerges beyond their equal,
Its effects are beyond measure.
Thus, only the Sage can
Turn many who cannot win
Into great victory.

Excellence in virtue brings hegemony—
Winning the people’s hearts.
Victory over enemies brings strength—
Employing the people’s strength.
Yet without winning the people’s hearts,
No one can employ their strength.
How then can enemies be defeated?
How can strength achieve hegemony?
Virtue is self-mastery.
Self-mastery makes gentle strength conquer the rigid.
Thus, to defeat those not one’s equal,
Yet submit to those one’s equal—
This is virtue being equal.
Gentle strength emerges beyond one’s equal—
Then virtue surpasses even me.
Thus, only the Sage can
Turn many who cannot win
Into great victory.

*Completing the Continuation of the True Mysterious Classic, Volume Four.*

**Continuation of the True Mysterious Classic, Volume Five**
*Southern Valley Master Du Daojian’s Compilation*
**Chapter on Dao and Virtue**

Wenzi asked about the Dao, and Laozi replied:
*"Learning and inquiry are not refined, listening to the Way is not profound.
All who listen seek to attain wisdom,
To perfect their conduct,
To achieve fame and merit.
Without refinement, there is no clarity;
Without depth, there is no understanding.
Thus, the highest learning listens with the spirit,
The middle with the heart,
The lowest with the ears.
He who listens with the ears learns only on the surface;
He who listens with the heart learns to the marrow;
He who listens with the spirit learns to the bone.
If listening is not profound, knowledge remains unclear;
If knowledge is unclear, one cannot exhaust its essence;
If one cannot exhaust its essence, conduct cannot be fulfilled.

The principle of listening is this:
Empty the heart, clear the mind,
Diminish the qi, let it not overflow,
Hold no thoughts, no deliberations,
Let the eyes see nothing in vain,
Let the ears hear nothing carelessly.
Concentrate with precision, accumulate within—
Let the inner intent gather and merge.
Once attained, guard it firmly,
Hold it steadfastly.

Human nature is the Dao,
Nature’s goodness is virtue—
Dao and virtue reside within us.
Thus, the Sage knows without learning—
This is called the Dao.
To cultivate it is called teaching—
Thus, what is within us is applied to others.

The middling and below can all learn it:
Listening with the ears brings delight to the heart,
Like a mirror polished—
Its light shines forth from within.
Is this not what the heart inherently possesses?
Through study, it is made clear—
To cultivate the self brings virtue to fruition,
To govern the state brings success to undertakings.
This shows how refined learning illuminates the heart.

The Dao has no beginning in origin,
It begins with gentleness and weakness,
Completes with strength and vigor,
Begins with few and short,
Completes with many and long.
A ten-surrounding tree begins as a sapling,
A hundred-foot tower begins with a foundation.
This is the Dao of Heaven.

The Sage follows it:
The lowly humble themselves,
The retiring step back,
The frugal diminish themselves,
The self-effacing reduce themselves—
Thus, lowliness becomes exalted,
Retreat becomes precedence,
Frugality becomes abundance,
Self-effacement becomes greatness.
This is how the Dao of Heaven is fulfilled.

Qi is the formless essence,
Things are the formed *qi*.
The formless is thus gentle and weak,
The formed is thus strong and rigid.
Thus, *qi* is the origin of form.
The Dao generates virtue, nurtures it,
From subtlety to manifestation.
The Sage follows it,
Establishing its essence, enacting its function—
The Dao of Heaven is complete.

The Dao is the root of virtue,
The root of Heaven,
The gate to blessings—
All things depend on it to arise,
Depend on it to flourish,
Depend on it to find peace.
The Dao is non-action, formless,
Within, it cultivates the self;
Without, it governs others—
Achieving success, establishing deeds.
It aligns with Heaven,
Does nothing yet accomplish all—
No one knows its essence,
No one knows its truth,
Yet within it, there is sincerity.

When the Son of Heaven has the Dao,
The world submits, and the altars endure.
When lords and marquises have the Dao,
The people live in harmony, their states unlost.
When scholars and commoners have the Dao,
They preserve their bodies, protect their kin.
The strong with the Dao conquer without war;
The weak with the Dao gain without strife.
Undertakings with the Dao succeed, bringing blessings.
Between ruler and subject, the Dao brings loyalty and benevolence;
Between father and son, the Dao brings filial piety and kindness;
Among scholars and commoners, the Dao brings mutual love.
Thus, with the Dao, there is harmony;
Without it, there is harshness.

From this, it is seen:
The Dao applies to all people.
Virtue roots in the heart,
Spirit manifests outward.
Success achieved, deeds established—
Aligned with Heaven.
From the Son of Heaven to the commoner,
