With the Dao, there is prosperity;
Without it, there is stagnation.

The Dao:
Small practice brings small blessings,
Great practice brings great blessings,
Perfect practice brings the world’s submission—
Submission brings loyalty.
Thus, the Emperor is the world’s *adaptation*,
The King is the world’s *direction*—
Without adaptation or direction, one cannot be called Emperor or King.
Thus, without the people, even the Sage cannot succeed;
Gaining the people yet losing the Dao cannot be sustained.

Those who lose the Dao:
Are extravagant, arrogant, and licentious,
Proud and insolent, boastful and domineering,
Seeking to outshine others, grasping at power—
They become the instigators of strife,
The leaders of rebellion.
When commoners act thus, they bring ruin upon themselves;
When the great act thus, their states perish—
Shallowly affecting themselves,
Deeply affecting their descendants.
Thus, no crime is greater than losing the Dao,
No resentment deeper than lacking virtue.
This is the Dao of Heaven.

The root of the body lies in virtue,
The root of virtue lies in the Dao.
Whether small or great,
Practice it and you attain it—
The world submits to you,
Submission brings loyalty.
The Emperor is *adaptation*,
The King is *direction*—
The world adapts and follows—
This is called Emperor and King.
Yet only with the people can one succeed,
Only with the Dao can one endure—
Not becoming the instigator of war,
Not becoming the leader of rebellion.
Lose the Dao and there is chaos,
Lose virtue and there is resentment.
This is the Dao of Heaven.

Laozi said:
*"Those who practice the Dao make even the bold
So that spears cannot pierce them,
Make even the cunning
So that strikes cannot find them.
Yet even so, they may still be humiliated—
Better to make the bold not dare to strike,
The cunning not dare to attack.
But even this is not as good as
Making them have no such intent at all.
And even this is not as good as
Making them have no desire for gain.
If all men and women in the world
Were to joyfully seek what is beneficial—
Then even without a throne, one would be the sovereign,
Without an office, one would be the leader—
And all would willingly seek what is beneficial for them.*

*The Dao does not deliberately respond to things,
Yet things respond to it.
To be unharmed by spears or strikes
Is not as good as
Making all joyfully desire what is beneficial—
Thus, even without a throne, one is the sovereign,
Without an office, one is the leader—
And all willingly seek what is beneficial for them."*

「In ancient times, there was a man named Gēngsāngzǐ, whose virtue was such.

Wénzǐ asked about virtue, and Lǎozǐ replied:
To **nurture** and **cultivate** [畜之养之],
To **fulfill** and **lead** [遂之长之],
To **comprehend** and **share benefits** without discrimination [兼利无择],
And to **harmonize** with Heaven and Earth [与天地合]—
This is what is called virtue [此之谓德].

What is meant by *rén* [仁]?
For the ruler, it means not **boasting** of one’s achievements [为上不矜其功],
For the subjects, it means not **shaming** their weaknesses [为下不羞其病].
Toward the great, one is not **arrogant** [於大不矜],
Toward the small, one is not **petty** [於小不偷],
To **cherish** and **love** without selfishness [兼爱无私],
And to endure without **waning** [久而不衰]—
This is what is called *rén* [仁].

What is meant by *yì* [义]?
For the ruler, it means **supporting** the weak [为上即辅弱],
For the subjects, it means **upholding** propriety [为下即守节].
When prosperous, one does not **indulge** in willfulness [达不肆意],
When in adversity, one does not **alter** one’s principles [穷不易操].
To **adhere** to a single standard and **conform** to the natural order [一度顺理],
Without **bending** to private gain or **perverting** justice [不私枉挠]—
This is what is called *yì* [义].

What is meant by *lǐ* [礼]?
For the ruler, it means **reverent** and **solemn** conduct [为上即恭严],
For the subjects, it means **humble** and **respectful** bearing [为下即卑敬].
To **retreat** and **yield**, guarding gentleness [退让守柔],
And to **embody** the receptive principle for all under Heaven [为天下雌],
To **stand** where none dare [立於不敢],
And to **establish** what none can [设於不能]—
This is what is called *lǐ* [礼].

Thus, when one cultivates virtue, the subjects **obey** one’s commands [修其德则下从令],
When one cultivates *rén* [仁], the subjects do not **quarrel** [修其仁则下不争],
When one cultivates *yì* [义], the subjects are **fair** and **upright** [修其义即下平正],
When one cultivates *lǐ* [礼], the subjects **hold one in honor** [修其礼则下尊敬].
If these four are all cultivated, the state is **secure** and **tranquil** [国家安宁].

Hence, **Dao/Way** is what gives rise to things [物生者道也],
Virtue is what makes them grow [长者德也],
*Rén* [仁] is what brings love [爱者仁也],
Justice is what brings rectitude [正者义也],
Respect is what brings reverence [敬者礼也].

Without **nurturing** or **cultivating**, one cannot **fulfill** and **lead** to growth [不畜不养，不能遂长].
Without **compassion** and **love**, one cannot **achieve** and **complete** [不慈不爱，不能成遂].
Without **rectitude** and **upholding**, one cannot **endure** and **last** [不正不匡，不能久长].
Without **reverence** and **favor**, one cannot **be held in esteem** [不敬不宠，不能贵重].

Thus, virtue is what the people **value** [德者，民之所贵也],
*Rén* [仁] is what the people **cherish** [仁者，民之所怀也],
*Yì* [义] is what the people **fear** [义者，民之所畏也],
*Lǐ* [礼] is what the people **revere** [礼者，民之所敬也].
These four are the **orderly principles** of culture [此四者，文之顺也],
By which the **sage** governs all things [圣人之所以御万物也].

If a **gentleman** lacks virtue, the subjects **resent** him [无德即下怨],
If he lacks *rén* [仁], the subjects **quarrel** [无仁即下争],
If he lacks *yì* [义], the subjects **act violently** [无义即下暴],
If he lacks *lǐ* [礼], the subjects **become disorderly** [无礼即下乱].
The four **classical teachings** [四经] not established—
This is called **lacking Dao/Way** [谓之无道].
There is none who lacks Dao/Way yet does not **perish** [无道不亡者，未之有也].

Virtue is the **overarching name** of the Five Constants [德者，五常之总名].
A person with virtue possesses the Five Constants in full [有德之人，五常备焉].
*Cí* [慈] is **benevolence** [七则慈],
*Yì* [义] is **appropriateness** [义则宜],
*Lǐ* [礼] is **reverence** [礼则敬],
*Zhì* [智] is **clarity** [知则明],
*Sīn* [信] is **truthfulness** [信则实].
These are called the Five Constants [有之是谓五常],
Or also the Five Virtues [一曰五德].
No **gentleman** has ever lacked virtue yet been able to **serve the state** [君子未有无德而能为国家者矣].

Wénzǐ’s inquiry was for the **benefit** of all under Heaven and future generations [文子之问，为天下后世发也],
His virtue was indeed **vast** [其德博哉].

Lǎozǐ said:
In the age of **supreme virtue** [至德之世],
Merchants found **profit** in the markets [贾便於市],
Farmers took **joy** in the fields [农乐於野],
Great officials **rested content** in their duties [大夫安其职处],
Scholars **refined** their **Dao/Way** [士修其道],
And the people **rejoiced** in their **occupations** [人民乐其业].
Thus, the winds and rains did not **destroy** or **break** [风雨不毁折],
Grasses and trees did not **wither** and **die** prematurely [草木不夭死],
The Yellow River **unveiled** its **Turtle Scripture** [河出图],
And the Luo River **revealed** its **Book of History** [洛出书].
But when the age **declined** [及世之衰也],
Taxes and levies were **without measure** [赋敛无度],
Slayings were **without end** [杀戮无止],
And **punishments** were meted out to the **virtuous** [刑谏者，杀贤士],
Thus the mountains **collapsed** and the rivers **ran dry** [山崩川涸],
Worms and insects **teemed** without rest [蠕动不息],
And the fields **yielded no grain** [野无百蔬].
Hence, when the age is **well-ordered**, even the **foolish** cannot **act alone in chaos** [愚者不得独乱],
And when the age is **disordered**, even the **wise** cannot **restore order alone** [贤者不能独治].

Thus, the **sage** **harmonizes** and **adapts**, **preserving tranquility**—
This is **life** [生也].
To **fulfill one’s aspirations** and **practice the Dao/Way**—
This is **fate** [命也],
So **life** depends on **fate**, and only then can one **act** [生遭命而后能行],
And **fate** is realized when the **time** is right [命得时而后能明].
There must first be its **age**, and only then can there be its **person** [必有其世，而后有其人].
Heaven’s **regard** for humanity is like a father’s **regard** for his children,
Its **love** is **equally distributed** [其爱均也].

The **ruler** is Heaven’s **firstborn son** [天之元子],
And the people are Heaven’s **cherished children** [民，天之赤子].
If the ruler does not **care** for the people [上不恤下],
Heaven will **send warnings** [天必示儆].

In the age of **supreme virtue**, love is **equally distributed** and **harmonizes** with Heaven [爱均合天],
All things **grow** in accordance [万物遂长],
And the people **rejoice** in their **occupations** [民乐其业].
When the age **declines**, harsh governance **disrupts** harmony [苛政干和],
Heaven’s **wrath** **shakes** and **reveals** itself [天怒震发],
The earth **manifests** its **calamities** [地见其灾],
The state **abounds** in **difficulties** [国家多难],
And **plagues** spread among the people [流毒民人].
Only the **sage** **harmonizes** and **adapts**, **preserving tranquility**,
Fulfills his **aspirations**, and **practices the Dao/Way**,
Thus the people and things **grow** in accordance [民物遂生].
This is what is meant by:
There must first be its **age**, and only then can there be its **person** [有其世而后有其人].

Wénzǐ asked about **sagely wisdom** [圣智],
And Lǎozǐ replied:
To **hear** and **know** [闻而知之] is **sagely wisdom** [圣也],
To **see** and **know** [见而知之] is **wisdom** [智也].
Thus, the **sage** always **listens** to where **calamity** and **blessing** arise,
And **chooses** his **Dao/Way** accordingly [而择其道],
While the **wise** always **observe** where **calamity** and **blessing** take form,
And **chooses** their **conduct** accordingly [而择其行].
The **sage** knows the **Dao/Way** of Heaven’s **auspicious** and **inauspicious** signs [故知祸福所生],
Thus he knows where **calamity** and **blessing** arise.
The **wise** **foresee** the **formed** signs,
Thus he knows the **gates** of **calamity** and **blessing** [故知祸福之门].
To **hear** before they arise—this is **sagely wisdom** [闻未生，圣也],
To **foresee** the **formed** signs—this is **wisdom** [先见成形，智也],
Without **hearing** or **seeing**, one is **foolish** and **bewildered** [无闻见者愚迷也].

Why does the **sage** not say *shēngzhī* [生知] but *wénzhī* [闻知]?
For **innate knowledge** is **within oneself** [生知道在我者也],
While **acquired knowledge** is **from external matters** [闻知事在外者也].
The **sage** **knows** before it **happens** [闻於未然],
Thus **calamity** and **blessing** are **foreseen** [祸福先知].
**Wisdom**, however, must **await** the **formation** of events before **seeing** [必待事成而后见],
And the **foolish** are **drowned** in **hearing** and **seeing**,
Yet **never** truly **know themselves** [终不自知],
Their **bewilderment** is indeed **great** [迷亦甚矣].

Lǎozǐ said:
If a **ruler** **loves** to **know** [好知],
He will **trust** the **times** and **rely** on himself [信时而任己],
**Discard** the **calculations** of fate [弃数],
And **employ** **benevolence** [用惠].
Things are **abundant** in **wisdom** but **shallow** in **understanding** [物博智浅],
To **sustain** abundance with shallowness—
This has never **existed** [以浅赡博，未之有也].
To **rely solely** on one’s **wisdom**,
One’s **failures** will be **many** [独任其智，失必多矣].
To **love wisdom** is to **exhaust** one’s **arts** [好智，穷术也],
To **love courage** is to **court** **peril** and **ruin** [好勇，危亡之道也].
To **love giving** is to **lack** a **fixed portion** [好与则无定分],
If the ruler’s **portion** is **not fixed** [上之分不定],
Then the people’s **expectations** will be **without end** [下之望无止].
If he **taxes excessively**, he will **become a target** for the people’s **resentment** [若多敛即与民为条],
Taking little yet giving much—
His **numbers** will **never** be **sufficient** [其数无有],
Thus, to **love giving** is the **path** to **inviting grievances** [故好与，来怨之道也].
From this, it can be seen:
That **wealth** is **insufficient** to **sustain** governance,
And that **Dao/Way** and **arts** can **depend** on **clarity** [财不足任，道术可因明矣].

The **wise** do not **do** what they **cannot** do [智者不为其所不能为],
Nor **force** what they **cannot** force [强所不能].
If one does so, it is **not wisdom** [则非智矣].
Then can wisdom truly be **loved**?
To **love wisdom** is to **exhaust** one’s **arts** [好智则术易穷],
To **love giving** is to **lack** a **fixed portion** [好与则分不定].
Excessive **taxation** **provokes** the people’s **retaliation** [多敛民雠],
And **inviting grievances** is the **means** to **disorder** [来怨之媒也].

Wénzǐ asked:
How did the **ancient kings** govern all under Heaven by **Dao/Way** [古之王者以道莅天下为之奈何]?
Lǎozǐ replied:
By **holding to the One** and **doing nothing** [执一无为],
**Conforming** with Heaven and Earth’s **transformations** [因天地与之变化].
All under Heaven is the **great vessel** [天下大器],
Which **cannot** be **grasped** or **acted upon** [不可执也，不可为也].
To **act** upon it is to **ruin** it [为者败之],
To **grasp** it is to **lose** it [执者失之].
To **hold to the One** is to **see** the **small** [执一者见小也],
To **see** the **small** is to **achieve** the **great** [见小故能成大也].
To **do nothing** is to **guard** the **still** [无为者守静也],
To **guard** the **still** is to **bring rectitude** to all under Heaven [守静故能为天下正].」

To dwell in **great fullness** yet **not overflow**,
To occupy **high nobility** yet **not grow arrogant**.
To dwell in **greatness without excess**—
**Full yet unspent**, **elevated yet secure**—
**Full yet unspent**, so as to **long preserve wealth**;
**Elevated yet secure**, so as to **long preserve nobility**.
Thus **wealth and nobility** never depart from one’s person,
And **blessings extend to descendants**—
This was the **ancient kings’ Dao/Way**.

To govern all under Heaven by **Dao/Way** is to **"hold to the One" and "do nothing"**—
How so?
**The One** is **established**.

Thus the **ancient kings**, conforming with Heaven and Earth’s **transformations**,
**Ruled by doing nothing**,
**Saw the small yet did not abandon it**,
**Guarded stillness yet did not act**—
The **great vessel** stood firm, and all under Heaven was **rectified**.

Therefore, to dwell in **greatness without excess**,
To occupy **high station without arrogance**—
**Wealth and nobility** may be **preserved**,
And **descendants** may endure.

Lǎozǐ said:
The people have **Dao/Way** to follow in common,
And **laws** to uphold in common,
Yet **morality cannot bind them**,
Nor **authority compel them**—
Thus **kings are established** to **unify them**.

When the king **holds to the One**, order is achieved;
When he **abandons constancy**, chaos ensues.
The **Way of the king** is not to **act**,
But to **do nothing**.

The **wise** do not **wield virtue as a tool**,
The **courageous** do not **use force as violence**,
The **benevolent** do not **grant favors by rank**—
This is **the One**.
**The One** is the **Dao/Way of no partiality**,
The **root of all things**.

If the king **frequently alters laws**,
And the state **frequently changes rulers**,
Then people **act according to their positions**,
**Expressing their likes and dislikes**—
The common folk **live in fear**, beyond governance.
Thus, when the king **loses the One**, the **foundation of disorder** is worse than **having no king**.
Only when the king **holds to the One** can he **unify the people**.

Though all under Heaven is vast,
The king **unifies it by the One**.
When the king **holds to the One**,
**Dao/Way** requires no **action**—the people **follow in common**;
**Laws** require no **change**—the people **uphold them in common**.
The **wise** do not **wield virtue as a tool**,
The **courageous** do not **use force as violence**,
The **benevolent** do not **grant favors by rank**—
**Unified**, this is the king’s **Way**.

If the king **frequently alters laws**,
And the state **frequently changes rulers**,
This is worse than **having no king**.

Wénzǐ asked:
How many **principles** does the **Way of kingship** encompass?
Lǎozǐ replied:
**One** alone.

Wénzǐ said:
In antiquity, some ruled by **Dao/Way**, others by **force**—
Why are they not the same?
Lǎozǐ said:
Those who ruled by **Dao/Way** did so by **virtue**;
Those who ruled by **force** also did so by **virtue**.

There are **five kinds of warfare**:
**Righteous war**, **Responsive war**, **Angry war**, **Greedy war**, **Arrogant war**.
To **punish tyranny and aid the weak** is called **righteousness**;
When the enemy attacks and there is **no alternative**, to use force is called **responsive**;
To **quarrel over trifles and fail to control one’s anger** is called **anger**;
To **covet another’s land** or **desire their wealth** is called **greed**;
To **rely on one’s nation’s size** and **boast of one’s people’s multitude**, desiring to **appear superior** to enemy states, is called **arrogance**.

**Righteous war** brings **kingship**,
**Responsive war** brings **victory**,
**Angry war** brings **defeat**,
**Greedy war** brings **ruin**,
**Arrogant war** brings **extinction**—
This is the **Dao/Way of Heaven**.

**Dao/Way** is the **root of governance**;
The king rules by **Dao/Way**.
When the **root is lost**, **chaos arises**,
And **warfare ensues**.

Thus, though some ruled by **Dao/Way** and others by **force**,
Though they seemed different, they **both returned to virtue**.
Therefore, of the **five kinds of warfare**, only **righteous war** can bring **kingship**—
Though it is **war**, it is also **Dao/Way**.
Hence it is said: **One alone**.

**Responsive war** brings **victory**,
**Angry war** brings **defeat**,
**Greedy war** brings **ruin**,
**Arrogant war** brings **extinction**—
These are the affairs of the **Warring States**, not the **warfare of kings**.

Lǎozǐ said:
To **abandon Dao/Way** and **rely on cleverness** is **perilous**,
To **discard principles** and **use talent** is **constraining**.
Thus, to **follow one’s proper role** and **conform to reason**—
When **lost**, one need not **fear**,
When **gained**, one need not **rejoice**—
**Success is not what one strives for**,
**Gain is not what one seeks**.

When **receiving**, one **accepts without taking**,
When **giving**, one **bestows without bestowing**.
To **generate in spring**, to **withdraw in autumn**—
What is **generated** is not **resented**,
What is **withdrawn** is not **held in grudge**—
This is **approaching Dao/Way**.

**Dao/Way** lies in **governance**,
**Principles** relate to **timing**.
To **rely on cleverness** and **abandon Dao/Way**,
To **use talent** and **discard principles**—
These are the **steps to peril and constraint**.

Only by **following one’s proper role** and **conforming to reason**,
When **gained**, one does not **rejoice in achievement**,
When **lost**, one does not **fear retreat**—
When **receiving**, one **accepts without taking**,
When **giving**, one **bestows without bestowing**,
To **act in accordance with the seasons**,
**Generation** is not **resented**,
**Withdrawal** is not **held in grudge**—
Then one **approaches Dao/Way**.

Wénzǐ asked:
How does a king **win the people’s affection**?
Lǎozǐ replied:
Like the **great rivers and seas**—
**Diluted**, **without distinct flavor**,
**Used without depletion**,
**First small, then vast**.

Those who **seek to surpass others** must **lower themselves in speech**,
Those who **seek to lead others** must **place themselves last in action**.
All under Heaven will **emulate their affection**,
**Advance their benevolence and righteousness**,
And **lack harshness**.
To **stand above** yet the people **do not feel burdened**,
To **lead ahead** yet the masses **do not feel harmed**—
All under Heaven **joyfully follow** without **tiring**,
Even in **remote lands and alien customs**,
Even among **insects and creatures**—
None fail to **hold them dear**.
There is **nothing they cannot communicate with**,
And **nothing they cannot accomplish**—
Thus they are **held in highest esteem**.

The **rivers and seas** excel in **humility and capacity**,
Thus all streams **flow to them**.
The **ancient kings** modeled themselves after this to **govern**.

To **contain the people** and **nurture the masses**,
Thus they **won the people’s affection**,
And the people **joyfully submitted**—
All under Heaven **rallied to them**.
Even **grasses, trees, and insects** would **submit in awe**—
Their **nobility** is evident.

Lǎozǐ said:
To **clutch the laws of one’s age**,
And **reject the customs of succession**,
Is like **gluing a pillar to a zither**—
**rigid and out of tune**.

The **sage** adapts to **circumstances**, **varies with the times**,
**Sees the form** and **applies what is fitting**.
When **ages change**, **affairs must change**,
When **times shift**, **customs must adapt**.
To **discuss the age** and **establish laws**,
To **follow the times** and **conduct affairs**.

The **ancient kings** had **different laws**—
Not **intentionally contrary**,
But because **circumstances differed**.
Thus, they did not **follow rigidly the laws already set**,
But **followed the principles behind them**,
And **moved with transformation**.

The **sage’s laws** are **worthy of observation**,
But their **origins cannot be traced**,
Their **words can be heard**,
But their **intentions cannot be fully grasped**.

---

The **Three Sovereigns** and **Five Emperors** held the world lightly,
**Cherished all things** with care,
**Equalized life and death**,
**Unified change and transformation**,
**Embraced the Dao/Way** and **pursued sincerity**,
To **mirror the emotions** of all things,
**Above**, they **made the Dao/Way their friend**,
**Below**, they **transformed with humanity**.

Now, if one wishes to **learn their Dao/Way**,
They cannot grasp their **clear and profound, sacred and august** virtue/power,
But must **guard their legal precedents**,
And **follow their decrees and ordinances**,
Or they will **never be able to govern properly**.

The **single qi** circulates,
The **four seasons** shift in succession.
There are **things that cannot be otherwise**—
Such is the **Dao/Way of Heaven**.

The governance of **emperors** and **kings** differs,
Not because of **human contrivance**,
But due to **Heaven’s unfolding**.

Thus, one cannot **cling to the laws of a single age**
And **expect them to govern successive eras**—
Only by **acting according to the times**,
And **adapting measures to circumstances**,
Can one **discuss the age** and **establish laws**.

Like **winter cannot wear hemp cloth**,
And **summer cannot wear fur garments**,
Those who **know the Dao/Way of Heaven** can do this.

**Wenzi** asked about governance,
And **Laozi** replied:
**"Rule with the Dao/Way**, nurture with **virtue/power**,
**Do not flaunt wisdom**, **do not force strength**,
**Reduce excess** while **holding to unity**,
**Do not show favor**, **do not reveal desires**,
**Be square without cutting corners**, **be sharp without piercing**,
**Neither proud nor boastful**.

**"Rule with the Dao/Way**, and the people will **attach themselves**,
**Nurture with virtue/power**, and the people will **submit**,
**Do not flaunt wisdom**, and the people will **remain content**,
**Do not force strength**, and the people will **retain their simplicity**."

**"Do not flaunt wisdom"** means **to be humble**,
**"Do not force strength"** means **to avoid coercion**.
**Below**, they **gather** with **generosity**,
**Take** with **moderation**,
**Practice frugality** to **preserve themselves**,
And **never dare to be complacent**.

**If they do not gather**, they will **scatter**,
**If they do not nurture**, they will **rebel**,
**If they flaunt wisdom**, the people will **vie for advantage**,
**If they force strength**, the people will **resent their rule**.

**When the people scatter**, the **state’s power weakens**,
**When they rebel**, the **ruler loses authority**,
**When they vie for advantage**, they **disregard right and wrong**,
**When they resent their ruler**, the **throne is in peril**.

If these **four principles** are truly **cultivated**,
The **Dao/Way** is nearly **fulfilled**.

The **ancients governed** with **virtue/power and Dao/Way**,
Later ages governed with **achievement and force**.
**With virtue/power and Dao/Way**, the people **submit** and **customs become pure**,
**With achievement and force**, the people **resent** and **wars arise**.

Thus, **"rule with the Dao/Way**, nurture with **virtue/power**,
**Do not flaunt wisdom**, **do not force strength**,
And the world will be **governed without action**."

**Laozi** said:
**"When the ruler speaks, the people act**—
**When the people speak, the ruler acts**—
**When the ruler speaks, it is constant usage**,
**When the people act, it is expedient adaptation**.
Only the **sage** can **know expedient adaptation**,
**Speak and always be believed**,
**Set expectations and always meet them**.

The **highest conduct** in the world:
**To be straight and honor one’s father**,
**To be trustworthy and die for one’s mother**—
Who can **value this**?

Thus, the **sage** **judges right and wrong in affairs**,
**Accommodates with bending and stretching**,
**Has no fixed rituals or appearances**,
**When prosperous, they are called a ‘named lord’**,
**When drowning, they are struck like a father**—
**Power compels this**.

**Expedient adaptation** is what the **sage** **alone perceives**,
**To first oppose and then unite** is called **expedient adaptation**,
**To first unite and then meet** is **not to know expedient adaptation**,
**Not to know expedient adaptation** is **to turn good into evil**.

**"When the ruler speaks and the people act"** is the **substance of expedient adaptation**,
**"When the people act and the ruler responds"** is the **application of expedient adaptation**.
**Substance and application** of **expedient adaptation** **complement each other**,
**No matter the affair**, **all will be fitting**.

Only the **sage** **knows expedient adaptation**,
**Speaks and is always believed**,
**Sets expectations and always meets them**,
**Accommodates with bending and stretching**,
And **judges their opposition and unity**.

If one **clings to the middle without expedient adaptation**,
They **cannot adapt to changing times**.
**Gluing a pillar to a zither**—
**Goodness turns to folly**.

**Wenzi** asked:
"Master, your words say
**That without virtue/power and Dao/Way, one cannot govern the world**,
Yet **the rulers of ancient times**,
**Who inherited their positions and relied on former achievements**,
Also had **those without the Dao/Way** who **preserved their reigns**,
And **no calamity or ruin**—
How was this **Dao/Way** accomplished?"

**Laozi** replied:
"From the **Son of Heaven** down to the **common people**,
Each **lives their own life**,
Yet their **living has degrees of depth**—
The world **at times sees states and families destroyed**,
Not because of **lack of virtue/power and Dao/Way**.

**With virtue/power and Dao/Way**, they **diligently strive day and night**,
**Tremble and fear constantly**,
Ever **mindful of impending danger**.
**Without virtue/power and Dao/Way**, they **indulge desires and grow lax**,
Their **ruin comes without warning**.

If **Jie and Zhou** had **practiced virtue/power and Dao/Way**,
Even **Tang and Wu**, though **wise and virtuous**,
Could **not have established their achievements**.

**Virtue/power and Dao/Way** are **how one nurtures and sustains**,
**How one fosters mutual affection**,
**How one upholds respect and honor**.

Even **deaf insects**, though **foolish**,
**Do not harm what they love**—
If **all people in the world** **harbored hearts of benevolence and righteousness**,
How could **calamities and disasters arise**?

Those **without virtue/power and Dao/Way** yet **without ruin or harm**,
Their **benevolence has not yet perished**, their **righteousness has not yet died**.

Though **benevolence has not yet perished**,
And **righteousness has not yet died**,
The **feudal lords already disregard their ruler**—
When **feudal lords disregard their ruler**,
The **court becomes disrespectful**,
And **even if they do not rebel**, they **do not obey**.

When **benevolence perishes** and **righteousness dies**,
The **feudal lords rebel**,
The **common people seize governance**,
The **strong oppress the weak**,
The **great invade the small**,
And **the people make warfare their profession**—
Disasters **arise**, calamities **begin**,
Their **ruin is imminent**—
How can they **hope to avoid calamity**?

The **ancients who governed the world**,
**Virtue/power and Dao/Way declined** while **benevolence and righteousness took precedence**,
When **benevolence and righteousness declined**, **calamities and wars arose**.

In the **earliest ages**, **successive rulers** like **Jie and Zhou**,
Though their **states were not yet ruined**,
The **feudal lords already held disrespectful thoughts**,
Then **virtue/power and Dao/Way were lost**,
And **benevolence and righteousness declined**.

Thus, **Tang and Wu arose** and **cut off the Xia and Shang**,
And **the Kings You and Li of Zhou** were like **Jie and Zhou**—
Only **a slight difference in talent**,
So they **could not escape** the **great slaughter of war**,
Or the **death at Mount Li**—
The **feudal lords seized governance**,
The **strong oppressed the weak**,
The **great invaded the small**,
The **Six States followed the balance**,
And **Zhou perished with them**—
Is this not **a warning**?

**Laozi** said:
**"When laws are excessive and punishments severe**,
The people **will deceive**,
**When the ruler acts excessively**, the people **will act deceitfully**,
**When demands are many**, **rewards will be few**,
**When prohibitions are many**, **success will be little**,
**Using affairs to create more affairs**,
And **again using affairs to stop affairs**—
Like **stoking a fire and hoping it will not burn**;

**Using wisdom to create troubles**,
And **again using wisdom to guard against them**—
Like **stirring water and hoping it will remain clear**."
**Laws are originally for seeking**,
But **excessive governance brings chaos**;

「Punishments are originally for restraint,
Yet harsh severity will breed deceit.

If the ruler acts excessively and yet expects the people to be free of affairs,
This is like forcing a man to drink and then blaming him for being drunk—
It is to blind the people.

How can this be called governance?

**Laozi** said:
When the sovereign favors **virtue/ren**,
Then the unworthy are rewarded and the guilty are pardoned;
When he favors punishments,
Then the worthy are ruined and the innocent are condemned.

To neither favor nor hate,
He executes punishment without resentment and bestows rewards without expecting gratitude—
He lets the measure flow and the line guide itself,
Uninvolved in affairs, like Heaven and Earth—
How could he not cover and load all under his rule?

To unify and harmonize,
This is the **Dao/Way** of the sovereign;
To separate and execute,
This is the **Dao/Way** of the law.

When the people receive punishment without grievance,
This is called **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way**.

To favor **virtue/ren** yet not know how to govern,
To reward the unworthy and pardon the guilty—
This can still harm governance.

To favor punishments yet not know how to apply the law,
To ruin the worthy and execute the innocent—
The harm is profound indeed.

Only when justice is absolute and impartial,
Aligning with **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way**,
Rewards are not squandered and punishments are not cruel,
Then all officials fulfill their duties and the people embrace their labors,
And the realm thrives in perfect balance.

**Laozi** said:
The world’s right and wrong have no fixed standard,
Each age upholds what it likes and rejects what it dislikes.
Those who seek what is right
Do not seek **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way**,
But what aligns with themselves.
To reject what is wrong
Is not to reject evil,
But to reject what opposes the heart.

Now, if I wish to choose what is right and dwell in it,
To choose what is wrong and discard it,
I do not know what the world calls right and wrong.

Hence, governing a great state is like poaching small fish—
Do not stir them, lest they spoil.

To pursue what aligns with others,
If words are true yet actions grow distant,
Or if the body is near yet plans are made from afar—
This will surely invite suspicion.

Now, if I wish to correct myself and await the responses of others,
How can I know what the world will use to measure me?

If I rush along with the customs,
I am like one fleeing the rain—
There is no place untouched by dampness.

To desire emptiness yet fail to embody it,
Or to not seek emptiness yet naturally attain it—
This is what one desires and will surely achieve.

Thus, those who grasp the **Dao/Way**,
Like a cart axle that does not move itself,
Yet with the wheel reaches a thousand miles,
Turning through endless plains—

Hence, the sage embodies the **Dao/Way** and returns to its origin,
Does not act to transform but waits for transformation,
Moves yet does nothing—

The distinctions of names and ranks, the debates of right and wrong,
The separation of good and evil—
These are the **Dao/Way** of governance,
Not seeking absolute justice but taking one’s own perspective.

To call wrong right and evil good,
Yet hope for names and ranks to be correct and laws to be clear—
This is difficult indeed.

Only when one corrects oneself and awaits the responses of others,
Without abandoning absolute justice,
Then, like a cart on land or a boat on water,
One travels without obstruction—
How could there be any falling into imbalance?

**Laozi** said:
He who often wages war and frequently wins,
His state shall perish.
Often waging war exhausts the people,
Frequent victories breed arrogance in the ruler—
With an arrogant ruler leading exhausted people,
How could his state not perish?

When the ruler is arrogant, he grows reckless,
Recklessness leads to exhausting the people,
When the people are exhausted, they harbor resentment,
Resentment leads to extreme measures—
If both ruler and people reach extremes yet the state does not perish,
This has never occurred.

Thus, when achievements are complete, the ruler retires—
This is the **Dao/Way** of Heaven.
Victories are not constant; defeat follows—
This is the **Dao/Way** of reversals.
How can one seek frequent victories in war?

When the ruler prevails yet grows arrogant,
And the people are exhausted yet still wage war—
The axe of ruin for his state.

War is like fire—
If not restrained, it will burn itself out.
Is this not a warning?

King Ping asked Wenzi:
I have heard that you learned the **Dao/Way** from Lao Dan.
Even if the wise have the **Dao/Way**,
Yet encounter an age of licentious chaos,
With the authority of one man,
How could they transform the people who have long been in disorder?

Wenzi replied:
**Virtue/power** and **Dao/Way**,
Rectify evil to establish what is right,
Stabilize chaos to bring order,
Transform licentiousness and decay into simplicity—
Pure **virtue/power** revives,
And the world is at peace.
This depends on one person.

The sovereign is the teacher of the people,
The ruler is the model for the subjects.
If the ruler is beautiful, the people will emulate beauty,
If the ruler has **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way**,
Then the people will have **virtue/ren** and **righteousness**,
And if the people have **virtue/ren** and **righteousness**,
Then there will be no age of licentious chaos.

Accumulating **virtue/power** leads to kingship,
Accumulating resentment leads to ruin,
Piling stones forms mountains,
Gathering water forms seas—
Without accumulation, how could anything be achieved?

Those who accumulate **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way**,
Heaven aids them, Earth assists them,
Ghosts and spirits support them,
Phoenix birds hover in their courts,
Qilin roam their outskirts,
Qiantong dragons dwell in their marshes.

Thus, to rule the world with the **Dao/Way**,
Is to embody the world’s **virtue/power**;
To rule the world without the **Dao/Way**,
Is to become the world’s thief.

To make the world your enemy with one man’s power,
Even if you desire longevity, it cannot be attained.

Emperor Yao and Shun flourished through this,
King Jie and Zhou’s last tyrant perished through this.

King Ping said:
I respectfully hear your words.

Wenzi, from the family of Ji, lived near Bo,
Long studied under Laozi,
And upon hearing the **Dao/Way**, became widely learned.
King Ping invited him and asked about the **Dao/Way**,
Wenzi answered with *accumulating virtue leads to kingship, accumulating resentment leads to ruin*.
Emperor Yao and Shun flourished through this,
King Jie and Zhou’s last tyrant perished through this.

King Ping said:
I respectfully hear your words.

By this standard,
If King Ping had resolve to govern,
Why did he not cultivate **virtue/power** and pardon resentment,
Yet instead he trusted slander and harbored suspicion,
And executed the Wu clan?
This is why Wenzi left Chu for Yue.

Zi Chu urged Wu to attack Chu,
And suffered the humiliation of being flogged after death—
Great indeed.

Rulers of states,
Can resentment not be pardoned?

*(End of the fifth chapter of *The True Classic of the Mysterious Origin*, annotated with the following notes from the *Jizhen Edition*:
#1 After this sentence: *"Thus, the brave who dare are slain, the brave who do not dare are spared."*
#2 *"志得"* (Zhìdé) is *"至德"* (Zhìdé) in the *Jizhen Edition*.
#3 *"执一者见小也"* (Zhíyī zhě jiàn xiǎo yě) is *"执者见小也"* (Zhí zhě jiàn xiǎo yě) in the *Jizhen Edition*.)*

**Chapter Six of *The True Classic of the Mysterious Origin* – *The Upper Virtue Section*
Compiled by Master Du Daojian of Nangu Valley**

**Laozi says:**
The ruler is the heart of the state.
When the heart is ordered, all limbs are at ease;
When the heart is disturbed, all limbs are in chaos.

Thus, when one’s own person is ordered,
The limbs support one another;
When a state is ordered,
The ruler and ministers forget their distinctions.

**Virtue is singular yet twofold:**
Nurturing all things is the **virtue/power** of Heaven,
Cherishing the people is the **virtue/power** of the ruler.

The ruler is the heart of the state—
When the ruler has **virtue/power**, the heart expands, the body grows robust,
**Qi** remains unruffled, and the person governs itself.
To govern a state is like governing one’s own person—
When ruler and ministers are at ease,
How could the state fail to be ordered?

Thus it is said:
*The Upper Virtue does not cling to virtue,*
Yet this is virtue—
Not boasting of it as virtue,
Yet truly possessing it.

**Laozi says:**
He learned from Changcong,
Observing the tongue yet guarding softness,
Gazing upward at the house’s rafters,
Retreating to gaze upon the river,
Studying his shadow to know how to follow behind—
Thus the sage abides in emptiness and follows,
Ever yielding yet never leading,
Like kindling piled—
The fire that burns last stands highest.

Changcong was a sage of antiquity.
Laozi learned from Changcong,
As Confucius learned from Laozi.
When the **Dao/Way** is seen, the spirit resonates in harmony—
Thus he could observe the tongue and guard softness.
Studying his shadow to know how to follow behind,
The sage thus follows in emptiness and non-action,
Not contending, not daring to lead—
This is called *Upper Virtue*.

**Laozi says:**
A clanging bell destroys itself with sound,
A candle consumes itself with light.
The tiger’s stripes invite arrows,
The monkey’s agility invites confrontation—
Thus the bold die by their strength,
The eloquent are ensnared by their cleverness,
Knowing through cleverness yet failing to know *not* knowing through cleverness.

Thus, those skilled in one ability,
Sharp in one argument,
Can be led by devious speech,
Yet cannot be broadly responded to.
What is noble is to have **virtue/power**,
Not to have much cleverness.
Excessive cleverness issues from oneself
And returns upon itself—
Few escape self-harm.
The tiger’s stripes invite arrows,
The monkey’s agility invites confrontation—
The scholar’s boldness dies by strength,
His cleverness is ensnared by eloquence—
This is as it should be.

Only those who do not know through cleverness
Yet know *not* knowing through cleverness
Are not confined to one thing,
And their responses are broad.

**Laozi says:**
The **Dao/Way** is formless in its essence—
It cannot be seen, cannot be heard—
Called *profound and obscure*.
*Profound and obscure* is how the **Dao/Way** is spoken of,
Yet it is not the **Dao/Way** itself.

The **Dao/Way** is inward reflection—
Thus one does not slightly awaken,
Nor greatly delude,
Nor slightly enlighten,
Nor greatly dull.
One does not discern in flowing waters,
But discerns in still pools—
For its essence is inwardly preserved, still, and not externally agitated.

The moon wanes when the sun is full—
Yin cannot sustain yang.
When the sun rises, stars vanish—
They cannot contend for brightness.
The end cannot overpower the root,
The branch cannot exceed the trunk.
What is above is heavy, what is below is light—
Its overturning is inevitable.

One pool does not hold two dragons,
One female does not mate with two males—
One is fixed; two contend.
Jade in the mountains makes grasses and trees lush,
Pearls in the depths keep the shores from withering.
The earthworm, lacking strong bones or sharp claws,
Eats the withered above and drinks from the Yellow Springs below—
Its **qi** is singular.

The **Dao/Way** is formless in its essence—
Thus it can move in parallel.
Things, with form as their function,
Cannot coexist.
Formless in essence, it is singular and fixed.
With form as function, they are dual and contend.

Yet there are degrees of great and small,
Distinctions of ruler and minister.
When the ruler embraces the **Dao/Way** and holds **virtue/power**,
He does not claim possession of it—
This is why the **Dao/Way** is vast,
And **virtue/power** endures.

Who dares contend with strength?
Clarity as brightness—
A cup of water reveals the pupil.
Turbidity as harm—
The Yellow River obscures Mount Tai.
Orchids and osmanthus need no praise to be fragrant;
A boat floats on rivers and seas—
It does not sink for want of riders.
The gentleman walks the **Dao/Way**—
He does not halt for want of recognition.
This is the nature of things.

To enter purity from impurity is to invite constraint and humiliation;
To enter impurity from purity is to invite ruin.
Heaven’s two **qi** form the rainbow,
Earth’s two **qi** cause leakage and concealment,
Man’s two **qi** bring illness.
Yin and yang cannot persist—
Now winter, now summer;
The moon does not know day,
The sun does not know night.

Wide rivers breed great fish,
Tall mountains grow towering trees,
Broad lands nurture deep **virtue/power**.

Thus fish cannot be caught without bait,
Beasts cannot be summoned without traps.
Mountains harbor fierce beasts—
The forests are left uncut;
Gardens harbor venomous insects—
The melons and hemp are left unharvested;
States have worthy ministers—
They resolve conflicts a thousand *li* away.

Those who master the Dao move like a wheel turning in its hub—
not driven by themselves, yet carrying all to a thousand *li*,
ending only to begin anew, revolving in the boundless source.

Thus, to raise the crooked alongside the straight—
what difference does it make?
To raise the straight alongside the crooked—
do not comply.

Clear water mirrors shadows,
a clear mind discerns all things—
this is the nature of illumination.
When muddied, it grows dim.

The virtuous and the petty cannot coexist—
their forces cannot stand together.
Winter’s cold and summer’s heat do not run concurrently—
if they do, calamities arise.
If forces clash, misfortune follows.

Yet though the petty may hold office,
should the virtuous withdraw because the Dao cannot be practiced?
There is a bird about to arrive—
set the net and await it.
He who catches the bird is the one who set the net’s eye.

If you set a net for one eye,
you will never catch a bird.
Thus some matters cannot be preordained,
some things cannot be foreseen.
Hence the Sage nurtures the Dao and waits for the time.

To catch fish, first clear the valley;
to summon birds, first grow the trees.
Water gathers, fish assemble;
trees flourish, birds congregate.
Those who catch fish do not drag them from the depths,
those who catch monkeys do not carry them up the trees—
they simply let them come where they are drawn.

The path you tread is shallow,
yet you must wait for what you do not tread to move forward.
The mind’s knowledge is limited,
yet you must wait for what it does not know to become clear.

When rivers dry, valleys empty;
when hills level, abysses fill.
When lips perish, teeth grow cold.
When the Yellow River’s waters recede,
the land lies bare upon the mountains.

Still water becomes clear,
clear water becomes level,
level water reveals all forms—
forms cannot coexist,
thus they can be measured.

To know the use of what is useful,
yet not know the use of what is useless—
such a one cannot speak of governance.

To govern a state depends on one person;
without one, many cannot rule.
To seek worthy ministers without first nurturing scholars—
is this possible?

Thus the Sage nurtures the Dao and waits for the time.
When worthy men arise, none refuse to serve.
What makes leaves fall? The wind shakes them.
What makes water murky? Things disturb it.
A jade’s smoothness comes from the whetstone’s work,
a blade’s sharpness comes from the grindstone’s force.
The gnat and the steed both travel a thousand *li*—
yet the gnat does not fly,
the steed lacks provisions and does not starve.

When the clever hare is caught, the hunting dogs are boiled;
when the high birds are exhausted, the fine bows are hidden.
Success brings retreat—
this is the Way of Heaven.

Anger arises from what is not anger,
action comes from what is not action.
To see what is unseen,
to hear what is unheard—
then you perceive what is seen,
then you hear what is heard.

Birds return to their nests,
hares flee to their burrows,
foxes die on their birth mounds,
cicadas cling to trees—
each returns to its origin.

The Dao can be walked alone,
but affairs cannot be monopolized.

A man may run a hundred *li* and then tire,
but with a good horse, a thousand *li* is possible.
Anger from what is not anger—
thus Wen and Wu brought peace to the people.
Action from what is not action—
thus Yao and Shun established the age of settled governance.

If a ruler governs well and uses men wisely,
who among the people would not return to him?

Fire and water repel each other,
yet the cauldron mediates between them.
The five flavors blend—
bone and flesh love each other,
yet slanderers intervene,
and father and son become endangered.

Dogs and pigs eat without discrimination,
growing ever fatter—
yet they approach death.

The phoenix soars to the heights, yet none can summon it;
the axe’s blade is fixed to its handle, yet it cannot strike itself.
The eye sees a hundred paces away,
yet cannot see its own corner.

Build high and it becomes a mountain—
stable and not precarious.
Build low and it becomes a marsh—
fish and turtles gather there.

Ditches overflow in floods,
dry up in droughts.
The Yellow River’s source is deep and never exhausted.

The deaf lack ears, yet their eyes cannot be covered—
they are precise in sight.
The blind lack eyes, yet their ears cannot be covered—
they are precise in hearing.

Muddy water can wash my feet,
but clear water can wash my headgear.
Silk begins as raw silk—
sometimes it becomes a cap,
sometimes a belt.
The cap is worn on the head,
the belt is wrapped around the feet.

No man is always right,
no thing is always wrong.
Qi in harmony unites,
qi in conflict separates.

Fire ascends, water descends—
the cauldron mediates between them,
and its function is fulfilled.

If the father is kind and the son dutiful,
yet slanderers intervene,
then the grudges of the Big Dipper and the Southern Cross arise.

Dogs and pigs grow fat and approach death,
the phoenix soars high and none can summon it.

Clear water washes the headgear,
muddy water washes the feet—
if one cannot purify oneself,
yet blames others for filth,
should this not be examined?

Gold’s nature overcomes wood—
one blade cannot harm an entire forest.
Earth’s nature overcomes water—
one handful cannot dam a river.
Water’s nature overcomes fire—
one sip cannot extinguish a cartload of firewood.

Winter thunder, summer hail—
cold and heat do not alter their course.
Frost and snow fall thickly,
yet melt at dawn.

To tilt is to risk falling,
to lean is to risk toppling,
to assist is to risk overreaching,
to moisten is to risk rain.

Orchids and osmanthus bloom fragrant,
yet cannot endure frost.
Toads avoid weapons,
their lifespan ends at the fifth month’s full moon.

When essence is wasted, the body weakens,
when flowers bloom out of season, they cannot be eaten.

Generation and destruction, restraint and domination—
each has its fixed portion.
To bear great burdens with little strength
is to invite reversal and subjugation.

Wood overcomes metal,
water overcomes earth,
fire overcomes water—
yet this is not their inherent nature.

Winter thunder, summer hail—
these are not their proper times.
Even a morning’s duration is brief—
how can one not measure oneself?

The tongue against the teeth—
which wears first?
The bowstring against the arrow—
which snaps first?

What bends the shadow is the form,
what muddies the echo is the sound.

To treat the dying as the sick—
this is difficult for a good physician.
To counsel the doomed as the living—
this is impossible for loyal advisors.

The one who makes the flute play and the artisan refine the pipes—
though they follow the proper rhythm, they cannot make the music resound.
The ruler’s form is lost when they are constrained.

The deaf cannot sing to find joy,
the blind cannot observe to engage with others.
One who walks in the forest cannot follow a straight path,
and one who travels in peril cannot step on a plumb line.

The sea produces great things,
yet when placed in an unsuitable land or dependent on the wrong people,
it is difficult indeed.

The rigid tooth breaks first,
the stiff arrow snaps first.

To treat the dying as the sick—
this is difficult for a good physician.
To counsel the doomed as the living—
this is impossible for loyal advisors.

If the ruler does not follow the Dao,
yet the minister forces it upon him,
with schemes born of divided hearts—
how can success be achieved?

The sun does not rise in pairs,
the fox does not have two kings,
the divine dragon does not mate,
the fierce beast does not gather in herds,
the ravenous crow does not fly in twos.

Without the handle, the parasol cannot shield,
without the axle, the wheel cannot chase swiftness—
yet even a well-made parasol and wheel are not enough to rely upon.

To draw the bow and shoot—
without the string, the arrow cannot fly,
and the act of releasing the arrow is but one-tenth of the skill of shooting.

A hungry horse in the stable makes no sound,
but when fodder is placed beside it,
the struggle for it begins.

A three-inch pipe cannot hold the wind,
yet the heavens cannot be filled.
A ten-stone vessel may overflow,
but a hundred dou is sufficient.

If one cuts along the plumb line, it will not stray,
if one weighs with the balance, it will not err.
To apply ancient laws by analogy—
sometimes they succeed, sometimes they fail.
The use of standards and measures—
sometimes they are applied, sometimes not.
To act rightly is called discernment;
to act wrongly is called chaos.

The sun does not rise in pairs,
the divine dragon does not mate,
the fierce beast does not gather in herds—
how much more so for humans?

The handle stretches the canopy,
the axle turns the wheel—
this is the Dao of ruler and minister aiding each other.

A hungry horse struggles for fodder;
rewards must not be given recklessly.
To cut along the plumb line is to judge without error;
ancient laws and standards must be applied at the proper time—
it depends on those who wield them.

The farmer labors while the noble is nourished;
the foolish speak while the wise choose.

When things are clear before one,
treat them as jade and stone—
when they are obscure,
keep their counsel.
A hundred stars’ light
is not as bright as one moon’s,
and ten windows open
are not as bright as one door.

The viper cannot be a foot,
the tiger cannot be a wing.

Now, if one has a six-foot mat,
lying down, one can easily step over it,
but standing, even the most skilled cannot leap over it—
the application of force differs.

The assistant at sacrifice gets to taste,
the one who breaks up a fight gets injured,
and he who shelters beneath an unlucky tree
may be struck by thunder and lightning.

The sun and moon wish to shine,
yet floating clouds obscure them;
the river wishes to be clear,
yet sand and mud defile it;
the clump of orchids wishes to flourish,
yet autumn winds wither them;
human nature wishes to be balanced,
yet desires corrupt it.
To walk through dust and yet avoid being stained—
this is impossible.

A golden tortoise pendant,
the worthy wear as an ornament;
fertile soil spread across the land,
the capable use to grow rich.

Thus, to give gold and jade to the weak
is not as good as giving them a simple scroll.

If the farmer does not labor,
the noble has no means to be nourished.
If the noble does not govern,
how can the farmer be at peace?

The serpent has no feet,
the tiger has no wings—
one cannot have both.

Floating clouds obscure the sun—
the fault lies not with oneself.
Desires corrupt nature—
the illness is not of the person.

A hollow hub stands with thirty spokes,
each pulling straight toward its notch,
yet if one spoke alone were to enter,
all others would be abandoned—
how could it reach near or far?

Citrons grow in their native soil,
reeds grow in their clumps,
beasts with similar feet gather to roam,
birds with similar wings gather to soar.

To observe all nine regions of the land
without a thousand-li journey,
without the source of governance and teaching,
and yet wish to rule the people—
this is difficult.

The cunning gain their prey,
the swift strike their mark.

Thus, great whiteness seems like shame,
vast virtue seems like insufficiency.

The noble has wine,
the petty man beats his pot—
though it may not be praised,
it can still be despised.

The Supreme Polarity stands at the center,
relying on the eight ministers before it can be established.
The hub of the wheel is hollow within,
depending on the spokes before it can move.

The Son of Heaven stands at the center of the world,
placed above all people,
yet without the source of governance and teaching,
how can he rule?
Only by maintaining proper position and dignity,
with officials fulfilling their roles,
can the realm be governed without descending from the hall.

Human nature prefers loose clothing and hanging silks,
yet when threatened, one immediately dons armor—
this is to abandon what is inconvenient
for what is advantageous.

Thirty spokes share one hub,
each straight toward its notch,
yet none can enter another—
just as ministers each guard their duties.

The skilled user of people is like the insect’s feet,
many yet not harming one another,
like the tongue and teeth,
firm and soft, grinding yet not destroying.

Stone is born firm,
orchid is born fragrant—
if held young, their virtue grows clearer with time.

To support and to lift,
to decline and to yield,
to gain and to lose,
to assent and to refuse—
these differ by thousands of miles.

There is a method to every action,
and nothing fails to succeed.
There is a way to use people,
and none are beyond aid.

The spokes of the wheel each align with their notch,
illustrating how officials must each hold their posts.

Though the insect’s feet are many, they do not harm one another—
this is because they are used appropriately.

Stone is firm, orchid is fragrant—
if each is used according to its nature,
then the wise become clear,
the strong become powerful,
and success is achieved.

The twice-born do not gain,
flowers that bloom too early fall without frost.

To soil the standard,
to powder the brow,
to have a rotting rat beneath the ancestral altar,
to be choked by smoke in the hall—
to enter water and hate being wet,
to embrace stench and seek fragrance—
even the most skilled cannot craft perfection.

Winter ice can be shattered,
summer wood can be bound—
opportune moments are hard to find yet easy to lose.

When wood is in full vigor,
one can cut it all day and it regrows,
but when autumn frost descends,
it withers in a single night.

When the target is taut and the arrow is drawn,
when forests are dense and axes enter—
this is not summoned by will,
but by the nature of things.

A nursing puppy bites a tiger,
a crouching hen strikes a weasel—
when favor is bestowed,
it does not measure their strength.

Those who wait for profit to rescue the drowning will surely drown them with their own greed.
A boat can float or sink—fools do not know where to draw the line.

A swift steed urged forward will not advance,
pulled back will not stop—
a ruler who does not seek the Dao in governance
will find no steady path.

Opportune moments are hard to grasp yet easy to lose,
the people easily fall into hardship but rarely recover.

Thus, things that regenerate cannot be fully seized,
flowers that bloom too early fall without frost—
the times are not favorable.

When the target is taut and the arrow is drawn,
when forests are dense and axes enter—
this is the work of circumstance.

A nursing puppy bites a tiger,
a crouching hen strikes a weasel—
driven by instinct, not strength.

A rotting boat still carries its load,
a weary steed driven to death—
can the people endure further suffering?

Water, though level, must have waves,
a balance, though true, must have slight deviation,
a ruler’s measure, though precise, must have flaws.

Without the compass and square, one cannot define the square and round,
without the plumb line and level, one cannot rectify the crooked and straight.
Yet even those who use the compass and square
must have the mind to wield them rightly.

Tai Mountain’s height, doubled, is still unseen,
yet the finest autumn hair can be discerned—
so too with hidden truths.

Fire in bamboo does not ignite without kindling,
water in the earth does not emerge without digging.
An arrow’s speed does not exceed two *li*,
yet a limping tortoise, step by step, crosses a thousand.

Heap soil without end, and hills will rise.
Stand by the river wanting fish—
better to return and weave nets.

A bow must first be tuned before seeking strength,
a horse must first be broken before seeking excellence,
a man must first earn trust before seeking skill.

A master smith cannot melt wood,
a skilled artisan cannot cleave ice—
some things defy effort.
How can the virtuous not take heed?

If men cannot cross the river,
you cannot force the river to stand still.
If a pot does not say it is guiltless,
it will not fall into the well.

Water, though level, must have waves,
a balance, though true, must have deviation—
how much more must the heart be cautious?

The gentleman’s rites and propriety,
like the craftsman’s compass and square,
are tools to measure conduct.

A bow must first be tuned before seeking strength,
a horse must first be broken before seeking excellence.
If a man is not first trusted,
how can his skill be summoned?

Thus the gentleman acts only as he can,
and does not act where he cannot prevail.

Those who reproach my conduct seek my company,
those who praise my goods seek my trade—
a single chess move does not reveal wisdom,
a single plucked string does not stir sorrow.

Now take a burning ember—
touch it, and your fingers blister,
yet stand ten paces away, and you are unharmed.
Like qi gathered differently, some bear glory,
others bear sorrow.

Silk above, hemp below—
the high and low must align.

Great trees have deep roots,
tall mountains have firm foundations.
Bitter words bring profit,
bitter medicine cures disease.

Those who reproach my conduct seek my company—
the gentleman follows righteousness.
Those who praise my goods seek my trade—
the petty man follows profit.

Man’s fortune cannot be excessive—
clothing need not be extravagant.
Great trees have deep roots,
tall mountains have firm foundations—
when the people prosper, the state flourishes.

The *Laozi* says:
A drum does not store sound, yet it makes sound.
A mirror does not hold form, yet it reflects form.
Metal and stone make sound only when struck,
flutes and pipes make music only when blown.

Thus the sage holds inward, does not lead others,
responds when things come, answers when things arrive.
Heaven’s motion never ceases,
yet it returns to its beginning—
thus it endures.

A wheel turns where it should,
thus it travels far.
Heaven’s motion never strays—
thus there is no excess.

Heaven’s qi descends, earth’s qi ascends,
yin and yang intermingle—
all things are harmonized.
When the gentleman acts, the petty man fades—
this is the Way of Heaven and Earth.

When Heaven’s qi does not descend,
earth’s qi does not ascend,
yin and yang do not flow—
all things wither.
The petty man gains power,
the gentleman fades,
the five grains do not grow,
virtue is hidden within.

The Way of Heaven: it gathers the abundant and aids the scarce.
The Way of Earth: it reduces the high and raises the low.
The Way of Spirits: it humbles the proud and exalts the lowly.
The Way of Man: the many do not share.
The Way of the Sage: though low, none can surpass him.

The heart that does not store things can respond to things,
the drum that does not store sound can answer sound—
if it rings without being struck, it is unnatural.

Thus the sage holds inward, does not lead others,
but responds as needed.

When Heaven and Earth intermingle,
all things are harmonized—
this is the time of bright gatherings,
when the gentleman acts and thrives.

If one reverses this,
then it is the petty man’s day.

Only the sage stands high while listening low,
yet does not grow full of himself,
and does not seek to rise above.

When Heaven is bright, the sun shines,
and only then can it illuminate the four quarters.
When the ruler is enlightened, the ministers are enlightened,
and only then is the realm at peace.
When the realm has four enlightened corners,
only then can it endure.

To enlighten those who enlighten others—
this is to enlighten their transformations.

The Way of Heaven is *wen* (pattern),
the Way of Earth is *li* (principle),
one unites them in harmony,
time governs their work—
thus all things are completed.
This is called the Dao.

The Great Dao is broad and near—
cultivate it in oneself, and virtue becomes true.
Cultivate it in things, and virtue is unbroken.

Heaven covers all things,
bestowing virtue and nourishing them,
giving without taking—
thus all things return to their spirit.

To give without taking is the highest virtue—
thus it is called virtue.

Nothing is higher than Heaven,
nothing is lower than the marsh.
Heaven is high, the marsh is low—
the sage follows this,
and order is established.

Earth bears all things and sustains them,
giving and taking—
thus bones and flesh return to it.

To give and take is the lower virtue—
yet even the lower virtue, if not lost,
is still virtue.

Earth supports Heaven, thus it is stable.
Earth is stable, thus all things take form.
Earth is vast and thick, thus all things gather.
Stable and thick, it bears all things,
vast and deep, it contains all things.

Earth’s foundation is deep and firm,
thus waters gather within.
Earth’s Way is square and broad,
thus it endures.
The sage follows this—
virtue contains all things.

The Sovereign follows the Way of Heaven,
the Minister follows the Way of Earth.
Heaven is exalted, Earth is humble—
thus the distinction between Sovereign and Minister is fixed.

When the Sovereign is enlightened, the Minister is enlightened,
illuminating the four quarters—
is this not the radiance of Yao,
who brought clarity to all under Heaven?

Thus, to model Heaven and emulate Earth,
to complete all things—
this is the Way.

To nourish and give without taking is the highest virtue.
To give and take is lower virtue indeed.

Yin is difficult for Yang—
when all things flourish, Yang subdues Yin.
Yang serves Yin—
all things rest in tranquility.

When things flourish, none lack sustenance;
when things rest, none lack joy.
When all things rejoice, all things are well-ordered.

If Yin harms things, Yang bends in retreat;
when Yin advances and Yang withdraws,
the petty gain influence,
the virtuous avoid harm—
this is the Way of Heaven.

When Yang-qi moves, all things relax and find their place—
thus the sage follows the Way of Yang.

To follow things, things will follow in return;
to oppose things, things will oppose in return—
thus one does not lose the nature of things.

When the marshes overflow, all things grow in due measure;
when the marshes dry, all things wither in due measure.
Thus, when rains fail, the land is barren and lost,
Yang ascends then descends again—
thus it becomes the master of all things.

It does not cling to permanence,
and so it can end and begin anew,
end and begin anew,
and so endure forever.
To endure forever,
it becomes the Mother of all under Heaven.

Yang-qi accumulates before it can bestow,
Yin-qi gathers before it can transform—
nothing transforms without first accumulating.
Thus the sage is cautious in what it gathers.

When Yang extinguishes Yin, all things grow fat;
when Yin extinguishes Yang, all things wither.
Thus, when kings and lords uphold the Way of Yang,
all things flourish;
when they uphold the Way of Yin,
all under Heaven perishes.

Yang does not descend to Yin—
all things cannot form,
the Sovereign does not descend to the Minister—
virtuous transformation does not spread.
Thus, when the Sovereign descends to the Minister,
there is wisdom and clarity;
when not, there is darkness and deafness.

Yang gives life, Yin brings death—
thus the virtuous cherish life,
the petty embrace death.

When kings and lords uphold the Way of Yang,
all things flourish;
when they uphold the Way of Yin,
all under Heaven perishes.

Yang is noble, descending to Yin—
thus when the Sovereign descends to the Minister,
there is wisdom and the nation is well-ordered;
when not, there is darkness and deafness,
and virtuous transformation does not spread.

When the sun rises from Earth,
all things multiply and thrive,
kings and lords dwell above the people,
illuminating virtue and morality.
When the sun sets into Earth,
all things rest and hide,
the petty dwell above the people,
and all things flee in concealment.

When thunder moves, all things awaken;
when rain nourishes, all things are released—
the great act similarly.

The movement of Yin and Yang has fixed seasons,
the great act without exhausting things.

Thunder shakes the Earth, all things relax;
wind sways the trees, grasses and woods wither.
The great act by abandoning vice and embracing goodness—
the people do not flee far,
thus the people have choices to depart or stay.
Departure is more extreme,
approach is less so.

When wind does not move, fire does not rise,
when the great do not speak, the petty have no words.
When fire rises, it must await kindling;
when the great speak, it must be with truth.

With truth and sincerity, what cannot succeed?
Deep waters hold the earth,
mounds on mountains, valleys in the lowlands—
when Yang-qi is strong, it transforms into Yin;
when Yin-qi is strong, it transforms into Yang.
Thus desire cannot be fully satisfied,
joy cannot be fully indulged.

Anger without harsh words,
wrath without color—
this is called calculated gain.

Fire ascends in flame, water flows downward—
the sage’s Way seeks its kind:
the sage embraces Yang, bringing harmony to all under Heaven;
the sage embraces Yin, drowning all under Heaven.

Is Heaven’s sun the people’s Sovereign?
When the sun rises from Earth,
kings and lords dwell above the people,
all things multiply and thrive.
When the sun sets into Earth,
the petty dwell above the people,
all things flee in concealment.

The movement of Yin and Yang has fixed seasons,
thus the year does not disrupt its times.
The great act without exhausting things,
thus the people do not flee far.

Things pushed to extremes transform—
desire cannot be fully satisfied.

Yin and Yang are Heaven’s—
order and chaos depend on humanity.

Laozi said:
Thin accumulations become thick,
low accumulations become high.
The virtuous strive daily to achieve brilliance,
the petty rush daily to reach disgrace.

Though unseen in its waxing and waning,
thus one pursues goodness as if it might slip away,
and past evils as if they were omens of ruin.
If one turns toward goodness,
even past mistakes bring no blame;
if one does not turn toward goodness,
even loyalty cannot avert calamity.
Thus blaming others is not as good as blaming oneself;
seeking faults in others is not as good as seeking them within.

Sounds are self-summoned,
kinds seek their own,
names are self-claimed,
positions are self-assumed—
none of it is without cause.

Wielding sharpness to pierce,
wielding blades to strike—
why blame others?

Thus the virtuous are cautious of trifles.

All things bear Yin and embrace Yang,
converging qi to form harmony.
Harmony resides at the center—
thus grains grow in the heart of wood,
seeds in the pods of grass,
eggs and embryos in the center,
those without eggs or embryos
take time to gestate.

When Earth is level, water does not flow;
when light and heavy are balanced, the scale does not tilt—
the generation and transformation of things
are moved by response.

Yin and Yang respond to form harmony.
Thus all things are born,
Sovereign and Minister unite in the Middle Way—
thus all the people are at peace.

Therefore the virtuous harmonize their heart-mind,
striving daily to achieve brilliance.
The petty drown in Heaven’s harmony,
rushing daily to reach disgrace.

Thus the virtuous are cautious of trifles,
rectifying their hearts and seeking within themselves.
If I do not blame others,
others will not blame me—
is this not so?

Laozi said:
Mountains reach their height,
and clouds and rains arise there.
Waters reach their depth,
and dragons and serpents are born.
The virtuous perfect their Way,
and virtue flows forth.

Those with hidden virtue
will surely have manifest reward,
those with secret deeds
will surely have renowned name.
Planting millet does not yield barley,
planting resentment yields no gratitude.
To do good unseen is the highest good.
To do evil unseen is the highest evil.

Mountains conceal clouds to form rain and dew,
marshes conceal waters to birth dragons and serpents—
the virtuous harbor their Way,
and its blessings flow to the world.

Those with hidden virtue will surely have manifest reward—this is the blessing of Heaven.
Those with secret deeds will surely have renowned name—this is the esteem of humanity.
Only those with Heaven’s virtue know it.

---

**Chapter Seven of the *Tongxuan Zhenjing* (Continuation of the *True Scripture of the Perfected Dao*), Compiled by Master Du Daojian of Nangu, *Weiming* ("Mystic Clarity")**

Laozi said:
The Dao can be weak, yet strong,
soft, yet firm,
shadowed, yet bright,
enfolding yet expansive,
responding without fixed measure.
Shallow knowledge grasps its surface,
deep knowledge penetrates its core.
Coarse knowledge clings to its form,
refined knowledge perceives its essence.
Yet what is known as knowledge is ignorance,
and what is unknown as ignorance is true knowledge.
Who can fathom the formless form?

Thus all under Heaven know that goodness is goodness—
yet this is not true goodness.
The wise do not speak,
speakers lack wisdom.
The mystically bright—
this is the Dao.

See not with the eyes,
hear not with the ears,
but grasp it from Heaven and root it in the heart—
thus one can enfold Heaven and Earth,
respond without fixed measure,
not by cleverness or forceful seeking.
Only by knowing what is not known,
acting without contrivance,
speaking without words
does one attain it.

Wenzi asked:
Can one speak of the Dao in subtle ways?
Laozi replied:
Why not? Only to say what must be said.
Those who say what must be said
do not speak with words.
The fisherman wades into the water,
the hunter chases the beast—
not for their own delight.
Thus the ultimate speech transcends speech,
the ultimate action transcends action.
Shallow thinkers contend for trifles.

Speech has its root,
affairs have their master.
Only the ignorant
remain unrecognized by me.
The Dao cannot be spoken—
yet when spoken, it manifests things.
Words are not the Dao.
Yet without words, it remains unclear.

When Wenzi asked if one could speak of the Dao in subtle ways,
and Laozi answered that only knowing what must be said is possible,
this is speech without speaking,
and speaking without speech.
The fisherman and hunter—
these are those who do not know what must be said,
clinging to words and fixated on things,
thus their contests are for trifles.
How can one find those who transcend speech and action
to converse with them?

Wenzi asked:
Does governing a state also have its principles?
Laozi replied:
Now, those who pull a cart,
call out *"Left!"* in front,
and the rear responds in kind—
this is the song of urging the oxen forward.
Though the melodies of Zheng, Wei, Hu, and Chu differ,
none match this cart-puller’s true intent.
Governing a state relies on ritual propriety,
not on eloquent debate.
The more laws multiply, the more thieves and robbers arise.
Those who rule a state are like Heaven and Earth:
Heaven does not speak, yet the four seasons proceed;
Earth does not utter, yet all things flourish.

When Wenzi asked of the principles of governance,
Laozi answered with the cart-puller’s song—
front calls, rear echoes—
just as the Sage aligns with the natural order before Heaven,
and follows the seasons after Heaven.
Governing relies on ritual propriety,
not on the superficial brilliance of words.
To ignore the fundamental order
while multiplying laws
is to provide thieves with legal pretexts
and harm the people.

Laozi said:
The Dao has no fixed form yet can be the fixed form.
Like mountains and forests yielding timber,
yet timber does not equal mountains and forests;
mountains and forests do not equal clouds and rains,
clouds and rains do not equal yin and yang,
yin and yang do not equal harmony,
harmony does not equal the Dao.
The Dao is the state of form without form,
the image without image—
that which cannot be fully expressed,
yet between Heaven and Earth
can be shaped and transformed.
The Dao has no fixed form—
things conform and take shape accordingly.
To observe mountains and forests transforming
into the harmony of clouds, rains, yin, and yang—
then one knows that all formless, imageless things
between Heaven and Earth
can be shaped and transformed.
This is the Dao’s divine mystery.

Laozi said:
The Sage establishes teachings and administers governance,
ever mindful of their beginning and end,
and how they shape destiny.
Thus when the people know writing, virtue declines;
when they know calculation, benevolence declines;
when they know contracts, trust declines;
when they know mechanical arts, sincerity declines.
The zither does not sound,
yet its twenty-five strings each resonate with their own note.
The axle does not turn of itself,
yet its thirty spokes each exert their strength to revolve.
Strings must have slack and tight
to produce melody,
wheels must have labor and rest
to travel far.
To make the resonant silent,
to make the turning still—
this is the way.
When governing principles diverge above and below,
easy governance becomes chaos.
High station with great Dao brings fortune,
great affairs with small Dao bring misfortune.
Small virtue harms righteousness,
small goodness harms the Dao,
petty debate harms governance,
harsh scrutiny harms virtue.
The greatest rectitude is not precarious,
thus the people are easily guided.
The most refined governance is effortless,
thus the subjects do not rebel.
The utmost loyalty returns to simplicity,
thus the people harbor no deceit.

The Sage establishes teachings and administers governance,
not to seize for oneself—
but to observe their beginning and end,
and act where no action is needed.
To know writing, calculation, contracts, and mechanical arts
is to have one’s mind fixed on affairs,
thus virtue, benevolence, trust, and sincerity decline.

The ruler relies on ministers to govern,
just as the zither needs strings to sound,
and wheels need spokes to travel far.
To grasp the essential and employ the great—
then there will be no ungoverned world.

Laozi said:
When the principle of *"relative seating"* is established,
the common people grumble.
When the decree of *"reducing titles"* is proclaimed,
meritorious ministers rebel.
Thus those who focus on legal records
do not grasp the roots of order and chaos;
those who train in military formations
do not understand the strategic balance of temple and battlefield.

The Sage perceives blessings within the fortified pass,
and dangers beyond the unseen depths.

The foolish are bewildered by petty gains,
yet forget great harms.
Thus, affairs that bring profit in small matters
but harm in great ones,
are obtained here
while forgotten there.

Hence, there is no greater benevolence than loving others,
no greater wisdom than knowing others.
Loving others means no resentment or punishment,
knowing others means no chaotic governance.

Governance cannot be achieved through excessive affairs,
nor can law be effective with frequent changes.
Excessive affairs and frequent changes
will cause the common people to grumble,
meritorious ministers to rebel,
and the ruler will have means to summon them.

The roots of order and chaos,
the strategic balance of temple and battlefield—
the Sage discerns these in the unseen beyond form,
thus possessing foresight’s clarity.

Laozi said:
Though the rivers are vast,
their overflow is never beyond three days;
nor does a violent wind and rain
last longer than a moment in the sun.
Yet those who lack accumulated virtue
need not fear ruin,
for it will not reach them.

Worry is the source of prosperity,
joy is the source of ruin.
Thus, the virtuous turn weakness into strength,
transforming misfortune into blessing,
employing the Dao though it seems void,
yet never full of themselves.

When a state’s harsh governance erupts,
it is like the rivers overflowing,
or winds and rains raging violently—
they do not last long,
and ruin will soon follow.
Only those who lack virtue in their worry
yet know how to adapt changes
can still turn misfortune into blessing,
strength from weakness,
and understand the Dao’s unfathomable use.

Laozi said:
Serenity and tranquility
are the nature of humankind.
Ceremonial propriety and measured conduct
are the rules of affairs.
Knowing the nature of others
means one’s cultivation remains unerring,
knowing the rules of affairs
means one’s actions and movements are orderly.

A single decree issues,
yet no one contends with it—
this is called *the heart*.
Perceiving the root to know its branches,
grasping one principle to respond to all—
this is called *skill*.
To dwell in knowledge’s source,
act in accordance with its path,
understand how to ride upon affairs,
and know when to halt—
this is called *the Dao*.

If people praise and exalt me,
it is the power of my heart;
if they belittle and slander me,
it is the fault of my heart.

Words emerge from the mouth,
yet cannot be restrained by others;
actions originate from proximity,
yet cannot be restrained by distance.

Affairs are hard to complete yet easy to ruin,
reputation is hard to establish yet easy to discard.
All people tend to slight small harms,
neglect minor matters,
until great calamities arrive.

For calamity comes from within,
blessing is self-made.
Misfortune and fortune share the same threshold,
profit and harm are neighboring forces.
Without utmost precision,
none can distinguish them.
Thus, wisdom and foresight
are the gatekeepers of fortune and misfortune,
the pivotal mechanisms of action and stillness—
they must not be neglected lightly.
