Heaven’s principle and human desires
align in one heart,
while noble and petty folk
diverge in one self—
are they not the same origin yet different names?

To grasp one principle and respond to all is called *skill*,
to see movement and know stillness is called *the Dao*.
Words issue from the mouth,
actions spring from the heart—
fortune and misfortune, profit and harm,
come like reflections in water.
Without utmost precision,
who can separate them?
Can one not examine oneself
and reflect within the heart?

Laozi said:
All know the triggers of order and chaos,
yet none grasp the means of preserving life.
Thus, the Sage assesses the world to act accordingly,
and contemplates affairs to devise strategy.

The Sage can be yielding or firm,
soft or hard,
weak or strong—
adapting to the times in movement and stillness,
establishing merit through resources,
foreseeing the reverse of things,
observing one matter to discern its changes—
forming patterns as they transform,
responding as they unfold—
thus their entire lives are unconstrained.

Affairs may be those that can be spoken of yet not done,
or those that can be done yet not spoken of,
or those that are easy to initiate yet hard to complete,
or those that are hard to complete yet easy to ruin.

Those that can be done yet not spoken of—
are selection and rejection;
those that can be spoken of yet not done—
are deceit and trickery.
Those easy to initiate yet hard to complete—
are affairs themselves,
those hard to complete yet easy to ruin—
are reputations.

These four matters
are the Sage’s careful considerations,
the insights of the enlightened.

There are times of order and chaos,
while governance rests with humankind.
Knowing the triggers of order and chaos,
yet failing to grasp the means of preserving life—
the fault lies with humankind, not the times.

The Sage adapts to the times in movement and stillness,
observing their changes,
thus their entire lives are unconstrained.

When the times are right yet governance is rigid,
how can one avoid blame
while failing to grasp the means of preserving life?

Laozi said:
The Dao respects the subtle and small,
acts without losing the moment—
a hundred precautions against calamity,
yet misfortune does not grow.

Calculating blessings yet not foreseeing them,
worrying about misfortune yet overestimating it—
like plants struck by frost in the same day,
the sheltered ones are unharmed.
The foolish may prepare,
yet their efforts match those of the wise.

Accumulated love brings blessing,
accumulated resentment brings misfortune.
All know how to avert calamity,
yet none know how to prevent it from arising.

Preventing calamity’s birth is easy,
yet saving from calamity is difficult.
Now people do not strive to prevent calamity’s birth,
but instead focus on saving from its aftermath—
even the divine cannot devise such strategies.

The origins of calamity and misfortune
are countless and boundless.
The Sage retreats deeply to avoid harm,
remains silent to await the right moment;
the petty do not know the gates of fortune and misfortune,
yet act and fall into punishment—
though they twist their preparations,
they cannot fully preserve themselves.

Thus, the superior cultivator
avoids misfortune first, then seeks profit,
shuns disgrace first, then pursues reputation.

Hence, the Sage always attends to the unseen beyond form,
while neglecting the already completed within sight—
thus calamity has no path to arrive,
nor can praise soil them with dust.

The Dao respects the subtle and small,
acts without losing the moment—
this is the essence of caution at the outset.
The foolish may prepare,
yet their efforts match those of the wise—
for people’s calamities
cannot be repelled before they occur,
nor can salvation be sought after they have arrived.

Thus, the superior cultivator
prioritizes avoiding misfortune and disgrace,
while placing reputation and profit aside,
never demanding them.

The Master Laozi says:

The common person’s Dao:
desires a small **heart**,
yet a great **will**,
wishes for all-encompassing **wisdom**,
yet upright **conduct**,
seeks many **abilities**,
yet few **undertakings**.

The so-called small **heart**—
before misfortune arises,
they guard against calamity and tread with caution,
not daring to indulge their desires.

The great **will**—
embracing all under heaven,
unifying diverse customs,
where right and wrong converge,
and they stand at the center.

The all-encompassing **wisdom**—
endless yet boundless,
flowing in four directions,
deep yet unfathomable.

The upright **conduct**—
standing straight yet unyielding,
pure white yet unstained,
in adversity never altering their principles,
in prosperity never boasting.

The many **abilities**—
possessing both literary and martial arts,
balanced in action and stillness,
their movements and gestures fittingly adapted,
bending yet never breaking.

The few **undertakings**—
grasping the essential to govern the multitude,
holding the strict to manage the broad,
remaining tranquil to steady the restless.

Thus, the small-**hearted** guard against the slightest danger,
the great-**willed** embrace all things,
the all-**wise** know all things,
the upright-**conducted** have nothing they refuse,
the many-**able** can govern all things,
the few-**tasked** hold fast to what they possess.

Hence, the Sage’s approach to **virtue**—
they do not slight the small and fail to act,
nor overlook the slightest fault without correction.
Their actions require no **diviners** or **shamans**,
yet ghosts and spirits dare not act first—
this may truly be called the most precious **virtue**.

Yet they tremble and quake,
ever vigilant day by day,
thus attaining the sincerity of **non-action** and unity.

The foolish person’s **wisdom** is already limited,
and what they undertake is excessive—
thus their actions always fail.
If they govern with **rightness**, their efforts are easy yet certain to succeed;
if they govern with **deviance**, their efforts are difficult yet certain to fail.
Abandoning what is easy yet certain to succeed,
and persisting in what is difficult yet certain to fail—
this is the folly of the confused.

The **will** must be great while the **heart** must be small,
**wisdom** all-encompassing yet **conduct** upright,
**abilities** many yet **undertakings** few.
These six principles—
the common person must strive to embody them.

A great **will** can contain all things,
a small **heart** guards against the slightest danger,
all-encompassing **wisdom** makes all undertakings possible,
upright **conduct** stands firm yet unyielding,
many **abilities** ensure nothing remains unaccomplished,
few **undertakings** bring strictness and steadfastness.

The Sage’s actions do not require **diviners** or **shamans**,
yet ghosts and spirits dare not act first—
it is merely through sincerity that they attain unity.

The Master Laozi says:
**Ren** is what people admire,
**Yi** is what people revere.
To be admired and revered,
yet meet with personal death and national ruin—
this is because they did not adapt to the times.

Thus, those who know **ren** and **yi** yet fail to grasp worldly **power**,
have not attained the Dao.

The Five Emperors valued **virtue/power**,
the Three Kings relied on **Yi**,
the Five Hegemons depended on **force**.
Now, applying the Dao of the Emperors and Kings to the age of the Five Hegemons—
this is not their proper Dao.

Thus, **goodness** and **evil** are alike,
praise and blame depend on custom,
actions are equal,
yet **harmony** and **disorder** lie in the times.
Knowing the **Heavenly** Way’s workings,
and understanding human actions—
then one can govern the world.

To know **Heaven** yet not know people,
is to lack the means to interact with customs.
To know people yet not know **Heaven**,
is to lack the means to align with the Dao.

If one’s **will** is straight yet their emotions are suited,
they will be assailed by **firmness**,
and forced to serve others.
If one’s **will** is suited yet their emotions are straight,
they will be consumed by **yin-yang** forces.
The Dao is not practiced—
I know why.

The Five Emperors valued **virtue/power**,
the Three Kings relied on **Yi**,
the Five Hegemons depended on **force**.

In the age of the Five Hegemons,
yet applying the Dao of the Emperors and Kings—
this is because they did not adapt to the times.

To fail in adapting to the times,
is to fail in knowing **Heaven** and people.
How then can they govern the world?

Those who attain the Dao—
externally transform yet internally remain unchanged,
externally transform to know people,
internally remain unchanged to preserve themselves.
Thus, within they maintain a steadfast unity,
while externally they bend and adapt,
moving with the times,
yet never falling into error.
What is precious in the Dao,
is its **dragon-like** transformation.

To uphold one principle,
and act in one way—
even if they achieve fullness,
they never alter.
Clinging to minor virtues,
yet obstructing the great Dao.

The Dao is **stillness** and **emptiness**,
not forcing things externally,
nor imposing on oneself internally.
Thus, undertaking actions in accordance with the Dao—
is not the work of those who do not know the Dao,
but the application of the Dao itself.

The **dragon** and **tiger’s** transformations—
this is the Dao of the great person.

Those who attain the Dao
adapt to all things yet never fall into error—
thus Laozi was called the **"Dragon"** of the Dao.

To embody the Dao’s **emptiness**,
externally not imposing on things,
internally not imposing on oneself—
the Dao is applied to all,
and the world is transformed.

The heavens and earth cover and bear all things,
the sun and moon illuminate and shine upon them,
the yin and yang nurture them,
the rain and dew moisturize them,
the **Dao/Way** and **virtue/power** sustain them—
all share in the same **harmony/and**.

Thus, those who wear the Great Round
walk the Great Square,
those who gaze upon the Great Clarity
see the Great Brightness,
those who stand upon the Great Balance
dwell in the Great Hall,
and those who wander in the **mysterious/void** (*míngmíng*)
share the same light as the sun and moon—
shapeless yet giving rise to the shaped,
so the **true person** (*zhēnrén*) entrusts his time to the **spiritual platform** (*língtái*)
and returns to the **origin of things** (*wù zhī chū*).

To see in the **mysterious/void** (*míngmíng*),
to hear in the **silent** (*wúshēng*)—
within the **mysterious/void**,
only he has **awareness** (*xiǎo*);
within **stillness and emptiness** (*jìmò*),
only he has **illumination** (*zhào*).

His **application** (*yòng*) is to **not apply** (*wúyòng*),
only then can he truly **apply** (*yòng*).
His **knowledge** (*zhī*) is to **not know** (*wúzhī*),
only then can he truly **know** (*zhī*).

**Harmony/And** is the **universal Dao/Way** of the world.
The heavens’ covering, the earth’s bearing—
all align with the same **harmony/and**.

The **sage-king** (*huángdì*) wears the Round and walks the Square,
dwells in the Great Hall and stands upon the Great Balance—
can his **harmony/and** be lost?

The **true person** (*zhēnrén*) returns to the **origin of things** (*wù zhī chū*),
surely he must have **unique insight** (*dújiàn*) within the **mysterious/void** (*míngmíng*).

Thus, **application** is to **not apply**,
only then can he **apply**.
**Knowledge** is to **not know**,
only then can he **know**.
This is called **Great Harmony**.

The **Dao/Way** is what things **follow** (*dào*),
**Virtue/power** is what life **sustains** (*fú*),
**Ren** is the **manifestation of accumulated kindness** (*jī'èn*),
and **Yi** is the **principle of alignment with the heart and fitting for the multitude** (*bǐyú xīn ér héyú zhòngshì*).

When the **Dao/Way** fades, **virtue/power** flourishes;
when **virtue/power** wanes, **Ren** and **Yi** arise.
Thus, in the **Upper Age** (*shàngshì*), the **Dao/Way** is upheld but **virtue/power** is not yet emphasized,
in the **Middle Age**, **virtue/power** is guarded but **Ren** and **Yi** are not yet embraced,
and in the **Lower Age**, there is **fear of losing Ren and Yi** (*jīngjīng ér kǒng shī rén yì*), so **Ren** and **Yi** are clung to.
Therefore, the **noble person** (*jūnzǐ*) cannot live without **Yi**—
to lose **Yi** is to lose the very means of his existence;
the **petty person** (*xiǎorén*) cannot survive without **profit** (*lì*)—
to lose **profit** is to lose the very means of his survival.
Thus, the **noble person** fears losing **Yi**,
the **petty person** fears losing **profit**—
observing their fears,
the paths to **disaster** and **blessing** diverge.

When **Dao/Way** and **virtue/power**, **Ren** and **Yi** fracture,
the **sage-kings** and **kings** (*huángdì wángbó*) are divided—
this is the work of the **changing times** (*shìbiàn*).

The **Dao/Way** guides them,
**virtue/power** nourishes them,
**Ren** bestows kindness upon them,
and **Yi** makes them **proper/right** (*yí*).
Though the four ages differ in governance,
their **noble rulers** and **ministers** maintain their **proper distinctions** (*yǒu fēn zé yī*).

Those who uphold the **Dao/Way** but not **virtue/power** are **sage-kings** (*huáng*),
those who guard **virtue/power** but do not embrace **Ren** are **kings** (*dì*),
and those who fear losing **Ren** and **Yi** are **lords** (*wáng*).
When **Yi** loses its **proper alignment** (*qǐ yí shī qí yí*),
**crafty deceit** (*zhìzhà*) thrives.

Laozi said:
*"Some seek profit and thereby harm themselves,
some seek harm and thereby profit themselves.*
To **force** (*qiáng*) the **ill** (*bìng*) with **heat** (*wēn*) when they crave **warmth** (*rè*),
to **force** the **thirsty** (*kě*) with **cold** (*hán*) when they crave **coolness** (*lěng*)—
this is how the **common folk** (*zhòngrén*) **nurture** (*yǎng*) themselves,
yet the **skilled physician** (*liángyī*) knows this as **the cause of illness** (*wéi bìng*).

**Delight** (*kuài*) in the **eyes**,
**pleasure** (*yuè*) in the **heart**—
these are the **fool’s** (*yú*) **advantages** (*lì*),
yet the **person of the Dao/Way** (*yǒudào zhě*) **avoids** (*bì*) them.

The **sage** (*shèngrén*) first **encounters** (*féng*) and then **unites** (*hé*) with the multitude,
while the **common folk** first **unite** and then **encounter**—
thus, the **gates of disaster and blessing** (*huòfú zhī mén*)
and the **contraries of profit and harm** (*lìhài zhī fǎn*)
cannot be overlooked.

There are matters where seeking **profit** brings **harm**,
and seeking **harm** brings **profit**—
not even **human effort** (*rénlì*) can **guarantee** (*bì*) them,
for **things** may **cause** (*shǐ*) them.

Thus, those who **first oppose** (*xiān yǔ*) and then **unite** (*hé*)
follow the **sage’s Dao/Way** (*shèngrén zhī dào*),
while those who **first unite** and then **oppose** (*yǔ*)
follow the **common folk’s Dao/Way** (*zhòngrén zhī dào*).
**Disaster** and **blessing**, **profit** and **harm**,
lie in wait—
their **unpredictability** (*bùkě bù chá*) is thus.

Laozi said:
*"Those with **meritorious deeds** (*yǒng*) yet **divergent from Ren and Yi** will be **suspected** (*yí*),
those with **guilt** (*zuì*) yet **aligned with Ren and Yi** will be **trusted** (*xìn*).*
Thus, **Ren** and **Yi** are the **constant proper alignment** (*chángshùn*) of affairs,
the **most honored rank** (*zūnjué*) in the world.

Though **plans** (*móu*) and **calculations** (*jì*) may **seem correct** (*dāng*),
and **worries** (*yōu*) and **concerns** (*huàn*) may **be solved** (*jiě tú*),
if the **state** (*guó*) is preserved,
yet one’s **affairs** (*shì*) **diverge from Ren and Yi**,
his **merits** (*gōng*) will **not be fulfilled** (*bù suì*).

Though **words** (*yán*) may **not align** (*wúzhòng*) with **strategy** (*cè*),
and **calculations** may **not benefit** (*wúyì*) the **state**,
if one’s **heart** (*xīn*) is **loyal** (*zhōu*) to the **lord** (*jūn*),
and **affairs** are **aligned with Ren and Yi** (*héyú rén yì*),
one’s **life** (*shēn*) will **surely be preserved** (*bì cún*).

Therefore it is said:
*"A hundred words, a hundred calculations, often **not fitting** (*bùdāng*),
are **not as good as** (*bùruò*) **abandoning haste** (*shě qū*) and **carefully examining Ren and Yi** (*shěn zhū rén yì*).*

**Ren** and **Yi** are the **grandchildren** (*sūn*) of the **Dao/Way**,
the **children** (*zǐ*) of **virtue/power**—
yet the four **never fail to interrelate** (*fù ruò bù xiāngjí*),
and **never truly separate** (*xiānglí*).

Thus, **Ren** and **Yi** are the **most honored rank** in the world—
for those who **value their own bodies** as the **world** (*wéi tiānxià zhě*),
can they **not abandon haste** and **examine carefully** these **Ren** and **Yi**?

Laozi said:
*"The **noble person’s** (*jūnzǐ*) **teaching** (*jiào*) originates from **himself**,
the **petty person** receives its **benefits** (*zé*).
**Profit** (*lì*) originates from the **petty person**,
the **noble person** enjoys its **merits** (*gōng*).

To let the **noble person** and the **petty person** each find their **proper place** (*qí yí*),
is to **harmonize merits** and **transform nourishment** (*tōng gōng yì shí*)
and thus **achieve the Dao’s fulfillment** (*dào dá*).

Many **desire** (*yù*) and thereby **injure** (*shāng*) **Yi**,
many **worry** (*yōu*) and thereby **harm** (*hài*) **wisdom** (*zhì*),
so **governing** the **state** with **what brings joy** (*lè*) ensures its **preservation** (*cún*),
while **tyrannizing** the **state** with **what brings joy** ensures its **ruin** (*wáng*).

Water flows downward yet is **vast** (*guǎngdà*),
the **lord** is **benevolent** (*cí*) yet **wise** (*míng*).
If the **lord** does not **contend** (*zhēng*) with his **ministers** (*chén*),
the **governing Dao/Way** will **be fulfilled** (*tōng*).
Thus, the **lord** is the **root and foundation** (*gēnběn*),
the **ministers** are the **branches and leaves** (*zhīyè*).
If the **root and foundation** are **not beautiful** (*bù měi*),
yet the **branches and leaves** flourish—
this has never occurred.

The **noble person** and the **petty person**—
both are **people** (*rén*).
If the **noble person’s** **teaching** (*jiào*) is **not received** (*bèi*) by the **petty person**,
how can his **virtue/power** (*dé*) **provide sustenance** (*zī*) for the **petty person’s** **nourishment** (*yǎng*)?

**Governing** the **state** with **what brings joy** ensures its **preservation**,
**tyrannizing** the **state** with **what brings joy** ensures its **ruin**.
If the **lord** is **good at yielding** (*shàn xià*) yet **does not contend** (*bù zhēng*),
then his **ministers** will **offer their loyalty** (*xiàn qí zhōng*).

**Stability** (*zhì*) secures the **root** (*gēn*), **depth** (*shēn*) strengthens the **foundation** (*běn*),
and thus the **state** is **safe** (*ān*).

Laozi said:
*"The **loving father’s** (*cífù*) **care** (*ài*) for his **son** (*zǐ*)
is **not** to **seek** (*qiú*) his **reward** (*bào*),
for **this cannot truly be resolved** (*bùkě nèi jiě*) in the **heart** (*xīn*).
The **sage-ruler’s** (*shèngzhǔ*) **nourishment** (*yǎng*) of his **people** (*mín*)
is **not** for his **own use** (*wéi jǐ yòng*).
**Nature** (*xìng*) **cannot** be **restrained** (*kě jǐ*).
When he **relies** (*shì*) on their **strength** (*lì*)
and **depends** (*lài*) on their **merits** (*xūn*),
yet **must exhaust** (*qióng*) them to **achieve** (*wéi*) **anything** (*yǒu wéi*),
then his **kindness** (*ēn*) will **not reach** (*bù jiē*).

Thus, to **raise** (*yǔ*) what the **people** **love** (*zhòngrén zhī suǒ ài*),
is to **obtain** (*dé*) the **people’s** **strength** (*zhòngrén zhī lì*).
To **promote** (*tuī*) what the **people** **hold dear** (*zhòngrén zhī suǒ xǐ*),
is to **obtain** (*dé*) the **people’s** **hearts** (*zhòngrén zhī xīn*).

Therefore, **seeing** their **beginning** (*jiàn qí suǒ shǐ*),
one **knows** their **end** (*bì zhī qí suǒ zhōng*).

The **heart** of **father and son** (*fùzǐ zhī xīn*) is **natural** (*tiānxìng*).
The **father’s** **love** for his **son**,
the **ruler’s** **nourishment** of his **people**—
if there is a **heart of expectation** (*yǒu wàngbào zhī xīn*),
then his **kindness** will **be lost** (*qí shī*).*

Thus, to **follow** (*yīn*) their **profit** and **profit** them (*yǔ lì zhī*),
is to **obtain** the **people’s** **strength** (*dé zhòngrén zhī lì*).
To **extend** (*tuī*) their **goodness** and **benefit** them (*yǔ shàn zhī*),
is to **obtain** the **people’s** **hearts** (*dé zhòngrén zhī xīn*)."

The **son** may be **disobedient**, the **minister** may be **disloyal**—
thus **Laozi** says:

*"When **people** are bound by **righteousness** (*yì*) in **love** (*ài*),
and **clans** unite through **collective strength** (*qúnqiáng*),
then where **virtue** (*dé*) extends broadly,
**authority** (*wēi*) reaches far;
where **righteousness** is applied thinly,
**force** (*wǔ*) governs little.

When the **ruler** takes the **world** as his **heart** (*tianxià wéi xīn*),
and **people** are bound by **righteous love** (*yìài*),
then **loyalty** and **filial piety** (*zhōngxiào*) arise.
When **clans** unite through **collective strength**,
then **crafty tyrants** (*jiānxióng*) emerge.

Where **safety** and **danger** hinge—
how can one not **discern** (*chá*) and **debate** (*biàn*)?

**Laozi** says:
*'If gained through **unrighteousness** (*bùyì*) and not **disseminated** (*bù bùshī*),
then **calamity** will strike his **person** (*huàn jí qí shēn*),
he cannot **benefit others** (*bù néng wéi rén*),
nor **provide for himself** (*yòu wúyǐ zìwéi*).
This is the **fool**—
how does he differ from the **owl** that **loves its young**?'

Thus, **holding** and **filling** it to excess (*chí ér yíng zhī*),
is not as good as **stopping** (*bùrú qí yǐ*).
**Sharpening** and **grinding** it to a point (*chuǎi ér ruì zhī*),
cannot be **long preserved**.

Within **virtue** lies the **Way** (*dé zhōng yǒu dào*),
within the **Way** lies **virtue** (*dào zhōng yǒu dé*),
its **transformation** (*huà*) cannot be **exhausted**.

Within **yang** is **yin**, within **yin** is **yang**—
all **affairs** (*wànshì*) are thus,
inexhaustible in their **clarity**.

When **blessing** arrives, **auspicious signs** (*xiáng*) appear;
when **calamity** comes, **auspicious signs** precede.
If one **sees auspicious signs** but does not **do good** (*bù wéi shàn*),
then **blessing** will not come.
If one **sees inauspicious signs** but **does good**,
then **calamity** will not strike.

**Benefit** and **harm** share a **threshold** (*tóngmén*),
**calamity** and **blessing** are **neighbors** (*tónglín*).
None but the **sage** (*shèngshèng*) can **discriminate** (*fèn*).

Thus it is said:
*"Calamity* (*huò*) **leans** on **blessing** (*fú*),
**blessing** **hides** within **calamity** (*fú xià huò fú*).
Who can **fathom** its **limit** (*qí jí*)?

**Calamity** and **blessing** **lean and hide** (*yǐfú*),
like **shadows** following **form**.
**Profit** doubles in **gain** (*huò bèi ér rù*),
it must **double** in **loss** (*bì bèi ér chū*).

**Goodness** accumulated but **not properly used** (*shàn jī ér bù shàn yòng*),
is like **rearing an owl** as one’s **son**—
**few** escape **self-harm**.

The **mechanism** of **benefit** and **harm** (*lìhài zhī jī*)
**reverses** and **overturns** (*fǎn fù*),
none but the **sage** can **know** (*wú shèngshèng mó zhī*).

When **people** are about to **fall ill** (*jiāng jī*),
they must first **crave** the **flavor** of **fish and meat**.
When a **state** is about to **perish** (*jiāng wáng*),
it must first **spurn** the **words** of **loyal ministers**.

Thus, the **dying** cannot be **healed** by a **good physician**,
and the **perishing state** cannot be **saved** by **loyal counsel**.

**Cultivate** oneself (*xiū zhī shēn*),
then one may **govern the people**.
**Manage** the **household** (*jūjiā lǐ*),
then one may **extend** to **officials and superiors**.

Thus it is said:
*"Cultivate* oneself, and **virtue** becomes **true** (*dé nǎi zhēn*);
**Cultivate** the **household**, and **virtue** becomes **abundant** (*dé nǎi yú*);
**Cultivate** the **state**, and **virtue** becomes **flourishing** (*dé nǎi fēng*).

The **people’s** means of **livelihood** (*shēnghuó*)
are **clothing** and **food**.
If **matters** concerning **clothing and food** are **attended to**, there is **merit** (*yǒu gōng*);
if **neglected**, there is **none** (*wú gōng*).
If **matters** lack **merit**, **virtue** cannot **endure** (*dé bù cháng*).

Thus, **following the times** but failing to **succeed** (*suíshí wúchéng*),
do not **alter** the **laws** (*wú gēng qí xíng*).
**Conforming** to the **times** but failing to **succeed**,
do not **change** the **principles** (*wú gēng qí lǐ*).
When the **times** return, this is called the **pattern of the Way** (*dào jì*).

A **state** cannot **stand** without the **people** (*guó fēi mín bù lì*),
the **people** cannot **live** without **food** (*mín fēi shí bù shēng*)—
an **unchangeable principle** (*bù yì zhī lǐ*).

Thus, if the **people** are **sufficient** in **clothing and food**, they may **live** (*mín zú yú yīshí zé kě huó*);
if **deficient**, there is **no merit** (*bù zú zé wáng gōng*).
If **merit** is not **established**, **virtue** cannot **endure**.

The **Sage-King** enriches his **people** (*fù qí mín*),
the **Hegemon** enriches his **lands** (*fù qí dì*),
the **dangerous state** enriches its **officials** (*fù qí lì*),
the **governed state** appears **insufficient** (*ruò bù zú*),
the **perishing state** is **empty of grain** (*kùn cāng xū*).

Thus it is said:
*"When the **ruler** does **nothing**, the **people** prosper of themselves;
when the **ruler** does **not act**, the **people** transform of themselves."*

Raising an **army** of **ten thousand men**,
spending **a thousand gold** daily—
after the **campaign**, there will **surely** be **harsh years**.

Thus, **arms** are the **unauspicious instrument** (*bù xiáng zhī qì*),
not the **treasure** of the **noble** (*fēi jūnzǐ zhī bǎo*).

**Conciliating** great **grievances** will **leave lingering resentment** (*yǒu yúyuàn*),
how can this be **not evil** (*nǎihé qí wéi bùshàn*)?

In **ancient times**, the **near** were not **summoned** by **words**,
the **distant** were not **commanded** by **speech**.
The **near** were **pleased**, the **distant** **came**.

**Sharing** the **people’s desires** brings **harmony** (*tóngyù jí hé*),
**sharing** the **people’s defenses** brings **solidarity** (*tóngshǒu jí gù*),
**sharing** the **people’s thoughts** brings **wisdom** (*tóngniàn jí zhī*),
**gaining** the **people’s strength** brings **wealth** (*dé mín lì zé fù*),
**gaining** the **people’s praise** brings **renown** (*dé mín yù zé xiǎn*).

**Actions** may **invite bandits** (*xíng yǒu zhào kè*),
**words** may **bring calamity** (*yán yǒu zhì huò*).
Do not **speak before others** (*wú xiān rén yán*),
only **speak after others** (*hòu rén ér yǐ*).
**Whispered words** may **travel a thousand *lǐ*** (*liú wén qiān lǐ*).

**Speech** is **calamity** (*yán zhě huò yě*),
the **tongue** is the **mechanism** (*shé zhě jī yě*).
**Words** spoken **inappropriately**,
even **four horses** cannot **retrace** (*sì mǎ bù zhuī*).

There is **no eternal order** of **government** (*wú gǔjīn zhìluàn*),
nor **unchangeable chaos** (*wú bùyì zhī tǔyǔ*).
**Ancient** and **modern**, some **govern well**, some **govern poorly**—
this depends on **ruler and people** (*ér bù yī yú jūn mín yě*).

The **Sage-King** enriches his **people**,
the **Hegemon** enriches his **lands**,
the **dangerous state** enriches its **officials**,
the **governed state** appears **insufficient**,
the **perishing state** is **empty of grain**.

Thus, in the **Tang and Yu** era, the **world** was **prosperous** (*dàitiānxià wǎng*),
while the **Warring States** saw **no wealthy people**.

Once, **Zhōnghuáng Zǐ** said:
*"Heaven has **five directions**,
Earth has **five phases**,
Sound has **five tones**,
**Things** have **five flavors**,
**Colors** have **five hues**,
**People** have **five ranks** (*wùwèi*).
Thus, between **Heaven and Earth**, there are **twenty-five kinds of people** (*èrshíwǔ rén*).

The **upper five** are:
**Divine Person** (*shénrén*), **True Person** (*zhēnrén*), **Dao Person** (*dàorén*), **Perfect Person** (*zhìrén*), **Sage** (*shèngrén*).
The **next five** are:
**Virtuous Person** (*dérén*), **Exemplary Person** (*xiánrén*), **Wise Person** (*zhìrén*), **Good Person** (*shànrén*), **Persuasive Person** (*biànrén*).
The **middle five** are:
**Public Person** (*gōngrén*), **Loyal Person** (*zhōngrén*), **Trustworthy Person** (*xìnrén*), **Righteous Person** (*yìrén*), **Ceremonious Person** (*lǐrén*).
The **next five** are:
**Scholar** (*shìrén*), **Artisan** (*gōngrén*), **Hunter** (*yúrén*), **Farmer** (*nóngrén*), **Merchant** (*shāngrén*).
The **lowest five** are:
**Common Person** (*zhòngrén*), **Slave** (*núrén*), **Fool** (*yúrén*), **Brute** (*ròurén*), **Petty Person** (*xiǎorén*).
The **upper five** and the **lower five**—
are as different as **man** and **ox or horse**.

The **Sage** **sees** with his **eyes**,
**hears** with his **ears**,
**speaks** with his **mouth**,
**acts** with his **feet**.

The **True Person** **sees without clarity** (*bù shì ér míng*),
**hears without sharpness** (*bù tīng ér cōng*),
**acts without following** (*bù xíng ér cóng*),
**speaks without publicizing** (*bù yán ér gōng*).

Thus, what the **Sage** uses to **move the world** (*dòng tiānxià*),
the **True Person** has **never surpassed**.
What the **Exemplary Person** uses to **rectify the world** (*jiǎo shìsú*),
the **Sage** has **never observed**.

The so-called **Way** (*suǒwèi dào zhě*)
has **no front or back** (*wú qián wú hòu*),
**no left or right** (*wú zuǒ wú yòu*).
All **things** are **mysteriously unified** (*wànwù xuán tóng*),
**neither this nor that** (*wú shì wú fēi*).

**Zhōnghuáng Zǐ**—
was he the **True Person** of **antiquity**?

His words say:
*"People have **five ranks** (*wèi*),
each **rank** has **five degrees** (*dèng*),
combined, they make **twenty-five** (*fán èrshíyǒu wǔ*).

The **highest** is the **Divine Person** (*shénrén*),
the **lowest** is the **Petty Person** (*xiǎorén*).
The **upper five** and the **lower five**—
are as different as **man** and **ox or horse**,
for the **Petty Person** **defies the Way** and **betrays virtue** (*wéidào bèidé*),
like **ox or horse** adorned with **garments** (*rú mǎniú ér yīn jīnyú*).

The **Sage** does not **reach** the **True Person**,
the **Exemplary Person** does not **reach** the **Sage**—
only their **cultivation of the Way** has **depths** (*zàodào yǒu qiǎnshēn*),
thus their **ranks** follow accordingly."

**Continuation of *Tongxuan Zhenjing Yiyi* (卷之七 complete) #1 *Wēn* [湿]: *Jūzhēn* edition reads *"shī"* (湿).
#2 *Zhě* [者]: Original text has *"zhī"* (之), corrected here according to *Jūzhēn* edition.**

**卷之八: *Nángǔ Zǐ* Du Dàojiān’s *Zhuān* – *Zìrán Piān***

**Lǎozǐ yuē:**
*"Qīngxū zhě, tiān zhī míng yě; wúwéi zhě, zhì zhī cháng yě.*
To abandon favors and wisdom,
to discard the sage’s cleverness,
to reject the virtuous and capable,
to abolish rites and moral teachings,
to extinguish customs and debates,
to ban deceit and falsehood—
then the **Exemplary** and the **Petty** alike align with the **Dao**.

*Jìng, then all is unified;
Xū, then all is harmonized.*
**Supreme virtue** is **non-action**—
all things find their place within it.

The **Dao of emptiness and stillness**—
heaven’s duration, earth’s endurance,
**qi** subtly pervades, boundless.
Over all things, it holds no sway.

**Zìrán** is **heaven’s principle**;
**bù zìrán** is **human desire**.

When **clear and empty**, it shines—
this is **heaven’s natural state**.
When **non-action** governs, it is **ordered**—
this is **human nature’s way**.

**Zìrán**—then the **Exemplary** and the **Petty** align with the **Dao**.

Thus the **Sage**, divine and luminous,
illuminates the world—
yet no thing is ruled by him.

The **twelve months** cycle endlessly,
**wood, metal, water, fire, earth**—
their forces clash, yet their **Dao** depends on one another.
Thus **extreme cold** harms, yet **no cold** is without harm;
**extreme heat** harms, yet **no heat** is without harm—
so **what is permissible** and **what is not** both find their place.
Thus the **Great Dao** knows no **‘cannot’**—
what is permissible lies in its **principle**;
when one sees **what is permissible**, one does not rush toward it;
when one sees **what is not permissible**, one does not reject it.
**Permissible** and **impermissible** are like left and right,
like exterior and interior.

All things begin with **oneness**,
**time** marks their measure—
from antiquity to today, unchanging—
this is called **heaven’s principle**.

Above, it holds **Great Clarity**;
below, it manifests as **light**.
The **Dao** gives birth to all things,
its **principle** lies in **yin and yang**,
its transformation becomes the **four seasons**,
its division forms the **five phases**—
each attains its proper place.

In harmony with **time**, it flows with **law**,
its measures are constant.
Though the lowly lack ability,
the **Dao** above does not falter.
Ministers act in unity—
**heaven and earth’s Dao**:
**non-action** yet complete,
**no seeking** yet all is gained—
thus one knows that **non-action** brings benefit.

Between **heaven and earth**, what flows and transforms without ceasing?
It is **qi** alone.
There is **spirit** within it—
yet none can recognize it.

**Oneness** marks its beginning,
**time** its measure—
from ancient to present, it never errs—
this is called **heaven’s principle**.

The **Dao** gives birth to all things,
its measures are constant.
Something governs them—
yet none know who commands.
Thus the **Dao’s** grandeur and **virtue’s** nobility are unmatched.

**Lǎozǐ yuē:**
*"Pǔ zhìdà zhě wú xíngzhuàng,
Dào zhìdà zhě wú dùliàng,
gù tiān yuán bù zhòng guī,
dìfāng bù zhòng jǔ.
Wǎnggǔ láijīn wèi zhòu,
sìfāng shàngxià wèi yǔ.
Dào zài qízhōng ér mò zhī qí suǒ.
Gù jiàn bùyuǎn zhě bùkě yǔ yán dà,
zhī bù bó zhě bùkě lùn zhì.
Fú Dào yǔ wù tōng zhě wú yǐ xiāng fěi,
gù sānhuáng wǔdì fǎjí shūfāng,
qí dé mínxīn yī yě.

Ruó fú guījǔ gōulǐng,
xiāo zhī jù yě, ér fēi suǒyǐ wéi xiāo yě.
Gù wúxián suī shī wén bùnéng chéng qí qǔ,
túxián jí bùnéng dú bēi.
Gù xián, bēi zhī jù yě, ér fēi suǒyǐ wéi bēi yě.
Shì yú shén hé yóu yú xīnshǒu zhī jiān,
fàngyì xiěshén, lùn biàn ér xíng yú xián zhě,
fù bùnéng yǐ jiāo zǐ, zǐ yì bùnéng shòu zhī yú fù,
cǐ bùchuán zhī Dào yě.

Gù sù zhě, xíng zhī jūn yě, ér jìmò zhě, yīn zhī zhǔ yě.
Pǔ dà wúxíng, Dào dà wúliàng.
Tiān yuán dìfāng, Dào zài qízhōng.
Gù sānhuáng wǔdì, fǎjí shūfāng, qí dé mínxīn yī yě.
Ruó fú guījǔ gōulǐng, zé shì wéi xiāo zhī jù, fēi suǒyǐ wéi xiāo yě.
Shīwén zhī qín, yǒu lèi yú shì.
Lǎozǐ yuē:
*‘Tiān dì zhī Dào, yǐ dé wéi zhǔ, Dào wéi zhī mìng, wù yǐ zì zhèng.*
*Zhì wēi jìn nèi, bù yǐ shì guì.*
Gù bù dài gōng ér lì, bù yǐ wèi wéi zūn,
bù dài míng ér xiǎn, bù xū lǐ wéi zhuāng,
bù yòng bīng ér qiáng.
Gù Dào lì ér bù jiào, míng zhào ér bù chá.
Dào lì ér bù jiào zhě, bù duó rén néng yě;
míng zhào ér bù chá zhě, bù hài qí shì yě.
Gǔ rén zhìpǔ, qí sú tóng, gù bù dài jiào.
Hòurén jiāolí, qí sú yì, gù shèngrén yōu Dào zhī bùmíng ér jiào lì tiān,
tiān dì zhī Dào, yǐ dé wéi zhǔ, ér Dào wéi zhī mìng,
wù gè zì zhèng, zìrán ér yǐ.
Shèngrén hé yōng xīn zāi?
Fú jiàodào zhě, nì yú dé, hài yú wù,
gù yīnyáng sìjì, jīnmù shuǐhuǒtǔ tóng Dào ér yì lǐ.
Wànwù tóng qíng ér yì xíng,
zhī zhě bù xiāng jiào, néng zhě bù xiāng shòu,
gù shèngrén lìfǎ yǐ dǎo mín zhī xīn,
gè shǐ zìrán.
Gù shēng zhě wú dé yě, sǐ zhě wú yuàn yě.
Tiān dì bù rén yǐ wànwù wéi chúgǒu,
shèngrén bù rén yǐ bǎixìng wéi chúgǒu.
Fú cí’ài rén yì zhě, jìnxiá zhī Dào yě.
Xiá zhě, rù dà ér mí;
jìn zhě, xíng yuǎn ér huò,
shèngrén zhī Dào rù dà bù mí, xíng yuǎn bù huò,
cháng xū zìshǒu, kěyǐ wéi jí, yìwèi tiān dé.
Gǔ zhī jiào zhě yǐ Dào,
jīn zhī jiào zhě yǐ lì,
cǐ Dào zhī suǒyǐ bùxíng yě.
Fú shèngrén lìfǎ dǎo mínxīn,
shǐ gè ān qí zìrán zhī fèn,
shēng zhě zì shēng, sǐ zhě zì sǐ,
dé yuàn hé yǒu zāi?"*

The **Laozi** says: The **Sage** is like the heavens covering and the earth bearing, the sun and moon shining upon all, the **Yin** and **Yang** harmonized, the four seasons transforming—embracing all things yet not conforming, neither rigidly old nor newly fanciful, neither distant nor close in favor.

Thus, the **Sage** can follow the **Heavenly Dao**, for **Heaven** does not act at one time, the earth does not hoard one kind of wealth, and humans do not pursue one kind of affair. Therefore, undertakings are manifold, and actions proceed in diverse directions. Thus, those who wield arms may act with lightness or weight, greed or restraint—these four opposites cannot be uniformly applied.

The light of heart may wish to strike, the heavy of heart may wish to halt, the greedy may wish to seize, the restrained may see no profit—yet this is not their true nature. Therefore, the bold can be ordered to advance and fight, but not to hold fast to the unyielding; the heavy can be ordered to stand firm, but not to assail the enemy; the greedy can be ordered to attack and take, but not to divide spoils; the restrained can be ordered to keep their share, but not to advance and seize; the trustworthy can be ordered to uphold strictures, but not to adapt to circumstances.

The **Sage** employs these five qualities in combination, assigning them according to their talents. **Heaven** and **Earth** do not favor one thing, nor do **Yin** and **Yang** produce one kind of being. Therefore, the sea does not refuse the trickling waters to become vast, the mountains and forests do not reject the crooked and bent to become lofty, and the **Sage** does not spurn the humble words of the common folk to broaden their influence.

To cling to one corner while neglecting the rest, to take one thing while discarding others—this is to make one’s gains few and one’s governance shallow. **Heaven’s** command is singular, yet the **qi** of sensation varies, nature follows substance in difference, and transformation yields myriad distinctions—these cannot be arbitrarily forced into uniformity.

The **Sage** aligns with **Heaven** and follows the **Dao**, not regarding diverse regions and customs as obstacles, but covering and bearing them, nourishing and fostering them, all in one principle. **Laozi** says: What **Heaven** covers and the earth bears, what the sun and moon illuminate—though their forms differ and their natures vary, each finds its own place of rest.

What brings **joy** also brings **sorrow**, what brings **rest** also brings **peril**. Thus, the **Sage** tends the people by letting each follow their nature, rest in their dwelling, dwell where they suit, do what they are able, adapt to what they meet, and apply what they need. In this way, all things are in harmony, with no cause for mutual offense.

All things under **Heaven** have no intrinsic nobility or baseness; they are made noble by what is valued, and all things are noble. They are made base by what is despised, and all things are base. Therefore, the **Sage** who does not exalt the worthy does not cast fish into trees nor drown birds in deep pools.

A good shepherd of the people does not apply one law to all; though forms differ and natures vary, each finds their own place of rest. If one reverses this and forces uniformity, the **Dao** of life is lost.

Now then, what **Zhuangzi** called *Qíwùlùn* ("On Equalizing Things")—is it truly about equalizing things? It is about the **logic of equalizing things**. **Heaven’s** creation of beings assigns each according to their inherent talents and strengthens them accordingly.

In the time of **Yao**, his governance of the world saw **Shun** as Minister of Instruction, **Xie** as Minister of War, **Yu** as Minister of Works, **Houji** as Master of Fields, and **Xizhong** as Master of Crafts. In guiding the people, those dwelling near water were taught fishing, those in forests were taught hunting, those in valleys were taught herding, and those on land were taught farming.

The land suited their labors, their labors suited their tools, and their tools suited their talents. In the marshes, nets were woven; on the hillsides, fields were tilled. Thus, the people found ease in what they possessed, and what they lacked they exchanged for what they had skill in, while what they had skill in they exchanged for what they lacked. Therefore, few abandoned him, and many obeyed, like the wind passing through reeds, stirring them effortlessly.

Each responded according to their clarity or turbidity, and all sought what benefited them and avoided what harmed them. Thus, neighboring states looked upon one another, the sounds of chickens and dogs were heard across borders, yet footsteps did not cross into the domains of the lords, nor were tracks left beyond a thousand miles—all rested in their own dwellings.

Therefore, a chaotic state seems like a well-ordered one, a flourishing state seems like a decaying one, a state with abundance seems like one lacking, and a state with survival seems like one with excess.

The decaying is not without people, but each guards their own role; the flourishing is not from many people, but all are bent toward trivial pursuits; the abundant is not from excess wealth, but from moderation and few demands; the lacking is not from scarcity, but from sparse people and heavy expenditures.

Thus, the laws of the ancient kings were not their own creation, but what they followed; their punishments and executions were not their own doing, but what they upheld—the **highest virtue’s Dao**.

The ancient rulers of the world were at ease while their ministers labored, governing without action. In **Yao’s** time, **Shun** was Minister of Instruction, **Xie** was Minister of War, **Yu** was Minister of Works, and all officials held their posts, each according to their ability. When the right person was placed in office, the people rested in their dwellings, and the work was accomplished, the affairs settled—all the people said, *"This is as if by nature."*

**Laozi** says: To govern the world with the **Dao** is not to alter human nature, but to clarify and smooth what already exists. Therefore, to follow is great, to impose is small.

The ancient who dredged water followed its flow, those who grew crops followed the land’s suitability, and those who waged war followed the people’s desires. To follow is to have no rival in the world.

Things must first be in accordance with their nature before human affairs can be properly ordered. Therefore, the ancient kings’ institutions were based on human nature, establishing measures and regulations to guide it.

Without its nature, one cannot make it conform to instruction; with its nature but lacking the means, one cannot make it follow the **Dao**. Human nature has the potential for **virtue** and **righteousness**, yet without the **Sage’s** guidance, laws and regulations cannot make it turn toward the proper path.

To act against its nature, one must use what it dislikes to restrain deceit—thus, punishments are not applied, yet authority spreads like the divine. To act with its nature, the world listens and follows; to act against it, laws and regulations are proclaimed yet ignored.

The **Sage** establishes teaching in accordance with human nature and observes customs to govern. What the people love, the **Sage** loves; what the people hate, the **Sage** hates. Thus, the people’s hearts turn toward him, and he has no rival in the world.

**Dao** and **virtue** are the roots of merit and renown, what the people cherish. When the people cherish them, merit and renown are established.

The ancient who excelled in rule followed the **Dao** of the great rivers: the great rivers act without action to become vast, sink low to become broad, and thus endure as the world’s channels and valleys. Their **virtue** is sufficient, without action they receive all streams, without seeking they obtain, without striving they arrive. Therefore, they take the world.

#1 *"易所拙"* (easily exchange what they lack for what they are skilled in)

The **Dao** is such that the unadorned is most noble—thus wealth comes without striving,
the unseen is most clear—thus wisdom arises without display,
the unvaunted endures longest—thus greatness is achieved without self-aggrandizement,
dwelling where none reside.
Thus, one becomes the sovereign of the world:
without contention, none can contend with them;
when they cease to strive for greatness, true greatness is attained.
The rivers and seas are close to the **Dao**, thus they endure long and align with Heaven and Earth.
The sovereign who cultivates the **Dao** completes their work without claiming it—
not claiming it makes them strong and firm,
strong and firm without tyranny over others,
deep in the **Dao** means deep in virtue,
deep virtue brings lasting renown—this is called **mysterious virtue**, profound and distant,
contrary to the ways of things.

**Dao** and virtue are not tools for seeking fame,
yet fame follows naturally.
The ancient who excelled in rule
preserved the **virtue** of non-action,
dwelled in the position of non-contention,
completed their work without claiming it,
thus they could endure alongside Heaven and Earth.

The world has a beginning,
yet none know its principle—
only the **Sage** knows how.
Neither male nor female,
neither yin nor yang,
born yet not dying.

Heaven and Earth are completed,
yin and yang take form,
and all things are born.
Thus, yin and yang have round and square,
short and long,
existence and extinction—
the **Dao** names them.

Obscure and deep, yet without action,
in the heart it is subtle,
in the **Dao** it is correct,
life and death share the same principle,
all things transform and merge into the **Dao**—
simplifying life, forgetting death,
where can one not be long-lived?
Abandoning deeds and words,
following non-action,
guarding the **Dao** thoroughly,
not dominating things,
subtle and formless.

At the origin of Heaven and Earth,
all things revolve within the **Dao** yet differ in form—
utterly subtle, without substance,
thus they can embrace all;
utterly vast, without boundary,
thus they become all things;
utterly minute, without interior,
thus they are most precious to all things.
The **Dao** sustains life,
virtue stabilizes form.
The measure of the **Dao** is to abandon preferences and aversions,
without knowing yet without error,
easing the mind and harmonizing the heart,
without departing from the **Dao**.

Heaven and Earth unite as one,
yet divide into two,
then return and merge.
Above and below remain unlost,
united as one,
divided into five,
then returning to merge—
necessarily conforming to the square and compass.

The **Dao** is most intimate yet cannot be slighted,
most near yet cannot be distanced—
seeking it far away only leads back.
To know the ancient origins is to grasp the **Dao**’s principles.

Is the **Dao** the origin of the world?
Formless yet vast,
unspoken yet trusted,
transforming without measure,
its principle unknown—
only the **Sage** knows it.
Thus, daily responding to myriad affairs,
never opposing the **Dao**,
returning and merging—
this is the **Dao**’s principle.

Laozi said:
The **Emperor** has a name,
yet none know their essence.
The **Emperor** values **virtue**,
the **King** upholds righteousness,
the **Conqueror** grasps principle.

The **Sage**’s **Dao** in things is nonexistent—
only when paths narrow do they employ intelligence,
when virtue is shallow do they rely on punishment,
when insight is shallow do they scrutinize.
Those who rely on intelligence bring inner chaos,
those who rely on punishment invite grievance from above and below,
those who rely on scrutiny fail to seek goodness to serve their superiors and thus bring ruin.
Thus, the **Sage** follows Heaven and Earth in transforming their **virtue**—
Heaven covers, Earth bears,
guiding by season,
nourishment thus becomes abundant,
abundant nourishment brings order.
Even with divine sagacity,
what could replace this?

Abandon artificial knowledge,
reduce punishments,
return to clarity and stillness—
things will naturally correct themselves.

At the dawn of civilization, who was the **Emperor**?
In the **Primeval Age**, the Three Sovereigns:
the people were like infants,
breathing in harmony with the **Great Unity**,
without thought or action.
In the **High Antiquity**, the Three Sovereigns:
the people were like young children,
content with sucking and patting their bellies,
doing nothing deliberately.
In the **Later Antiquity**, the Three Sovereigns:
the people were like children,
simple and scattered, the **Dao** still in practice,
cooking meat and grains,
the world cherished them.
The Five **Emperors** valued **virtue**,
the world praised them.
The Three **Kings** upheld righteousness,
the world feared them.
The Five **Hegemons** lost principle,
the world scorned them.

When the **Mysterious Sage** does not act,
the **Plain King** follows,
establishing words and teachings,
becoming the teacher for ten thousand generations.
When a **Sage** arises again,
nothing can replace them.

The **Dao** as sovereign
is like a corpse standing solemn and silent,
yet the world receives its blessings—
one person covered by it is not excessive,
ten thousand covered by it is not confined.
Thus, excessive kindness is as harmful as excessive cruelty—
both encounter the **Dao**.

Kindness is giving without merit,
bestowing high rank without labor—
then those in office grow slack,
and idlers rush forward eagerly.
Cruelty is killing without guilt,
punishing those who follow the **Dao**—
then those who cultivate themselves cease to encourage virtue
while evildoers grow bold in defiance.

Thus, excessive kindness breeds deceit,
excessive cruelty breeds chaos—
deceit and chaos are the winds of a collapsing state.
Hence, when there are executions, the ruler is not angry,
when there are rewards, the ruler does not share in them—
those punished bear no grudge, for the ruler’s blame is just,
those rewarded feel no debt, for the ruler’s merit is complete.
The people know that punishments and rewards arise from themselves,
thus they strive for merit and cultivate their work without relying on others.
Therefore, the court is orderly without trace,
the fields are tilled without filth.

Thus, the **Supreme Sovereign** knows what is natural—
spring gives birth, autumn kills—
this is the **Dao** of Heaven.
The ruler follows Heaven’s **Dao**,
and subjects know what to fear and emulate.
Punishments and rewards arise from themselves—
no one harbors hopes of fortune.
The court is upright, the fields are clear—
the **Supreme Sovereign**’s ways can be restored.

The **Kingly Way** dwells in non-action,
practices speechless teaching,
remains still and unmoving,
fixed yet unshaken,
follows naturally without coercion,
achieves results without toil,
plans without error,
acts without fault,
speaks without rhetoric,
moves without ceremony,
advances and retreats in season,
active and still in accordance with principle.
Neither praising beauty nor hating ugliness,
neither favoring reward nor dreading punishment,
names are self-bestowed,
categories self-classified,
affairs arise naturally—
none originate from them.

If one seeks to constrain it, one only drives it apart;
if one seeks to adorn it, one only robs it of its essence.

Heaven’s **qi** is the **hun** (spirit-soul),
Earth’s **qi** is the **po** (corporeal soul).
Their interplay is profound and mysterious—
each abides in its proper domain.
Guard them and do not lose them,
and one ascends to **Taiyi** (the Supreme Unity).
The essence of **Taiyi** merges with Heaven.

The **Dao** of Heaven is silent and boundless,
without form or measure,
too vast to fathom, too deep to sound,
yet ever transforming with humanity—
wisdom cannot grasp it,
its motion turns without end,
its transformations unfold like divinity.
It follows naturally, ever yielding yet never leading.

In governance, one must hold a **empty heart** and **weakened resolve**,
clear and bright without obscurity—
thus ministers converge like spokes to a hub.
Whether wise or foolish, virtuous or base,
all contribute their fullest measure.
The sovereign gains the means to govern his ministers,
the ministers gain the means to serve their sovereign—
thus the clarity of ruling a state is achieved.

He who holds **all-under-Heaven** need not fear disorder,
but fear failing to secure the right people.
To secure the right people is to let the **Kingly Way** flourish above—
guard it and do not lose it,
and one ascends to **Taiyi**.

Its motion turns without end,
its transformations unfold like divinity.
Ministers converge, each contributing their fullest measure.
Thus it is known that the order or chaos of a state depends on its people.

**Laozi** says:
*"He who knows and loves to ask is sage,
he who is courageous and loves to ask is victorious.
He who rides the wisdom of the masses can accomplish anything,
he who harnesses the strength of the masses can overcome anything.
He who relies on the masses’ strength need not fear failure,
he who harnesses the masses’ momentum can achieve all-under-Heaven.
Without the **power** of proper **effort**, no **method** can succeed—
even the sage cannot force success.
Thus the sage, in undertaking any matter, never fails to utilize what is already present.
One who has a talent occupies a position,
one who has an ability undertakes a task.
If strength matches the task, the appointed will not labor in vain,
if ability matches the task, the executor will not find it difficult.
The sage combines and employs them all—
thus no person is discarded, no talent is wasted.
Wisdom, benevolence, and courage—
these are the universal virtues,
and love of inquiry enriches them.
To unite the wisdom of the masses,
to harness their strength—
then all-under-Heaven is unconquerable.
One who has a talent occupies a position,
one who has an ability undertakes a task—
the sage combines and employs them all,
thus no person is discarded, no talent is wasted."*

**Laozi** says:
*"What is called **non-action** is not that it does not draw near when summoned,
nor repel when pushed,
nor fail to respond when pressed,
nor remain unmoved when stirred,
nor become stagnant when still,
nor disperse when gathered.
It means private desires do not intrude upon public **Dao**,
passions do not cling to proper **method**,
actions follow **principle**,
achievements arise from what is already present,
pushing along the natural momentum—
artifice finds no place.
When the deed is done, the self takes no credit;
when the work is complete, the name is not claimed.
As water carries the boat,
as sand bears the **jue** (a type of cart),
as mud supports the **tuo** (a type of sled),
as mountains hold the **zhu** (a type of rope),
summer floods and winter pools—
all arise from high and low.
This is not what I do.
The sage does not shame his humble station,
but laments the **Dao**’s failure to prevail.
He does not grieve his short lifespan,
but grieves the people’s hardship.
Thus he remains ever **empty** and **non-acting**,
embracing simplicity and returning to naturalness,
not mingling with the myriad things.
**Non-action** is not having a heart like wood or stone—
unmoving.
The sage responds to things without preceding them,
following their natural momentum,
to shape all things—
what need is there for action?"*

**Laozi** says:
*"The ancient founders of imperial rule did not establish it to indulge their desires,
nor did the sage who ascended the throne do so for personal ease.
They saw how the strong oppressed the weak,
how the many tyrannized the few,
how the cunning deceived the simple,
how the brave overcame the timid—
and how the wise hoarded knowledge without teaching,
how the rich accumulated wealth without sharing.
Thus they established the **Son of Heaven** to unify all,
for one mind cannot illuminate all-under-Heaven,
so they appointed the **Three Ministers** and **Nine Officials** to assist,
for isolated states and distant customs could not receive his blessings,
so they installed the **feudal lords** to instruct them.
Thus Heaven and Earth, the four seasons—
none fail to respond.
No office hides its duties,
no state neglects its benefits—
thus the cold are clothed, the hungry fed,
the aged and weak cared for,
the weary rested—
none are left unprovided for.
Is the **Son of Heaven** then the heart of Heaven and Earth?
The vastness of the land, the multitude of the people—
without a ruler to govern them,
the strong oppress the weak,
the many tyrannize the few,
the cunning deceive the simple,
and the people cannot live in peace.
Thus the **Son of Heaven** is established,
the **Three Ministers** appointed,
to be parents to the people,
nurturing and guiding them.
The *Yijing* says:
*'The later sovereigns completed Heaven and Earth’s **Dao**,
supplemented what Heaven and Earth lacked,
governing the people left and right—
thus the heart of Heaven and Earth becomes visible.'*

**Shennong**’s body withered,
**Yao**’s limbs were emaciated,
**Shun**’s skin darkened from exposure,
**Yu**’s feet were calloused and cracked.
**Yi Yin** carried a caldron to serve **Tang**,
**Jiang Ziya** wielded a butcher’s cleaver before entering **Zhou**,
**Bai Li Xi** was sold into slavery,
**Guan Zhong** was bound in chains,
**Confucius** lacked even a single loyal follower,
**Mozi** had no warm bed—
not for greed of office or love of rank,
but to awaken the benefits of all-under-Heaven,
to eliminate the harms of the myriad people.
From the **Son of Heaven** to the commoner,
none escape laboring with their bodies,
none escape the strain of thought—
such is the way of seeking sufficiency in affairs.
The ancient sages’ ears, eyes, mouths, and noses were no different from others,
their hunger, thirst, cold, and warmth no different—
what differed was their **heart**.
They established the ultimate **principle** for all generations of living beings.
All who stand among the sages
rarely achieve their deeds without first exhausting their **heart-mind**,
yet when their deeds are done, they do not claim them—
this is what makes them sage.

**Laozi** says:
*"What is called the **Son of Heaven** is one who establishes all-under-Heaven with the **Dao** of Heaven.
The **Dao** of establishing all-under-Heaven
holds to **Oneness** as its safeguard,
returns to the root with **non-action**,
is **empty** and **still** without possession,
faint and boundless without end,
far-reaching without cessation—
seen, it has no form;
heard, it has no sound.
This is called the **classic course** of the **Great Dao**."*

The heavens and earth are the great parents of humankind.
All who bear blood and qi are the children of Heaven, with the sovereign as their leader, acting as Heaven’s deputy son.
Hence the title *Son of Heaven*.

The *Son of Heaven* regards the people as his own children—
if he does not neglect what nourishes them,
then Heaven’s gaze and Heaven’s hearing respond,
and this mutual resonance is called the *classic course* of the *Great Dao*.

**Laozi** says:
The *Dao* embodies roundness yet follows square law,
backs the yin while embracing the yang,
holds softness to the left and firmness to the right,
walks in darkness while wearing brightness,
transforms without constancy—
when grasped in its original essence, it adapts to all directions.
This is called *divine illumination*.

The heavens are round yet have no end—
thus their form cannot be observed.
The earth is square yet has no boundary—
thus no one can peer into its gate.
Heaven’s transformations flow without shape,
the earth’s growth extends without measure.

Things may have their masters,
but the *Dao* has none—
for it has no fixed form or power.
Its revolutions are endless,
like the sun and moon’s ceaseless motion,
or the seasons’ unending succession—
day and night, light and dark,
end and begin, manifest yet formless,
thus its work is accomplished.
It nurtures all things yet is not bound by them—
thus it conquers without being overcome.

The *Dao* gives birth to heaven and earth,
heaven and earth give birth to humankind,
just as an ancestor begets a father,
and the father begets a child.
Qi and spirit interact to shape myriad forms,
yet the *Dao* remains singular.

The *Dao* is formless, yet the body has form—
he who shapes form is formless,
he who commands things is not bound by them.
How divine!

The *regal warrior* is an emperor,
the *spirit-transformer* is a king.
The *regal warrior* follows the *Dao* of Heaven,
the *spirit-transformer* harmonizes the four seasons,
rectifies order within his lands,
and distant realms embrace his virtue.
He secures victory before battle,
and the feudal lords submit in homage.

The ancient *Dao*-adepts, when still, conformed to heaven and earth,
when active, followed the sun and moon,
their joys and angers aligned with the seasons,
their commands resounded like thunder,
their voice and qi did not clash with the eight winds,
their yielding and firmness did not alter the five tones.

They guided the people’s desires,
harnessed their strength,
and removed their tyrants and calamities.
Those who share benefits die for one another,
those who share grief aid one another,
those who walk the same path assist one another.
Move according to your own nature,
and the world will fight for you.
Thus, the skilled warrior uses what the enemy uses against itself—
the unskilled warrior forces the enemy to serve his own ends.
Using what the enemy uses against itself,
the world cannot resist—
but forcing the enemy to serve your own ends,
no one can be compelled.

When the sun and moon lose their brilliance, there is an eclipse;
when yin and yang lose harmony, there is war.
War brings injury to things.

The *regal warrior* rules with the emperor’s robes—
the world is governed.
The *spirit-transformer* returns his troops in triumph—
the people’s suffering ceases.

If Jie had received proper governance,
the battlefield at Muye would have been silent—
how could he have exploited the people’s desires
and used their strength
while abandoning his own lord?
It is only because the people had all abandoned their hearts
that one does not say *"regicide"* but *"removing tyrants and calamities."*

Alas.

*End of Volume Eight of the Continuation of the True Mystic Classic’s Meaning.*

**Volume Nine of *The Continuation of the True Mystic Classic’s Meaning*
*Southern Valley Master Du Daojian’s Compilation*
*The Chapter on Lower Virtue***

Laozi says:
To govern oneself, the highest is to nurture the spirit, the next is to nurture the form. When the spirit is clear and the mind is tranquil, all limbs are at ease—this is the foundation of life-nurturing.
To plump the flesh and fill the belly, to indulge desires—this is the superficiality of life-nurturing.

To govern the realm, the highest is to nurture the transformation of the people, the next is to rectify the laws. When the people naturally yield to one another, vie for humility, compete to receive less profit, and vie to undertake labor, day by day they transform toward the superior and abandon their old ways without knowing why—this is the foundation of governance.
When rewards are generous and thus virtue is encouraged, when punishments are feared and thus wrongdoing is avoided, when laws are properly established above and the people submit below—this is the superficiality of governance.

In ancient times, the focus was on the foundation; in later times, on the superficial. Lower virtue is the rigid adherence to virtue.

The highest in governing oneself is to nurture the spirit—this is the foundation of self-cultivation. The next is to nurture the form—this is the superficiality of self-cultivation.
The highest in governing the realm is to nurture the transformation of the people—this is the foundation of governance. The next is to rectify the laws—this is the superficiality of governance.
Descending further, there are still those beneath lower virtue.

Laozi says: The ruler who seeks to govern does not appear in every age; the capable minister who can assist him is not found in every ten thousand. To seek such a minister with a ruler who appears so rarely is why perfect governance is not achieved even once in a thousand years.

The achievements of hegemons and kings are not established in every age. If one follows the people’s good intentions and guards against their corrupt hearts, sharing with them the same Dao, then the people can be made virtuous, and customs refined.
What makes the sage noble is not his imposition of punishments for crimes, but his understanding of where disorder arises.
If one opens the sharp inclinations of the people and follows them into licentiousness and excess, then abandons them to the law and punishes them with penalties—even if they ravage the world, their deceit cannot be restrained.

The gathering of wise and virtuous leaders is a chance encounter once in a thousand years.
The sage ruler does not appear in every age; the capable minister is not found in every ten thousand. When a ruler who appears so rarely obtains even one such minister—from the Tang and Yu onward, like Tang’s encounter with Yi Yin or Wen Wang’s with Lü Wang—such instances are rare in history.
Even figures like Guan Zhong under Qi Huan are not common.
Thus, governance flourishes rarely, while disorder prevails often—perhaps because virtuous men are few while the petty are many?

Laozi says: To dwell above the lakes and rivers while one’s heart is beneath the imperial gate—this is to cherish life.
To cherish life is to disregard profit lightly.
Yet if one cannot overcome oneself and instead indulges, the spirit suffers no harm.
If one cannot overcome oneself and yet forcibly resists, this is called severe injury.
Those who suffer severe injury will not live long.
Thus it is said:
To know harmony is to know constancy;
To know constancy is to know clarity;
To benefit life is auspicious;
To let the heart command the qi is strength—
This is called *Xuan Tong* (Mysterious Harmony).
To use its radiance and return to its clarity.
Humans have careers and retirement;
The Dao knows no bending or stretching.
Though one’s body may be in the lakes and rivers while the heart is at the imperial gate, one does not forget the fields.
Thus, those who embody the Dao retire with this Dao, act with this Dao—when the time is to stop, they stop; when the time is to act, they act.
This is called *Xuan Tong*.

Laozi says: Nothing is easier than doing good, nothing harder than doing evil.
What is called easy to do as good is to remain still and do nothing, to follow nature’s excess without enticement, to preserve one’s true self without alteration—thus it is said that doing good is easy.
What is called hard to do as evil is usurpation, regicide, arrogance, and licentiousness—restless and desiring much, contrary to human nature—thus it is said that doing evil is hard.
What is now considered a great calamity arises from the absence of moderation and measure.
Thus, in places of gain and harm, at the juncture of fortune and misfortune, one must be discerning.
The sage has no desires, no avoidance.
If one desires something, it is enough to lose it;
If one avoids something, it is enough to invite it.
When the will desires, one forgets what one is doing.
Thus the sage examines the shifts between action and stillness, adapts to the measure of giving and receiving,
balances the emotions of love and hate,
harmonizes the rhythms of joy and anger.
When action and stillness are appropriate, calamity does not intrude;
When giving and receiving are measured, guilt does not accumulate;
When love and hate are balanced, worry does not approach;
When joy and anger are harmonized, resentment does not arise.
Good and evil differ in appearance but share the same origin in the heart.
Nothing is easier than doing good, nothing harder than doing evil.
The steps to fortune and misfortune are uncertain—what one seeks to gain is lost, what one seeks to avoid is encountered.
Thus the sage examines the shifts between action and stillness,
harmonizes the rhythms of joy and anger,
and in all things does no wrong—what cause is there for resentment?
Those who embody the Dao do not greedily take what they should not,
do not reject what they possess.
They are always full yet never overflowing,
constantly empty yet easily sustained.
Thus, if one aligns with Daoist principles and measures,
food suffices to fill the empty,
clothing suffices to ward off cold,
and the body of seven spans is kept warm and nourished.
Without Daoist principles and measures,
even the power of a great kingdom is insufficient for satisfaction,
and the wealth of the world is insufficient for joy.
Thus the sage maintains a tranquil heart and easy will,
guards the spirit within,
and external things cannot deceive.
There is a method to cultivating oneself and an art to navigating the world.
Those who embody the Dao uphold their natural endowment,
find ease in their fate,
and eat only to fill the empty,
clothe themselves only to ward off cold.
They do not greedily take what they should not,
do not abandon what they possess.
Misfortune cannot be avoided, but fortune is not sought recklessly.
In prosperity, they do not grow arrogant;
in adversity, they do not abandon their principles.
They take joy in the natural state,
and endure with the Dao.

Laozi said:

*"He who overcomes others has strength,
but he who overcomes himself is strong.
To be strong, one must use others’ strength;
to use others’ strength, one must win their hearts;
to win their hearts, one must first be self-mastered.
Never has one gained others while losing oneself,
nor has one lost oneself while gaining others.

Thus, the foundation of governance lies in calming the people;
the foundation of calming the people lies in ensuring their sufficiency;
the foundation of sufficiency lies in not disrupting the natural order;
the foundation of not disrupting the natural order lies in simplifying affairs;
the foundation of simplifying affairs lies in moderating expenditure;
the foundation of moderating expenditure lies in eliminating arrogance;
the foundation of eliminating arrogance lies in embracing emptiness and non-being.

Therefore, those who understand the nature of life do not strive for what cannot be attained;
those who understand the nature of fate do not strive for what cannot be controlled.
The eyes indulge in the five colors,
the mouth craves the five flavors,
the ears revel in the five tones—
the seven orifices vie in conflict,
harming the singular essence.
Daily indulging in perverse desires exhausts one’s natural harmony,
and even the body cannot be governed—
how then can one govern the world?

To conquer others, first conquer oneself.
None can conquer others without first conquering themselves.
He who conquers others relies on force,
but he who conquers himself is strong—
for reason conquers desire.
When reason prevails, one wins the hearts of others,
and people willingly lend their strength.
When desire prevails, one exploits others’ strength,
and people withdraw their loyalty.
The distinction between Heaven’s principle and human desire is profound.

So-called mastery of the world does not mean occupying high rank or bearing a noble title,
but rather aligning with the will of the world and harnessing its strength.
To hold the title of king yet lack the people’s trust is to have lost the world.
Thus, Jie and Zhou were not true kings,
while Tang and Wu were not rebels.

When the Son of Heaven embodies the Dao, his authority rests with the four quarters;
when he loses the Dao, his authority is held by the feudal lords.
When a feudal lord embodies the Dao, his authority extends to the borders;
when he loses the Dao, his authority is confined to his inner circle.
Thus it is said:
Do not rely on others not taking what is yours—
rely instead on making it impossible for them to take it.
To walk the path of what can be seized is not the way of a usurper or regicide,
yet it profits nothing in securing the world.
Petting me brings loyalty; oppressing me breeds enmity.
The sage aligns with the world’s will, harnesses its strength,
and thus the world is governed.
As for tyrants like Jie and Zhou—
though they bear the title of king, they lack even one loyal subject—
this is why Tang and Wu refused to serve them.
When the world loses the Dao, the feudal lords become its guardians—
whose fault is this?

Laozi said:
The wise ruler does not alter what is established,
nor does he abandon what is constant.
Anger defies virtue,
war is a cruel instrument,
and strife is the source of disorder.
To scheme in secrecy defies virtue,
to favor weapons of destruction exacerbates disorder—
this is the height of perverting human chaos.
None can bring ruin to others without first inviting ruin upon themselves.
Do not sharpen the blade to its edge,
do not stoke the flames to their height,
soften the light, blend with the dust.

Human nature longs to excel over others
and resents those who surpass them.
This longing breeds contention,
this resentment breeds strife.
Strife disturbs the mind and disrupts the qi.
Thus, ancient sage-kings eliminated contention and resentment,
and with their absence, the mind was at peace and the qi flowed harmoniously.
Hence it is said:
Do not exalt the virtuous—
so that the people do not contend.
When contention is insufficient, yielding is abundant—
this is the path of mutual benefit for self and others.

Anger defies virtue,
war is a cruel instrument,
and strife is the source of disorder.
To scheme in secrecy defies virtue,
to favor weapons of destruction exacerbates disorder—
this is the height of perverting human chaos.
Wenzhongzi transmitted these words to Fan Li.
When Yue sought to attack Wu, Li cited them in counsel,
but Goujian did not heed them and was defeated at Fuqiao.
Thus, those who seek to harm others only harm themselves.
Would the virtuous act thus?

Laozi said:
To govern things, one does not rely on things to achieve harmony;
to govern harmony, one does not rely on harmony to govern people;
to govern people, one does not rely on people to govern the ruler;
to govern the ruler, one does not rely on the ruler to govern desire;
to govern desire, one does not rely on desire to govern nature;
to govern nature, one does not rely on nature to govern virtue;
to govern virtue, one does not rely on virtue to govern the Dao.
Rooting the Dao in humanity’s nature,
free from corruption, enduring in all things,
one forgets its origin and merges with its true form.
Clothing, food, rites, and customs are not innate to human nature—
they are acquired from without.
Thus, human nature is inherently balanced,
while cravings disrupt it.
Only those who embody the Dao can abandon external influences and return to the self.
With self-awareness, one does not lose sight of things’ true nature;
without it, one acts in confusion and agitation.
To indulge desire is to lose one’s nature—
actions never align with the true path,
governing life leads to self-ruin,
and governing the state sows discord among the people.
Thus, those who do not hear the Dao cannot return to their nature.
Nature and desire are inherently distinct.
All people are thus, but rulers are especially so.

To govern things without relying on things to achieve harmony,
one must first clarify one’s own nature,
then clarify the nature of things.
To clarify the nature of things is to assist in nurturing and harmonizing with Heaven and Earth.
Ancient sage-kings attained this within themselves,
thus their commands were obeyed and prohibitions respected.
In all undertakings, one must first calm the mind and purify the spirit.
When the spirit is clear and the mind is tranquil,
only then can affairs be set aright.
If judgment is clouded by praise or blame,
if the eyes are dazzled by color,
how can affairs be rectified?
Thus, emptiness is prized.

Water, when stirred, creates waves;
qi, when disordered, clouds the mind.
A clouded mind cannot discern what is right,
and waves cannot settle into stillness.
Thus, the sage-kings held fast to the One
to govern the emotions and natures of things.
The One is the most precious—
unmatched in the world.
The sage-king entrusted himself to the formless,
thus commanding the world.

Ancient scholars cultivated for themselves,
establishing the path of self-cultivation
before enacting the path for others."*

The Sage attains all within himself,
thus clarity and luminosity reside in his person—
nothing distant escapes his discernment.
He comprehends the nature of all things,
and his commands flow throughout the world.

Laozi said:
*"Yin and yang mold all things,
all riding upon the One qi to arise.
When above and below lose harmony,
qi ascends in turmoil;
when ruler and subject are discordant,
the five grains fail to ripen.
Spring’s austerity and autumn’s flourishing,
winter’s thunder and summer’s frost—
all are born of perverse qi."*

Between heaven and earth lies a single body;
within the six directions, a single form.
Thus, those clear in their nature
cannot be coerced by heaven and earth;
those precise in their correspondences
cannot be deceived by strange things.

The Sage knows the near to grasp the far,
unifying all differences into oneness.
When qi steams through heaven and earth,
and rites, propriety, shame, and music are not established,
people do not harm or oppress one another—
this arises from their state of undifferentiated obscurity.

When shame and modesty decline in the age’s decay,
when expenditures exceed resources,
when labor is excessive yet sustenance is insufficient,
the people grow impoverished and resentful, spawning strife—
thus, *ren* (virtue) is prized.

When people are coarse and unaligned,
forming cliques and factions,
each advancing their own,
harboring cunning and deceit—
thus, *yi* (righteousness) is prized.

When men and women dwell together without distinction,
thus, *li* (rites) is prized.

When the nature of life and death is abandoned,
and desires press upon one another without restraint,
disharmony ensues—
thus, *yue* (music) is prized.

Therefore, *ren, yi, li, yue* are means to remedy defects,
not the path of comprehensive governance.

Heaven and earth are one body,
the world is one qi.
Yang transforms, yin nurtures,
molding all things,
all riding upon the One qi to arise.

The Sage cherishes and nourishes the people,
viewing them as one family—
thus, the world attains peace.

If expenditures exceed resources,
labor is excessive yet sustenance is insufficient,
the people grow impoverished and resentful, spawning strife—
this is not the path of comprehensive governance.

If one can establish clarity and luminosity throughout the world,
and return the heart to its origin,
then human nature is good.
When human nature is good,
then heaven and earth’s yin and yang enfold it naturally—
thus, resources suffice and the people are ample,
while greedy, vulgar, and contentious hearts cannot arise.
*Ren* and *yi* are not employed,
yet *dao* and *de* are established throughout the world,
and the people are not dazzled by color.

Thus, when *de* declines, only then are *ren* and *yi* adorned;
when harmony is lost, only then is music adjusted;
when rites are excessive, only then is decorum adorned.
Hence, knowing *dao* and *de*,
one then knows that *ren* and *yi* are insufficient to enact;
knowing *ren* and *yi*,
one then knows that *li* and *yue* are insufficient to cultivate.

The Sage, sincere and clear, returns to the origin of nature—
the people revert to goodness.
When human nature is good,
heaven and earth’s yin and yang enfold it naturally,
resources suffice, the people are ample,
greed and vulgarity do not arise,
and strife subsides.
*Ren* and *yi* are not employed,
