yet *dao* and *de* are established throughout the world.

Laozi said:
*"Governance through clarity and stillness—
harmonious and tranquil, yet profound and vast,
authentic and unadorned, serene and unhurried.
Within, it aligns with the Dao;
without, it conforms to righteousness.
Its speech is concise yet follows principle,
its actions persuasive yet accord with human nature,
its heart harmonious yet unfeigned,
its deeds natural yet undecorated.
It does not plot beginnings nor deliberate ends.
At ease, it remains; provoked, it acts.
It permeates heaven and earth,
harmonizes with yin and yang’s essence,
unifies with the four seasons,
shines with the clarity of sun and moon,
and aligns with those who transform with the Dao.
Cunning, deceit, and artifice
are not harbored in the heart.
Thus, heaven covers with *de*,
earth bears with music,
the four seasons never fail their order,
winds and rains do not act tyrannically,
the sun and moon remain clear and radiant,
the five stars follow their courses—
this is the luminosity of clarity and stillness.
When heaven is clear and earth is still,
only then can endurance be sustained.
The Sage governs through clarity and stillness,
imitating heaven and earth.
A clear heart aligns within with the Dao,
a still body conforms without to humanity.
Thus, transformation spreads without leaving home."*

Laozi said:
*"The duties of governing in a well-ordered age are easy to uphold,
its affairs easy to enact,
its rites easy to practice,
its responsibilities easy to fulfill.
Thus, one person does not hold multiple offices,
and one office does not oversee multiple tasks.
Farmers, scholars, artisans, and merchants
are distinguished by their districts and states.
Thus, farmers speak to farmers of storage,
scholars to scholars of conduct,
artisans to artisans of skill,
merchants to merchants of calculation—
so scholars lack no virtue in conduct,
artisans no toil in craft,
farmers no neglect in labor,
merchants no loss in trade.
Each finds ease in their nature.
Though forms and kinds differ,
their tasks are simple yet not contradictory.
If one’s calling is lost, they are held in contempt;
if one’s aspirations are fulfilled, they are honored.
Anciently, people held a single office,
and each office managed a single task—
thus, the ministers of a well-ordered age
found their duties easy to uphold,
their tasks easy to enact.
In later ages, the practice of holding multiple offices and shared duties arose.
Officials multiplied, records proliferated,
policies issued from many doors—
contrary measures undermined governance.
Those with foresight and distant vision
possess great talent,
yet in a well-ordered age, such abilities are not demanded of the people.
Those well-versed in literature and resolute in purpose,
eloquent and articulate,
possess abundant knowledge,
yet an enlightened ruler does not seek them from below.
Those who disdain the age and devalue things,
not conforming to convention,
exemplify scholarly integrity—
yet in a well-ordered age, such conduct is not used to transform the people.
Thus, the unattainable is not set as a standard for people,
and the unapproachable is not made the custom of the state.
Hence, talent cannot be monopolized by measure,
but Dao and technique can be transmitted through the ages.
Thus, national laws can be entrusted to the unlearned,
and military campaigns can be conducted by uniform methods.
Without awaiting ancient sages, people suffice as they are—
utilizing what they possess and combining their strengths.

The laws of the decadent age
set lofty standards yet fail to reach them,
impose heavy burdens yet cannot bear them,
demand the impossible yet dare not punish.
The people, burdened by triple responsibilities,
adorn their cunning to deceive above,
commit crimes and court danger—
though laws are severe and punishments harsh,
they cannot suppress their deceit.
When beasts are cornered, they strike;
when birds are cornered, they peck;
when people are cornered, they deceive—
this is what is meant."*

「Thus, talent cannot surpass virtue, nor words outdo deeds. Those with abundant talent yet prescient insight are not held accountable in times of governance; those with vast learning yet eloquent deceit are not sought by enlightened rulers.

The proud and self-righteous, who burden others with impossible tasks and demand what they themselves cannot perform, how could they avoid the deceit of desperate people? Laozi says:

The thunderous voice of *léitíng* can be likened to bells and drums;
the shifts of wind and rain can be known through music and rhythm.

What is grand and visible can be measured;
what is bright and discernible can be obscured;
what is audible can be tuned;
what is colored can be distinguished.

Yet the utmost grandeur of heaven and earth cannot be contained,
and the subtlest essence of *shénmíng* cannot be fully grasped.

When establishing calendars and measures,
distinguishing the five colors,
discriminating clear and turbid,
tasting sweet and bitter—
then the *pǔ* scatters and becomes tools.

When establishing *rényì* and cultivating rites and music,
then virtue shifts and becomes artifice.

The people adorn their cleverness to deceive the simple,
employ deceit to attack their superiors—
yet though some may uphold it, none can govern through it.

For when wisdom grows ever more intricate while virtue decays,
the truly great remain pure and unadorned, yet do not scatter.

The Sage carves from the *Dàpǔ* (Great Purity),
aligning with heaven and earth,
observing the patterns to craft tools—
as thunder and lightning are to bells and drums,
as wind and rain are to music and rhythm,
all are shaped from their likeness.

Yet as vast as heaven and earth,
as subtle as *shénmíng*,
there are limits to what can be fully explored.

Only the truly great remain pure and unadorned,
yet can serve as teachers for all ages.
Laozi says:

The governance of the truly great is empty and tranquil,
without visible desire,
as *shén* (spirit) and *xìng* (nature) align,
and form harmonizes with *qì* (vitality).
In stillness, the body attains;
in motion, the *lǐ* (principles) flow—
following the Dao of natural spontaneity,
as circumstances demand.

Indifferent and without contrivance, yet the world is in accord;
detached and without desire, yet the people return to simplicity.
No quarreling, yet resources suffice.

The giver does not claim virtue,
the receiver does not refuse—
virtue returns naturally,
and none can hinder it.

Debate without words,
the Way without teaching—
if one attains this,
it is called *Tiānfǔ* (Heaven’s Treasury).
Taking from it without depletion,
drawing from it without exhaustion—
none know its source,
this is called *Yáoguāng* (Shaking Light).
*Yáoguāng* is the nourishment of all things.

The *Běichén* (North Star) is the exalted sovereign of heaven.
Firmly seated at the center of the celestial sphere,
all stars bow before it.

The truly great take the Way as their model,
as *shén* and *xìng* align,
indifferent and without contrivance,
yet the world is in accord.

*Yáoguāng* resides in the ladle of the Big Dipper,
nourishing all things,
revolving leftward with the heavens,
as the sun completes its daily cycle.

When the Dipper does not descend its blessings,
the people are sustained in their sustenance.
Shun observed the *Xuánjī* (the rotating stars) to regulate the seven celestial officials—
was not his understanding of heaven profound?

Laozi says:

Heaven cherishes its *jīng* (refined essences),
earth its *píng* (levelness),
humans their *qíng* (affections).

The *jīng* of heaven are the sun, moon, stars,
thunder, lightning, wind, and rain.
The *píng* of earth are water, fire, metal, wood, and soil.
The *qíng* of humans are thought, perception, cleverness, and the emotions of joy and anger.

Thus, closing the four gates,
rectifying the five pathways#12,
one aligns with the Dao.

*Shénmíng* is hidden in the formless,
*jīngqì* returns to its true essence.
The eyes are bright yet do not gaze,
the ears are keen yet do not listen,
the speech is apt yet does not speak,
the mind is lucid yet does not deliberate—
abandoning yet not acting,
knowing yet not boasting,
following the natural inclinations of life,
yet understanding that harm is inevitable.

When *jīng* resides in the eyes, their vision is clear;
when it lingers in the ears, their hearing is acute;
when it stays in the mouth, their speech is precise;
when it gathers in the heart, their thoughts are profound.

Thus, closing the four gates,
one remains free from harm all their life—
the four limbs and nine orifices neither die nor live,
this is called the *Zhēnrén* (True Person).

The earth’s production of wealth
has no greater foundation than the five phases.
The Sage regulates the five phases,
and governance remains unerring.

Humans possess five *xìng* (natures) corresponding to heaven,
and five stars corresponding to earth.
When the five phases and five natures move,
the seven emotions arise—
how could one not love their body?

The spirit dwells in the heart,
the refined essence in the kidneys,
the *hún* (ethereal soul) in the liver,
the *pò* (corporeal soul) in the lungs,
the intention in the spleen,
and *shénmíng* in the formless.

Neither giving life nor ceasing it,
this is called the *Zhēnrén* (True Person).

Laozi says:

The balance between left and right
has no partiality in lightness or weight,
thus it can serve as *píng* (level).

The plumb line within and without
has no partiality in crookedness or straightness,
thus it can serve as *zhèng* (rectitude).

The ruler’s laws
have no partiality in favor or dislike,
thus they can serve as *lìng* (command).

Virtue has no fixed foundation,
grudges have no place to hide—
this is to follow the Dao and unite human hearts.

Thus, those who govern
know not to interfere.

Water overwhelms and shatters the boat,
wood strikes and snaps the axle—
yet they do not blame water and stone,
but punish the clumsy and the unskilled—
this is not wisdom.

Hence, when the Dao has wisdom, it brings chaos;
when virtue has intent, it becomes perilous;
when the heart has eyes, it grows dizzy.

The *quánhéng guījǔ* (weights and measures, square and compass)
are fixed and unchanging,
ever consistent and never deviating,
ever straight and never lingering.

Once shaped, they endure for ten thousand ages—
this is the Way of non-action.

Humans have partial inclinations,
and none can avoid harming the Dao.

The ruler without partiality,
thus the law is singular and commands are obeyed.

Hence, when virtue has intent, it becomes perilous;
when the heart has eyes, it grows dizzy.

Knowing that *quánhéng guījǔ* are fixed and unchanging,
then one knows that the singular is the Way of non-action.

A hundred rulers employed it,
ten thousand ages transmitted it.

Laozi says:

People say:
"A kingdom has a doomed sovereign,
but the world lacks a doomed Dao."

Humans may be desperate,
yet the principles of the Dao remain unbroken.
Thus, those who follow non-action,
are the essence of the Dao.

Attaining the essence of the Dao,
one responds to all things without end.
Hence, they do not rely on the Dao’s myriad principles,
nor monopolize their own abilities—
their limits are not distant.

The sovereign does not step outside their door
yet knows the world—
by observing things to discern things,
and by observing people to know people.»

Thus, when one accumulates the strength of what can be raised,
there is nothing it cannot overcome.
When the wisdom of the multitude is employed,
there is nothing it cannot accomplish.
A thousand-strong force lacks no provisions,
a ten-thousand-strong host lacks no function.

Craftsmen have no divergent skills,
scholars hold no dual offices,
each guards their station,
and none may overstep—
people find their proper place,
things find their proper rest.
Thus, tools are not ill-used,
and duties are not neglected.
Debts are few and easily repaid,
duties are few and easily upheld.
Burdens are light and easily encouraged.
The sovereign governs with minimal measures,
while the people achieve great deeds,
so ruler and subjects do not weary of one another.

Heaven does not govern itself—there are those who govern as Heaven,
Earth does not govern itself—there are those who govern as Earth.
Who are these "those"?
There are doomed sovereigns, but no doomed Dao.

Yī Yǐn five times approached Jié but was not used,
for he relied on his own abilities
and did not know the Way of aligning with others.
Thus, Yī Yǐn did not serve Xià but served Tāng instead.
Could Xià not have perished?
Could Tāng not have risen?

Laozi says:
The *Emperor* embodies the *Tàiyī* [Ultimate One],
the *King* follows the *Yīnyáng* [yin-yang],
the *Conqueror* aligns with the *Four Seasons*,
the *Lord* applies the *Six Lü* [musical pitches].

To embody the *Tàiyī* is to illuminate the nature of Heaven and Earth,
to harmonize the relationships of virtue and the Dao.
To be as luminous as the sun and moon,
to resonate with all things,
to balance movement and stillness with yin and yang,
to temper joy and anger with the Four Seasons—
covering all things with the Dao,
bestowing rewards without favor;
even the smallest creatures,
from the flying moth to the crawling worm,
all look up to virtue and thrive.
Virtue flows beyond borders,
and fame endures through the ages.

To follow *Yīnyáng* is to inherit the harmony of Heaven and Earth,
to share brilliance with Heaven and Earth,
to shine as brightly as the sun and moon,
to possess spirits as divine as ghosts and gods.
To wear the round and tread the square,
to embrace the plumb line and rest on the level,
to govern oneself internally,
to win hearts externally,
to issue commands—
and the world follows the wind.

To align with the *Four Seasons* is to let
spring generate, summer grow,
autumn harvest, winter store—
to give and take with moderation,
to enter and exit with measure,
to temper joy and anger, firmness and gentleness,
without straying from principle.
Gentle yet not fragile,
firm yet not harsh,
broad yet not reckless,
solemn yet not perverse—
to nurture the people with yielding and compliance,
to embrace the foolish and contain the unworthy,
without partial affection.

To apply the *Six Lü* is to know
when to give life and when to take it,
when to reward and when to punish,
when to give and when to take—
for without these, there is no Dao.
To suppress chaos, restrain violence,
to promote the virtuous and discard the unworthy,
to rectify and contain for order,
to repel danger and level the uneven,
to correct the crooked and establish the straight,
to illuminate the principles of opening and closing—
to ride the times and harness the people’s desires,
thus subduing hearts.

The *Emperor* who does not embody *Yīnyáng* will be encroached upon;
the *King* who does not follow the *Four Seasons* will be diminished;
the *Conqueror* who does not apply the *Six Lü* will be humiliated;
the *Lord* who loses the plumb line will be deposed.

Thus, the small acting as the great
remains unobstructed even in narrowness;
the great acting as the small
becomes confined even in vastness.

Why not call them *Huáng* [August] but instead *Emperor*, *King*, *Conqueror*, *Lord*?
It is to honor the *Huáng* [August].

The *Emperor* loses the Dao and only then virtue,
the *King* loses virtue and only then benevolence,
the *Conqueror* loses benevolence and only then righteousness,
the *Lord* loses righteousness and only then ritual.
When the great stands, the small is present;
when the small stands, the great is lost.

Laozi says:
Great territory and many people are not enough for strength;
strong armor and sharp weapons cannot guarantee victory;
high walls and deep moats are not enough for defense;
harsh punishments and severe laws are not enough for awe.

For those who govern with virtue,
even smallness ensures survival;
for those who govern without virtue,
even greatness ensures ruin.

Thus, the best defenders need no resistance,
the best warriors need no battle—
they ride the times and follow the people’s desires,
and take the world.

Therefore, those skilled in governance accumulate *virtue*;
those skilled in war cultivate *anger*.
When *virtue* is accumulated, the people can be used;
when *anger* is cultivated, awe can be established.

Where *culture* reaches deeply, *authority* is widely obeyed;
where *virtue* is broadly applied, *awe* extends far.
The broader it is, the stronger we become while the enemy weakens.

Those skilled in war first weaken the enemy, then fight,
so their expenditures are halved while their gains are tenfold.

A thousand-chariot state that practices *cultural virtue* will reign;
a ten-thousand-chariot state that favors war will perish.

The victorious army wins before battle,
the defeated army fights before seeking victory—
this is ignorance of the Dao.

Those who practice *cultural virtue*, though small, endure;
those who favor war, though great, perish.

Indeed, those skilled in governance accumulate *virtue*,
those skilled in war cultivate *anger*.
Only those who know war’s origin use it to stop chaos,
not to create it—
thus the people are unharmed and the state endures long.

According to the *Jūzhēn* edition, deletions made.

**#14** *Accumulate*: Original reads *"immediately"*, revised per *Jūzhēn*.
**#15** *Its*: Original reads *"has"*, revised per *Jūzhēn*.

---
**From *Tōng Xuān Zhēn Jīng* (Continuation of the *True Scripture of the Mystic Dao*), Volume Ten**
**Southern Valley Master Du Dàojiān’s Compilation**
**The Chapter on Supreme Benevolence**

**Laozi says:**
The Dao of the noble one is to cultivate stillness to refine the self, and frugality to nourish life.
Stillness prevents the lower ranks from disturbance; frugality prevents the people from grievance.
When the lower ranks are disturbed, governance falters; when the people grieve, virtue wanes.
When governance falters, the worthy do not offer counsel;
when virtue wanes, the brave do not fight.

But a chaotic ruler does otherwise:
In a single day, he possesses the wealth of the realm,
dwelling in the lord’s authority, exhausting the people’s strength
to indulge the desires of his ears and eyes.
His ambitions fix on palaces and pavilions, moats and gardens, fierce beasts and rare curiosities.
The poor suffer hunger, while tigers and wolves gorge on fodder;
the people freeze, yet the lord’s halls are draped in embroidered silks.
Thus, when the ruler hoards these useless things,
the realm’s people lose their natural harmony.

The supreme benevolent one practices stillness to refine the self,
frugality to nourish the people—
this is what the noble one must do.
Disturbance below leads to chaotic governance;
the people’s grievance thins virtue—
the noble one does not act thus.

How could one willingly harm what is beneficial to cultivate what is useless,
hoard what is without use to corrupt what is useful?

Laozi says:
Without simplicity, virtue cannot be illuminated;
without tranquility, the distant cannot be attained;
without magnanimity, judgments cannot be decisive.

With the eyes of the realm to see, the ears of the realm to hear,
the wisdom of the realm to deliberate, the strength of the realm to contend—
thus commands descend to their full extent,
while the sincerity of ministers reaches the sovereign’s ear.
Officials act with precision, and ministers gather like spokes to a wheel.

He does not reward with gifts for joy,
nor punish with crimes for anger.
Laws are enforced without cruelty;
ears and eyes are open without deception.
Good and ill are laid bare daily without obstruction.
Thus the worthy exhaust their wisdom,
the unworthy exert their strength,
the near find security in their nature,
the distant are drawn by virtue—
this is the Dao of employing people.

Simplicity, tranquility, magnanimity, and fairness—
these are the affairs of benevolent governance.

The Dao of employing people:
If one sees and hears with the eyes and ears of the realm,
then clarity and insight are vast;
if one deliberates and contends with the wisdom and strength of the realm,
then achievements are great.
Thus the worthy exhaust their wisdom,
the foolish expend their strength,
the near are bound by favor,
the distant submit to virtue—
this is why the Three Dynasties endured,
while later ages could not match them.

He who rides in carriages and horses
travels a thousand *li* without labor;
he who sails in boats and junks
crosses rivers and seas without exertion.
If words are true, even a commoner with a load of firewood
should not be discarded;
if words are false, even a sovereign or minister
should not be employed.
Right and wrong cannot be judged by rank or status.

If a plan is useful, one does not shame its position;
if speech is practicable, one does not prize its eloquence.

But a dark ruler does otherwise:
Few ministers offer sincere loyalty,
for their persons are not employed.
Instead, he favors the corrupt and crooked;
the worthy cannot be seen,
while the distant and lowly, though they exhaust their strength and loyalty,
cannot be heard.

If one speaks, he is trapped by words;
if one remonstrates, he is punished by law.
Yet such a one seeks to pacify the realm and preserve the world—
how far he strays from wisdom!

The Creator lends me existence; if taken, I have none.
To borrow is to wield great power indeed.

Carriages and horses replace footsteps,
boats and junks cross rivers—
thousand-*li* journeys reached without labor,
for their strength is borrowed.
The sovereign borrows ministers to govern,
ministers borrow the sovereign to fulfill their ambitions.
To lose the use of borrowing is to be a solitary tyrant.
Only the enlightened sovereign can attain this.

Laozi says:
Those who honor life
do not, though rich and noble, let nourishment harm the body,
nor, though poor and lowly, let profit burden the form.

Now, having inherited the noble rank of our ancestors,
we must cherish it—
for life’s origin is long,
yet to lose it lightly is bewildering indeed.

Thus, if one governs the realm with the body’s virtue,
one can entrust the realm to it;
if one loves the realm with the body’s virtue,
one can then #2*deposit* the realm with it.

No one values life more than to honor it.
To honor life is to avoid desires that kill the self.

Men receive their ancestors’ blessings yet fail to guard themselves,
seeking from their sustenance what instead brings ruin to their bodies and families—
how much deeper their confusion grows!

**Wénzǐ asked:** What is the root of governing the state?
Laozi said: The root lies in governing the self.
Never has it been heard that a disordered self brings order to the state,
or that a disordered state brings order to the self.
Thus it is said:
Refine the self, and its virtue becomes genuine.
The Dao’s utmost subtlety:
A father cannot teach it to his son,
nor a son receive it from his father.
Thus, the Dao that can be spoken is not the constant Dao;
the name that can be named is not the constant name.

The state’s root is the family;
the family’s root is the self.
**Wénzǐ asked** about the foundation of harmonizing the state,
and Laozi spoke of the root being in governing the self—
thus, when the self is governed, the family is governed;
when the family is governed, the state is governed.

The self is like the state;
the state is like the self.
The *Shījīng* says:
*"He who wields the axe to fell the axe—
his model is not distant."*

**Wénzǐ asked:** What deed makes the people love their ruler?
Laozi said:
Rule them in due season, and treat them with reverence and caution,
as if standing at the edge of a precipice,
as if walking on thin ice.
Between heaven and earth,
the good are what I nurture,
the wicked are what I oppose.

In ancient times, the ministers of Shang and Xia
turned against their tyrants, Jié and Zhòu,
and served Tāng and Wǔ instead;
the people of Hòushā
attacked their own lord
and submitted to Shénnóng’s rule.
Thus it is said:
What the people fear, one must also fear.

Shùn’s virtuous deeds made the realm emulate him.
**Wénzǐ asked** what deed makes the people love their ruler,
and Laozi spoke of ruling them in due season,
treating them with reverence and caution—
the good, I nurture; the wicked, I oppose.

Then it follows that if a ruler treats his ministers like dogs and horses,
his ministers will treat him as an enemy.

The people can both uphold their ruler and overthrow him—
this is what makes him worthy of fear.

Laozi said:
To govern the great, the Dao cannot be trifled with;
to rule vast lands, measures cannot be narrow;
to hold high office, duties cannot be burdensome;
to lead many people, instruction cannot be harsh.

When duties are burdensome, they become difficult to manage;
when laws are harsh, they become difficult to enforce;
when demands are excessive, they become difficult to fulfill.

Measure an inch, and by the time you reach a *zhàng*,
the error will be great;
weigh a *shú* of grain, and by the time you reach a *dàn*,
the excess will be heavy.

But if you weigh a *dàn* and measure a *zhàng*,
the errors will be few.

What is broadly measured is easier to govern wisely,
but what is minutely debated is difficult to make wise.

Thus, what brings no benefit to governance but fosters chaos,
the Sage does not do;
what serves no purpose but wastes resources,
the wise do not practice.

Therefore, deeds should not be excessive in their demands,
matters should not be overcomplicated,
demands should not be too many.

When deeds are concise, they are easily accomplished;
when matters are simplified, they are easily governed;
when demands are few, they are easily fulfilled.
When entrusted to the people, this is effortless.

Thus, petty debates harm principle,
petty principles undermine the Dao.
The Dao, when made small, cannot prevail;
to prevail, it must be simple.

Measurements have their breadth and narrowness,
wisdom has its depth and shallowness.
To rule vast lands and many people,
shallow wisdom and narrow measures are insufficient.

How much more so for one in high office governing great affairs—
could they be entangled in trivialities and laziness?

Petty debates harm principle,
petty principles harm the Dao—
this is the business of the petty man;
the noble does not engage in it.

The river flows far because it meanders,
the mountain stands high because it rises gradually,
the Dao transforms because it moves with ease.

To master one skill, to be precise in one matter,
to excel in one ability—
one may be eloquent in detail,
but cannot broadly respond.

The harmonizer of sounds tightens the small strings
and loosens the large ones;
the organizer of affairs burdens the lowly
and grants ease to the noble.

The words of the Dao say:
Vague and indistinct, aligning with Heaven’s might,
those who share its *qi* become Emperors,
those who share its virtue become Kings,
those who share its achievements become Conquerors,
those lacking in all three perish.

Thus, without speaking, they inspire trust;
without bestowing, they inspire benevolence;
without anger, they inspire awe—
this is how they move Heaven’s heart.

To bestow and yet lack benevolence,
to speak and yet lack trust,
to anger and yet lack awe—
this is to act through mere appearance.

Therefore, with the Dao to govern,
even few laws suffice to rule;
without the Dao to govern,
even many laws suffice only to bring chaos.

The small vessel fills quickly and cannot endure long;
the great vessel matures late but attains lasting life.

Those who share *qi* with Heaven become Emperors,
those who share virtue become Kings,
those who share achievements become Conquerors,
those lacking in all three perish.

Thus, when the Dao governs affairs,
laws are few yet rule is easy;
when the Dao does not govern,
laws are many yet chaos ensues.

Laozi says:
A whale out of water is dominated by ants;
a ruler who abandons his proper role and contests matters with his ministers is dominated by his officials.

If a ruler relies on his own ability and despises those who guard their posts—
listening only to flatterers while hiding the wise—
and seizes authority for himself,
his intelligence grows constrained while his burdens increase.

When schemes fail below, he cannot enforce justice;
when conduct falters in office, he cannot maintain control.
Lacking wisdom to govern, lacking authority to punish,
he cannot properly engage with the realm.

Joy and anger show on his face,
desires reveal themselves outwardly—
then those who guard their posts abandon principle to curry favor,
and officials pervert the law to follow the wind.
Rewards do not match deeds, punishments do not fit crimes—
thus superiors and inferiors grow estranged, and ruler and minister harbor resentment.

Officials grow chaotic, yet wisdom cannot resolve it;
praise and blame arise, yet clarity cannot illuminate.
When faults are not his own yet he blames himself,
the ruler grows ever more exhausted while ministers grow ever more idle.

This is like a substitute carpenter—
few who replace the master carpenter do not injure their hands.

When a ruler loses the Dao, he is controlled by his ministers,
just as a whale out of water is dominated by ants.

The Way of the ruler lies in appointing the worthy and selecting ministers.
If ministers are chosen well, all officials will be upright,
and the realm will be well-governed.

But if he abandons his proper role and contests ability with his ministers,
before they can dominate him, he will already be ensnared.

Chasing horses in a race, his sinews snap before he can catch up—
once mounted, he takes the reins, and the horse obeys below.
Berlek selects the horse, Wang Liang drives it,
and the wise ruler rides it—
without effort in guiding or selecting, yet reaching a thousand *li*.
This is the art of utilizing others’ strengths.

The ruler’s Way is to act without doing,
to establish without preferring.
To act is to invite criticism;
to prefer is to invite flattery.
Criticism can be seized against him,
flattery can be used to deceive him.
He who relies on his own strength to rule cannot govern the state.

Thus, the skilled ruler does not act visibly—
his influence is formless.
Only those who embody *shen* transformation cannot be overcome.

When inner desires are restrained, it is called *jiān* (bolting);
when external evils are blocked, it is called *bì* (closing).
With inner bolting and outer closing,
what matter cannot be regulated?
With outer closing and inner bolting,
what matter cannot succeed?

Thus, without acting yet achieving,
without doing yet accomplishing—
speaking without boasting,
acting without usurping,
following names to demand reality,
letting each official attend to his duties,
taking "not knowing" as the Dao,
and making restraint the sovereign’s principle—
then all official matters will have proper measures.

To chase horses in a race is for the ruler to contend with ministers in strength;
to mount the carriage and travel far is to entrust ministers with governance.
To act is to invite debate;
to prefer is to invite flattery—
only those who embody *shen* transformation can regulate all affairs,
prevent private audiences, and ensure officials fulfill their duties with matters properly measured.

Laozi says:
Food is the people’s foundation;
the people are the state’s base.
Thus, the ruler harmonizes with Heaven’s timing,
exhausts Earth’s resources,
and employs human effort.
Therefore, all living things flourish,
and ten thousand things grow abundant.

In spring, he cuts the withered;
in summer, he harvests the fruits;
in autumn, he stores vegetables and grains;
in winter, he gathers firewood and steam.
He provides for the people—
in life, no want; in death, no abandoned corpses.

The ancient kings’ laws forbade:
hunting in groups without proper tools,
draining ponds to fish,
burning forests to hunt.
Before the jackal has sacrificed to the beasts,
no snares may traverse the wilds;
before the otter has sacrificed to the fish,
no nets may enter the waters;
before the hawk and falcon have struck,
no fowling nets may be set in the valleys;
before the grasses and trees have fallen,
no axes may enter the forests;
before insects have nested,
no fires may be set in the fields;
pregnant creatures must not be killed,
nor eggs taken from nests;
fish under a *chi* in length may not be taken,
pigs and dogs under a year may not be eaten.

Thus, all things thrive as if steamed.
This is how the ancient kings adapted to the times,
prepared thoroughly,
enriched the state,
and benefited the people.

It is not that they saw with their eyes and acted with their feet—
if benefiting the people is never forgotten in the heart,
then the people will naturally prepare themselves.

To enrich the state is to nourish the people.
The people are the foundation of life.
Harmonizing with Heaven’s timing,
exhausting Earth’s resources,
employing human effort—
the three *cai* are complete,
then all living things flourish,
ten thousand things grow abundant,
the people rely on them for sustenance,
and the state gains its wealth.

Is this not the great Way of generating wealth?

Laozi says:
In ancient times, wise rulers
took from the people with moderation,
nourished themselves with restraint,
and calculated yearly harvests,
measured the people’s stored wealth,
and knew the numbers of surplus and deficit—
then they took only what was sufficient.
Thus, they received what Heaven and Earth bestowed
and avoided hunger and cold.

To grieve with the people—
when the state has the hungry, food lacks variety;
when the people have the cold, winter lacks fur-lined robes.
Sharing their joys and sorrows,
the realm has no rebellious subjects.

But a tyrannical ruler does otherwise:
he takes from the people without regard for their strength,
demands from below without measuring their stores.
Men and women cannot tend to farming and weaving to fulfill his demands.
Exhausted in labor, depleted in wealth,
they have mornings but no evenings,
and ruler and minister grow mutually resentful.

Moreover, for a person’s livelihood,
one plowman’s labor does not increase ten *mu* of land,
and even in middle-quality fields, the yield does not exceed four *dan*.
Wives, children, the elderly, and weak depend on it for food.

The Way of governance unfolds thus:

When calamities strike and the people cannot fulfill his demands,
then the ruler pities them.
But the greedy and cruel tyrant drains his subjects like a fisherman who dries the pond,
to sate his insatiable desires—
then the common folk suffer neither the harmony of Heaven nor the virtue of Earth.

Emperor Yao ruled with compassion for his people as though they were his own,
taking from them with moderation and giving with measure—
thus no one bore ill will toward him,
and even the humblest could be ennobled.

For a wise ruler’s governance,
he must first assess the year’s abundance or scarcity,
and gauge the people’s strength or depletion—
then take accordingly.
If he serves the people without resentment,
Heaven itself will not reproach him.

The Master Lao said:
The *qi* of Heaven and Earth is nothing greater than harmony.
Harmony means the balance of yin and yang,
the division of day and night.
Thus all things sprout at the Spring Equinox and mature at the Autumn Equinox—
their growth and completion depend on the essence of harmony.

Excessive yin does not generate,
excessive yang does not transform—
only when yin and yang intermingle
does harmony emerge.
Hence the Sage’s Way is broad yet restrained,
stern yet warm,
soft yet upright,
fierce yet benevolent.

Too rigid, it breaks;
too yielding, it coils—
the true Way lies between hardness and softness.

The rule of the *guī* (plumb line) is thus:
It can be coiled and held,
unwound and stretched,
laid straight yet not rigid—
long without bending, short without exhaustion,
straight without harshness.
Therefore the Sage embodies it.

When benevolence is extended, it becomes easy—
easy without authority;
when severity is extended, it becomes fierce—
fierce without harmony;
when love is extended, it becomes indulgent—
indulgent without order;
when punishment is extended, it becomes ruinous—
ruinous without distinction.
Thus harmony is most prized.

*Qi* thrives when balanced—
Heaven and Earth in harmony, and all phenomena manifest;
yin and yang in harmony, and all things flourish;
ruler and minister in harmony, and the court is governed;
father and son in harmony, and the household prospers;
superior and subordinate in harmony, and human affairs succeed;
the *róng* (nourishing) and *wèi* (defensive) *qi* in harmony, and the body is at ease.

The meaning of harmony is vast indeed!

The Master Lao said:
The reason a state endures is because it upholds the Way;
the reason it perishes is because it obstructs *lǐ* (principle).
Thus the Sage observes transformations to discern its essence.

Virtue waxes and wanes,
the winds stir its first signs.
He who upholds the Way, though humble, will prevail;
he who shows signs of ruin, though powerful, will fall.
A state’s destruction is not due to its vastness,
the Way’s success is not due to its smallness.
Thus endurance lies in upholding the Way, not in vastness,
and ruin lies in abandoning the Way, not in smallness.

Hence rulers of chaotic states
pursue only territory and wealth,
not virtue and righteousness,
seek only high rank,
not the Way and moral power.
They abandon what sustains them
and create what destroys them.

If the ruler above disrupts the radiance of the Three Lights,
and the people below lose faith in their sovereign—
who can endure?

Therefore the prudent ruler
need not prepare against all men.

The Way that pervades the world is *Dao*,
the principle that never alters is *lǐ*.
Thus the Way and principle are the greatest.

From ancient times to now, states that uphold the Way flourish,
those that lose principle perish.
To strive for high rank and vast territory
without cultivating virtue and righteousness
is like a tree without roots—
its withering is inevitable.

In ancient times, rulers who deeply practiced the Way were called virtuous and righteous,
those who practiced it shallowly were called benevolent and righteous,
and those who practiced it superficially were called observant of rites and wise.
These six—virtue, righteousness, benevolence, rites, wisdom, and the Way—
are the cords that bind the state.

To practice them deeply brings abundant blessings,
to practice them shallowly brings meager blessings,
to practice them fully brings universal submission.

In ancient times, cultivating virtue and righteousness governed the world,
cultivating benevolence and righteousness governed a state,
and cultivating rites and wisdom governed a village.
The deeper the virtue, the greater the power;
the shallower the virtue, the lesser the power.
Thus the Way does not rely on strength or might,
nor on cunning or competition,
nor on greed or rivalry for attainment.

Its foundation lies in the world embracing you,
its victory in the world submitting to you,
its attainment in the world giving itself to you—
not in seizing it for yourself.

Hence yielding becomes strength,
softness becomes victory,
benevolence and righteousness bring true gain,
and non-contention means none can contend with you.
Thus the Way in the world
is like the rivers and seas.

Virtue, righteousness, rites, and wisdom—
rooted in the heart—
when one man practices them, there are degrees of depth and breadth,
and their application varies in weight.
Names arise from reality,
and the six are thus distinguished.

Yet though they differ,
the same goodness prevails—
when the superior is good, the inferior dare not fail to be.

Later ages neglected the cultivation of virtue and righteousness,
relying instead on coercive power to govern—
and thus their states grew perilous.

The Way of Heaven is natural;
artifice brings its loss.
Reputation is the guest of substance—
no greater name than that of a ruler.
If a ruler has virtue, his name requires no seeking,
for the world bestows it upon him.

To receive it is to embrace it.
What the world embraces is virtue.
Thus it is said:
The virtuous ruler, the world embraces;
the benevolent ruler, the land embraces;
the righteous ruler, the state embraces;
the observant of rites, the village embraces.
Without these four, the people will not embrace.
If they do not embrace, then war is used—
and war is the perilous Way.

Thus it is said:
War is an inauspicious instrument—
used only when there is no other choice.
When used, it slays and wounds men;
when victorious, do not revel in it.
Thus it is said:
In the land of death, thorns and brambles grow—
there one weeps in sorrow,
and there one dwells in mourning rites.
Hence the noble pursues virtue and righteousness,
and does not rely on heavy arms.

The Way of Heaven is natural;
artifice brings its loss.
Reputation is the guest of substance—
no greater name than that of a ruler.
If a ruler has virtue, his name requires no seeking,
for the world bestows it upon him.

To receive it is to embrace it.
This is why the Two Sovereigns and Three Kings excelled,
and why the Five Hegemons and Seven Heroes declined.
To abandon virtue and rely on arms
is to stray far from the Way of Heaven.

Wénzǐ asked:
Why are benevolence and righteousness inferior to virtue and the Way?

For those who act with righteousness, they must clarify it through giving and taking.
Within the Four Seas, sorrow and joy cannot be universal—
exhausting the treasuries’ wealth and goods is insufficient to provide for all the people.
Thus, it is known that wisdom is inferior to cultivating the Dao and practicing virtue,
following the natural disposition of Heaven and Earth,
so that all things spontaneously align,
and the world is abundantly nourished—
benevolence and righteousness then arise naturally.

Hence, the noble man dwells in substance, not in superficiality.

Ritual is the adornment of substance;
benevolence is the manifestation of kindness.
Thus, ritual conforms to human nature without exceeding its limits,
and benevolence does not exceed kindness—
sorrow and joy arise from genuine emotion,
and honoring the dead aligns with benevolence.

Wénzǐ asked how benevolence, righteousness, and ritual are inferior to virtue and the Dao.
Lǎozǐ replied:
For the benevolent, sorrow and joy must be the measure;
for the righteous, giving and taking must be clarified.

Sorrow and joy, giving and taking—
these involve artificial action.
Among the people of the world, those worthy of sorrow are many,
those worthy of joy are few;
the wealth of the treasuries, when taken, is limited,
when given, easily exhausted.
Virtue and the Dao require no artificial action,
trusting in the spontaneous alignment of all things,
and thus the world is abundantly nourished.

Nourishing life does not force what people cannot achieve,
nor does it cut off what they cannot endure.
Moderation in measure ensures nothing is lost—
then praise and blame have no cause to arise.

Thus, music is sufficient to harmonize joy,
without exceeding harmony,
clarifying the distinction between life and death,
and understanding the balance between extravagance and frugality.

But in the later ages, it is not so:
words and deeds contradict each other,
emotions and appearances oppose each other,
ritual becomes burdensome with excess,
music becomes chaotic with indulgence,
customs grow corrupt in the world,
praise and blame converge at court—
thus, the sage is abandoned and not employed.

Race a fine steed alongside a man—
the man cannot outpace the steed;
place the man upon a chariot—
the steed cannot outpace the man.
Thus, those skilled in the Dao
use what others possess to establish their achievements,
relying on their own abilities to compensate for others’ limitations.

When the ruler gives in due time, the people repay with wealth;
when the ruler meets them with ritual, the people repay with their lives.
There are nations in peril, but no secure rulers;
there are anxious sovereigns, but no joyful ministers.
When virtue exceeds one’s station, it brings honor;
when emolument exceeds one’s virtue, it brings misfortune.
Virtue’s worth is boundless, righteousness’ demands are not excessive.
To hold high station without virtue is to usurp;
to take without righteousness is to steal.

The sage finds contentment in poverty, takes joy in the Dao,
does not harm life through desire,
does not burden himself with gain,
thus does not violate righteousness to take what is unjust.

Life in the world has endured long—
those who receive their due thrive and multiply.
Thus, the worthy ruler does not force what people cannot achieve,
nor does he cut off what they cannot endure,
ensuring all people receive their due sustenance.

Therefore, to find contentment in poverty and joy in the Dao,
to not harm life through desire,
to not violate righteousness in taking what is just—
what more desire could there be?

In ancient times, without virtue, one was not honored;
without ability, one was not appointed.

The man without merit is not rewarded;
the man without crime is not punished.
In advancing men, he relies on **rites** (*lǐ* 禮);
in dismissing them, he relies on **righteousness** (*yì* 義).

In the age of the petty man,
advancing men is like the earth’s sky—boundless;
dismissing them is like the depths of a well—unfathomable.
Speaking of antiquity is but to lament the present.

In selecting horses, one errs by favoring the sour;
in choosing men, one errs by favoring the poor.
A fat pig fills the kitchen, but a lean one is not made an **official** (*guān* 官).
The **gentleman** (*jūnzǐ* 君子) discerns truth,
and does not trust slanderous words.

If a **loyal subject** (*zhōngchén* 忠臣) fails to admonish his lord’s faults,
he is not truly loyal;
if the lord does not heed admonishment,
he is not truly enlightened.

If the people sink into ruin and the ruler takes no concern,
he is not a **worthy ruler** (*xiánjūn* 賢君).
Thus, to uphold **principle** (*jié* 節) and die in hardship
is the duty of a subject;
to endure cold and hunger
is the kindness of a filial father.

To use the **great** (*dà* 大) for the **small** (*xiǎo* 小) is called **perverting men** (*biànrén* 變人);
to use the **small** for the **great** is called **defying Heaven** (*nìtiān* 逆天).
Though one may rise to the heavens in the beginning,
he will surely fall into **calamity** (*kùn* 困) in the end.

Hence, in the village,
one respects the **elderly** (*lǎo* 老) by seniority,
and does not abandon the **poor** (*qióng* 穷);
in the court,
one ranks by **noble title** (*jué* 爵),
and distinguishes **honor and lowliness** (*zūn bēi* 尊卑) with proper order.

The method of selecting officials must consider **virtue/power** (*déxíng* 德行), speech, governance, and scholarship—if one excels in any of these, he should be fit for office. If he lacks all four, then he is but a monkey adorned with a scholar’s cap.

In antiquity, those without **virtue/power** were not honored, those without ability were not appointed, those without merit were not ennobled, and those with guilt were not spared. Thus, offices were not mismatched with men, nor were men left unused.

To **cherish the noble** is to draw near the ruler;
to **honor the elderly** is to draw near one’s kin;
to **respect the elder** is to draw near one’s brother.

Those born noble grow arrogant,
those born rich grow extravagant.
Thus, if the wealthy and noble do not reflect on the **Dao/Way** with clarity,
few will refrain from wrongdoing.

To study without surfeit is to cultivate oneself;
to teach without weariness is to govern the people.
With **worthy teachers and fine friends**, few will abandon virtue.

To know the **worthy** is wisdom,
to love the **worthy** is benevolence,
to honor the **worthy** is righteousness,
to revere the **worthy** is propriety,
to delight in the **worthy** is joy.

Father and son uphold **filial virtue** (*én* 恩),
ruler and subject uphold **righteous principle** (*yì* 义).
To grasp **virtue/power** and **righteous principle** is the foundation of loyalty and filial piety.

To **cherish the noble**, honor the elderly, and respect the elder—
this is to know the roots.

If one can know, love, honor, revere, and delight in the **worthy**,
then the **Dao/Way** of seeking, nurturing, and employing the **worthy** is attained.

The ancients who governed well did nothing yet accomplished all—
thus their governance was all-encompassing.
To embrace this all-encompassing **virtue/power** (*róng* 容),
to act without doing yet achieve results;
to fail in this **virtue/power**,
every action brings calamity.

To govern with this **virtue/power** is like:
**Cautious** as one crossing a river in winter—
**Fearful** as one wary of neighbors—
**Solemn** as a guest—
**Dissolved** as melting ice—
**Unadorned** as uncarved wood—
**Mixed** as muddy water—
**Vast** as a valley.

Thus:
**Cautious** as one crossing a river in winter—
he does not dare to act rashly;
**Fearful** as one wary of neighbors—
he fears self-harm;
**Solemn** as a guest—
he is reverent and respectful;
**Dissolved** as melting ice—
he does not dare to hoard;
**Unadorned** as uncarved wood—
he does not dare to be polished;
**Mixed** as muddy water—
he does not dare to be clear;
**Vast** as a valley—
he does not dare to be full.

He who advances does not dare to act first,
he who retreats does not dare to lead—
he who fears self-harm guards **softness and weakness** without arrogance—
he who is reverent in respect humbles himself to honor others—
he who does not dare hoard diminishes himself without rigidity—
he who does not dare be polished lacks nothing yet is incomplete—
he who does not dare be clear endures filth without pride—
he who does not dare be full sees insufficiency without self-exaltation.

Thus, the **Dao/Way** retreats yet leads,
guards **softness and weakness** yet stands firm,
humbles itself yet elevates others,
diminishes itself yet remains whole,
endures filth yet remains pure,
sees insufficiency yet suffices.

The **Dao/Way** of the **worthy** is to do nothing yet accomplish all.

The **virtue/power** of the **Great Virtue** (*Kǒngdé* 孔德) follows the **Dao/Way** alone.

The ancients who governed well did nothing yet accomplished all—
the vastness of the world, the multitude of people and things,
all were contained within it.
Thus, the greatness of **non-action** (*wúwéi* 無為) is its function,
and the people of the world thus revere it without burden.

---
*[End of Volume Ten of the* **Communicated Meanings of the *Tongxuan Zhenjing*** *]*

---
*[Volume Eleven of the* **Southern Valley Master Du Daojian’s Compilation*** *]*

**Volume Eleven: *Southern Valley Master Du Daojian’s Compilation*
*The Chapter on Supreme Virtue***

Laozi says:
All who seek the **Dao/Way** must clearly discern the distinction between Heaven and humanity, grasp the root of order and chaos, and clarify the mind to preserve it. To perceive its beginning and end, returning to the void and emptiness—this is called enlightenment.

The root of governance lies in **virtue/power** and righteousness; its branches are laws and institutions. That which sustains life is the root; that which does not is the branch. Root and branch are one body, yet their love is dual. To prioritize the root over the branch is the **worthy**; to prioritize the branch over the root is the petty.

Laws arise to aid righteousness—yet if laws outweigh righteousness, it is like valuing one’s cap and shoes while neglecting one’s head and feet. **Virtue/power** and righteousness are vast and exalted. To extend their breadth without deepening their substance is ruin; to elevate height without broadening the foundation is collapse.

Thus, without great beams, one cannot bear great weight. To bear weight, nothing surpasses the **lignum vitae**; to govern a nation, nothing surpasses **virtue/power**.

The ruler’s relation to the people is like the foundation to a city, the roots to a tree. Deep roots mean a firm foundation; a thick base means stability above. Therefore, what lacks foundation in **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way** cannot be called doctrine; what does not align with the ancient sages cannot be called the **Dao/Way**.

To merely select techniques for single acts or deeds is not the universal **Dao/Way**. The **worthy** discerns the distinction between Heaven and humanity, grasps the root of order and chaos.

Governance has roots and branches; knowing what to prioritize brings one near to **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way**. Can such methods govern the world?

Laozi says:
The **Dao/Way** of governing people is like **Zhaofu’s** driving of four horses. The reins and bit are aligned, the chest is straightened, the heart is internally harmonized, and the horses’ will is externally attuned. Thus, they follow the path, arrive far, with surplus **qi** and strength. Advance, retreat, turn, or curve—all as intended. Truly, he has mastered the technique.

Now, power and authority are the ruler’s chariot; great ministers are the four horses. The body cannot abandon the chariot’s stability, nor can the hands lose the horses’ harmony. Thus, if the four horses are not attuned, **Zhaofu** cannot take the path; if ruler and ministers are not in accord, even the sage cannot govern.

Wield the **Dao/Way** to guide them, and mediocrity can be exhausted; clarify distinctions to instruct them, and deceit can be halted. Observe changes as things arise, respond to transformations as events unfold. The near will not be disordered, and the distant will be governed.

Without contrived teachings, yet attaining the natural **Dao/Way**, all actions will be without error. Heaven and Earth are one horse; all things, one finger. The sage’s penetration to the utmost is likened to chariots and horses for power and ministers, the ruler riding upon them without exertion, conquering all without resistance—this is the method of governance.

How can one who wields the reins speak otherwise?

Laozi says:
All who practice the **Dao/Way** block the crooked paths, prevent darkness before it arises. They do not prize their own correctness but prize not doing wrong. Thus it is said:
*"Do not allow desire to arise; then there will be no daily seeking.
Do not allow what can be taken to arise; then there will be no daily contention."*

Thus, private desires are released, and the public **Dao/Way** prevails. What is in excess is restrained by measure; what is applied is sufficient, and thus the world can be unified.

If one abandons duty to listen to praise and blame, discards merit to favor factions, then odd talents flee and hide, officials neglect their posts; the people’s customs decay in the state, and ministers contend in court. Thus, with the **Dao/Way**, one governs others; without it, one is governed by others.

A skilled physician does not treat existing illness but prevents illness before it arises. Those who practice the **Dao/Way** block the crooked paths, govern what has not yet occurred—are they not like the skilled physician?

Thus, not prizing one’s own correctness but prizing not doing wrong means there will be no desire to seek what can be desired, no contention over what can be taken. Therefore, with the **Dao/Way**, one can govern others; without it, one is controlled by others.

Laozi says:
Governance of the state has constants, yet benefiting the people is the root. **Political teachings** have a **Dao/Way**, yet proper conduct is paramount. If it benefits the people, it need not conform to antiquity; if it suffices for affairs, it need not follow custom.

Thus, the sage adapts laws to the times and rites to customs. Clothing and tools are made convenient for use; laws and decrees are adapted to necessity. Therefore, altering antiquity is not wrong, nor is following custom sufficient praise.

Reciting the books of the ancient kings is not as good as hearing their words; hearing their words is not as good as understanding their intent. To grasp their intent is beyond words.

Thus, the **Dao/Way** that can be spoken is not the constant **Dao/Way**; the name that can be named is not the constant name.

The sage’s path is called the **Dao/Way**, like metal and stone—once tuned, it cannot be altered again; like the *qin* and *se*—when the melody ends, the tuning changes. Laws, institutions, rites, and music are the tools of governance, not the governance itself.

Thus, the narrow-minded cannot discuss the supreme **Dao/Way**, for they are bound by conventions and constrained by teachings.

The **Dao/Way** is the essence of law; law is the application of the **Dao/Way**. Governance of the state has constants, yet benefiting the people is the root—that is the **Dao/Way**. **Political teachings** have a **Dao/Way**, yet proper conduct is paramount—that is law.

The sage adapts laws to the times and rites to customs; laws and decrees are adapted to necessity. Thus, the narrow-minded cannot discuss the supreme **Dao/Way**, for they are bound by teachings.

Laozi says:
Is there truly a constant law in the world? What aligns with worldly affairs, accords with human nature, harmonizes with Heaven and Earth, and attends to spirits and gods—this can rectify governance.

In ancient times, the Three Sovereigns governed without laws, yet the people obeyed. The Five Emperors established laws but had no punishments. The Xia did not break their word; the Yin took oaths; the Zhou made alliances.

In the declining ages, they endured humiliation yet despised shame, coveted gain yet lacked disgrace.

The laws and decrees must be discussed in light of customs and adjusted to the urgency of the times. Tools and implements must be adapted to changing circumstances to suit their purpose.

Those who adhere strictly to laws cannot be entrusted with distant undertakings; those bound by ritual cannot be expected to respond to change. Only those with singular insight and acute hearing can then claim to embody the **Dao/Way** and act accordingly.

Those who understand the origins of law will adapt it to the times; those who do not grasp the source of governance, though they follow tradition, will only bring chaos.

Nowadays, scholars cling to ancient teachings, clutching texts and rigid doctrines, hoping to govern by them—like trying to fit a square peg into a round hole. They will find it difficult to achieve what is fitting.

To preserve safety and restore order, even the wise are powerless; to uphold the **Dao/Way** by emulating antiquity, even the foolish have an advantage.

Thus, laws that are not applied, the sage does not enact; words untested, the enlightened ruler does not heed.

The sage establishes laws to prohibit deceit, redress wrongs, and protect the people. The Three Sovereigns ruled without decrees, yet the people obeyed. From the Five Emperors onward, the laws, rewards, and punishments of each dynasty differed—because the times changed.

How can an enlightened ruler fail to investigate this?

**Wénzǐ** asked: *"Where do laws originate?"*
**Laozi** replied: *"Laws arise from *yì* (proper conduct), and *yì* arises from satisfying the many. When the many are satisfied, this is the essence of governance. Laws do not descend from Heaven nor emerge from Earth; they arise among people and return to self-regulation. If one attends to the root, disorder in the branches will not occur. Knowing the essentials, one is not confused by doubts; possessing them within oneself, one does not criticize others; lacking them within oneself, one does not blame those who uphold them. Those governed below must not neglect what is above; what is forbidden to the people must not be practiced by the ruler himself. Thus, the ruler’s laws serve as his own standard. If restraint begins with the ruler, then his commands will prevail among the people."*

When **Wénzǐ** asked where laws originate, **Laozi** taught that laws arise from *yì*—what is fitting. The ancient kings established laws to suit the people’s sentiments, so they first set themselves as the standard: what they forbade among the people, they themselves dared not violate. Thus, their decrees were followed, and the world obeyed.

Laws are the measure of the world, the standard of the ruler. To countenance lawlessness is to defy law itself. Once laws are established, those who conform are rewarded, and those who deviate are punished—even the noble are not lightly rewarded, and the lowly are not harshly punished. Those who break the law, though wise, must be punished; those who conform, though unworthy, are guiltless. Thus, public virtue flourishes while private desires are curbed.

In ancient times, officials were appointed to restrain the people, preventing them from acting recklessly; rulers were established to control officials, preventing them from acting unilaterally; laws and **Dao/Way** were instituted to restrain rulers, preventing them from acting arbitrarily. When none may act recklessly, the **Dao/Way** prevails, and order is restored. Thus, it returns to *wuwei* (non-action)—not that it is inactive, but that its authority emanates from within.

Laws are the ruler’s measure, the world’s standard. Once established, they must be applied without exception: those who break the law, even if noble, must be punished; those who conform, even if lowly, are guiltless. Thus, private desires are curbed, and public virtue prevails.

In ancient times, officials were appointed, rulers established, and rites and laws instituted. When these three were not neglected, the world had no grievances, and society could return to simplicity—what need then for laws and decrees?

**Laozi** said:
*"The skilled in rewards spend little yet inspire many;
the skilled in punishments minimize penalties yet deter vice;
the skilled in giving use sparingly yet cultivate virtue;
the skilled in taking amass much yet incur no resentment.*

Thus, the sage encourages the good by aligning with what the people favor and restrains vice by addressing what they abhor. Reward one, and the world rushes to emulate; punish one, and the world fears to repeat. Hence, the greatest rewards cost nothing, and the severest punishments are never excessive. The sage governs with restraint yet achieves broad results—this is what is meant.

To give life, take life, and conceal—these are the **Dao/Way** of Heaven; to reward, punish, take, and give—these are the **Dao/Way** of humanity. The sage aligns above with Heaven’s **Dao/Way** and below with the people’s hearts, establishing the foundation for equitable governance.

Those who hold the **Dao/Way** of the world must, in every matter, act with perfect virtue. Thus, reward one, and the world rushes to emulate; punish one, and the world fears to repeat.

**Laozi** said:
*"The **Dao/Way** of the subject is to discern what is right, act first in matters, uphold duty, and clarify distinctions to achieve success. Thus, when ruler and subject differ in **Dao/Way**, order prevails; when they share the same **Dao/Way**, chaos ensues. Each must find their proper place, and all will have their due—then above and below can govern each other. Hence, branches must not surpass the trunk, nor extremities the root; speech and action must balance in weight and measure.*

Those who wield authority hold little yet bear great responsibility, adhere to strict principles yet govern broadly. A tree ten *zhàng* in girth supports a thousand-*jūn* house—this is the power of structure; a five-*cùn* hinge controls the opening and closing—this is the power of position.

Orders that must be obeyed bring benefit to those who comply and harm to those who resist—none dare disobey, for it is natural. When commands are issued and prohibitions enforced, it is through the collective force of the people.

*Yì* (proper conduct) does not seek to benefit all under Heaven; benefiting one inspires the world to follow. Tyranny does not seek to harm all within the seas; harming one provokes universal rebellion. Thus, every action and decision must be carefully considered.

The ruler depends on the subject to stand, the subject depends on the ruler to act. The ruler must act with *wuwei* above, the subject must act below. To discern what is right, uphold duty, and clarify distinctions—this is the subject’s role.

When ruler and subject each find their proper place, then above and below can govern each other, and great and small can restrain each other."*

「Thus, differing from the Dao/Way is the path to governance—every action and decision holds weight in determining order or chaos, and the ruler cannot be too cautious in his deliberations.

Laozi said: *"To bend by an inch and extend by a foot, to yield in small ways and stand upright in great ones—this is the way of the sage."* Yet, when rulers judge their subjects, they fail to weigh their grand achievements, overlook their broad impact, and instead demand minor virtues, thereby losing the path to recognizing true merit.

Hence, one with profound *德* (virtue/power) should not be faulted for trifling matters; one with great renown should not be tarnished by petty flaws. For all human nature has its shortcomings, yet what matters is their grand vision—even if they have minor faults, these do not undermine their worth. If their grand vision is lacking, then their conduct in common affairs is unremarkable.

Thus, those overly strict in small matters achieve no great success, and those who censure others’ conduct are shunned by the masses. Those who grasp the greater whole have looser small constraints; those with vast ambition earn distant praise. This is the way of judging subjects.

True full talents are rare in the world—this has always been so. The master carpenter seeks beams and pillars, not clinging to minor flaws, thus securing great materials. If a sovereign judges his ministers and advisors with the understanding of *"bending by an inch to extend by a foot,"* then great sages can be found.

Indeed, no one is flawless in all things, nor are all deeds perfect. Abandoning the small to take the great—what grand endeavor cannot be accomplished? Overlooking the short to follow the long—what matter cannot succeed?

Laozi said: *"From ancient times to today, none have been able to perfect their conduct."*

Thus, the noble does not blame others for their failings. The square does not cut itself, the sharp does not slice itself, the straight does not overstep, the broad does not belittle—when it comes to *道* (Dao/Way), *德* (virtue/power), and martial prowess, the noble does not blame others.

Cultivating oneself through the Dao/Way and not blaming others is effortless. Cultivating oneself through the Dao/Way means one will have no regrets.

The *huang* (jade) of the Xia sovereign could not be without blemishes; the pearls of the bright moon could not be without impurities. Yet the world treasures them, not allowing minor flaws to overshadow their great beauty.

Now, if one focuses on others’ shortcomings and forgets their strengths, yet still seeks to find merit in the world—this is indeed difficult. The common view holds that those of low station and humble birth, when faced with great dishonor, do not comprehend their grand vision.

Thus, the way of judging others is: observe their actions when in prosperity, their generosity when in wealth, their integrity when in poverty, and their restraint when in lowly status. See how they endure hardship to know their courage; observe their response to joy to gauge their resolve; test them with riches to measure their benevolence; frighten them to test their virtue. So, one may discern their true nature.

Those who excuse themselves do not reform their faults; those who blame others cannot maintain true friendship. The noble does not blame others, knowing that not all are Yao or Shun, and thus cannot excel in every matter. If one has great talent, how can they be discarded for minor flaws?

Laozi said: *"To bend is to seek extension; to be crooked is to seek straightness. Bending by an inch to extend by a foot, yielding in small ways to stand upright in great ones—this is the way of the noble."*

Though all streams flow together, those that do not empty into the sea are not valleys. Though all paths diverge, those that do not return to virtue are not the way of the noble. Good words are valued for their practicality; good deeds are valued for their benevolence and righteousness.

The noble’s faults are like the eclipses of the sun and moon—they do not diminish their brilliance. Thus, the wise do not act rashly, the brave do not kill recklessly. They choose what is fitting and act according to propriety, so that their deeds are accomplished and their achievements can be relied upon, and though they die, their names endure.

Even if one has wisdom and ability, they must root themselves in benevolence and righteousness to stand firm. When wisdom and ability are both cultivated, the sage uses benevolence and righteousness as the central measure—those who conform are called noble, those who do not are called petty.

Though the noble may die, their name does not perish; though the petty may gain power, their crimes are not absolved. To hold the map of all under Heaven in one hand while slaying oneself with the other—even the foolish would not do this, for life is more precious than all the world.

To face death for one’s sovereign and kin, one regards death as returning home—righteousness is heavier than life itself.

Thus, the greatest good of the world, when compared to life, is small; but life’s greatest weight, when compared to righteousness, is light. This is the measure of one who uses benevolence and righteousness as his standard.

The way of bending and extending is like the dragon and serpent in hibernation and arousal—nobles yield in small ways to stand upright in great ones, just as the dragon and serpent gather their *qi* before surging forward. Good words and good deeds nourish all living things, all arising from bending oneself to cultivate virtue.

The sage uses benevolence and righteousness as his standard, knowing that life is heavier than all the world, and righteousness is heavier than life itself. Thus, they can face death for their sovereign and kin.

Therefore, though the noble may die, their name does not vanish.

Laozi said: *"The ranks of Dao/Way and virtue/power are like the sun and moon—even the barbarians cannot alter their course."* If one’s inclinations and actions are balanced, then their reputation is not bound by common opinion; if their intentions and conduct are harmonized, then their rise or fall depends on the times.

If their deeds are complete in the world, then their achievements are fulfilled; if their efforts align with the times, then their names endure. Thus, those who establish achievements and renown are simple in the world yet meticulous in the times—when the time arrives, there is no pause for breath.

The ancient warriors did not fight for land or riches, but to preserve life and restore order, to remove harm from the people. Those greedy for wealth and power, who covet many desires, bring ruin to the world, causing the masses to be restless and never at peace.

When a sage arises, they punish the strong and violent, restore order to chaos, and remove harm from the world—turning turbidity into clarity, danger into security. Thus, they must inevitably rise and act.

The ranks of Dao/Way and virtue/power in humanity are like the sun and moon in the heavens; the five human inclinations are like the five stars. Even barbarians cannot alter their course.

Whether one follows or abandons is in their own hands, not determined by others’ opinions. Whether they are used or not depends on the times; whether they succeed or fail hinges on their fate. Thus, the noble acts in the Dao/Way when the time is right, without pause for breath.

The Vermilion Sovereign caused the fire calamity, so the Yellow Sovereign subdued him.»

共工 unleashed the water calamity, so Zhuanxu executed him.

He taught them with the Dao/Way, guided them with virtue/power, yet they refused to listen—then he confronted them with might and force. If they still did not comply, he subdued them with arms.

To slaughter the innocent and nourish the unrighteous—no greater harm exists. To exhaust the wealth of the realm for the desires of one—no deeper calamity exists. To indulge one’s own desires while prolonging the afflictions of the land—this is what the natural order rejects.

The reason kings are established is to suppress violence and chaos. Yet now, they exploit the strength of the people, only to become tyrants and bandits. How then can they not be removed? Just as one who raises fish must eliminate the otters, and one who tends beasts must eradicate the wolves—how much more so when shepherding the people?

Thus, the origin of arms and warfare.

The five phases of the state’s transformations have endured for ages. In the Fire Phase of the Vermilion Sovereign, the ruler lost his virtue/power, and fire became a calamity. In the Water Phase of共工, the ruler lost his virtue/power, and water became a calamity. Thus, it is known: when a ruler loses virtue/power, the corresponding phase brings disaster—this is the origin of arms and warfare.

All who govern lands—can they lose virtue/power and bring calamity upon themselves, yet remain unaware of warning?

Laozi said: *"The Way of governing a state is this: the sovereign issues no harsh decrees, officials impose no burdensome regulations, scholars engage in no deceitful conduct, and artisans practice no excessive craftsmanship. Their tasks are fulfilled without disturbance, their tools are complete without ornamentation.*

*Yet in chaotic times, it is otherwise: those who act seek to outdo one another in extravagance; those who uphold ritual vie in false pretension. Chariots are over-elaborated with carvings, utensils are excessively adorned with engravings. Those who seek wealth compete to hoard rare treasures as precious; those who criticize culture pursue frivolous complications as urgent. Scholars engage in false argumentation, delaying decisions endlessly without aiding governance but fostering disorder. Artisans craft bizarre devices, taking years to complete, yet they serve no practical purpose.*

*The Way of Shennong decreed: If men of prime vigor do not till the fields, there will be those who starve in the land. If women of childbearing years do not weave, there will be those who freeze. Thus, the sage himself plowed and his wife wove, setting an example for the people. He guided them, not valuing rare goods nor prizing useless objects. Therefore, the tillers did not toil excessively, yet had enough to sustain life; the weavers did not labor beyond measure, yet had enough to clothe their bodies. Neither surplus nor deficiency existed—each took only what they needed. With abundant food and clothing, deceit did not arise; with peace and ease, the realm was harmonious. The wise had no schemes to employ, the brave no valor to display. The methods of ancient and modern governance differ because customs change.*

*In ancient times, people were pure and simple: the sovereign issued no harsh decrees, officials imposed no burdensome regulations, scholars engaged in no deceitful conduct, and artisans practiced no excessive craftsmanship. Thus, the people’s hearts were easily satisfied, and governance was not difficult. In later ages, customs shifted and winds changed; the sovereign’s excesses were emulated below, luxury became the norm, and greed knew no bound. Hence, the people’s hearts were hard to satisfy, and governance became arduous."*

Laozi said: *"The Way of the hegemonic king is this: he plans with strategy, he schemes with tactics. He acts with righteousness, not for mere survival, but to secure or destroy. Thus, when he hears of a neighboring ruler oppressing his people, he raises his army to their borders, accusing them of unrighteousness and reproaching their misdeeds. Upon arrival, he commands his generals: ‘Do not cut down trees, do not dig up graves, do not destroy crops, do not burn stores, do not capture civilians, do not seize livestock.’ Then he proclaims: ‘Their ruler defies heaven and earth, insults the spirits, judges unjustly, and slaughters the innocent. Heaven condemns him, and the people seek vengeance. Our army comes to abolish unrighteousness and bestow virtue/power upon them. Any who resist the heavenly order and harm the people shall be exterminated—root and branch. Those who surrender their households shall be rewarded with their households; those who surrender their neighborhoods shall be rewarded with their neighborhoods; those who surrender their districts shall be ennobled as lords of their districts. We conquer the state, but spare its people. We depose the tyrant, reform his governance, honor the virtuous, exalt the worthy, aid the orphaned and widowed, relieve the poor, release the imprisoned, and reward the meritorious.’ The people open their doors to welcome them, offering grain and provisions, fearing only that they may not arrive. A righteous army reaches the border and halts without battle. An unrighteous army arrives amid piles of corpses and flowing blood, clashing until all is lost.*

*He who fights for the land cannot achieve kingship; he who fights for himself cannot establish his merit. He who acts for others gains their support; he who acts for himself loses them. Where the multitude aids, even the weak become strong; where the multitude abandons, even the mighty perish."*

The *Wenzi* comprises twelve chapters, of which three are primary and four secondary. It encompasses both the ancient sages and the hegemons—within it lie the emperors and kings. The age of hegemony was perhaps the autumn of the times? Observing its language—*"not for mere survival, but to secure or destroy"*—one sees the spirit of revival and restoration vividly, far removed from the unrighteous deeds of later ages.

Laozi said: *"The superior ruler governs the state, orders the realm, practices benevolence and righteousness, dispenses virtue/power and favors, establishes just laws, and blocks deceitful paths. His ministers and officials are loyal, the people are harmonious, and all hearts are united in purpose. The feudal lords submit to his authority, and the four quarters revere his virtue/power. He refines governance within the ancestral temple, and repels invasions a thousand li away. His decrees issued, the realm responds in unison—this is the highest attainment.*

*In the next rank: vast lands, numerous people, a wise sovereign, capable generals, abundant wealth, strong arms, reliable discipline, clear orders. When facing equal foes, before a single blade is drawn, the enemy flees in rout—this is the secondary attainment.*

*He knows the suitability of the land, masters the advantages of terrain, understands the shifts of oppressive governance, and discerns the tactics of battle."*

To wield the blade in close combat, to face arrows and stones like rain, to carry the slain and tend the wounded—this is the lowest application of virtue/Way.

The triumph or defeat of armies lies in governance. When governance overcomes the people, the ministers submit and the soldiers grow strong. When the people overcome governance, the ministers rebel and the soldiers grow weak. When virtue/Way is sufficient to embrace the people of the realm, when undertakings are adequate to meet the realm’s crises, when appointments and promotions win the hearts of worthy scholars, and when counsel and deliberation resolve matters of weight—this is the highest virtue/Way of the military.

The *Art of War* states that the first to act is the host, the one who responds is the guest. There is a saying about warfare: *"I dare not be the host and instead be the guest,"*—meaning one must not act rashly in battle.
