When use of arms is unavoidable, then just cause is supreme; the enemy’s flight is secondary, and combat itself is the lowest recourse. Laozi said: *"The reason a state grows strong is that its people are willing to die."* The reason they are willing to die is that their cause is just. The just cause succeeds because of its righteous might. Thus, to govern with *civil* means and align with *martial* force is called the way of inevitable victory; when righteous might and just cause are united, this is called strength.

In battle, when blades clash and arrows and stones fall like rain, and officers vie to lead—it is because rewards are trustworthy and punishments are clear. If a ruler regards his subjects as sons, they will serve him as fathers. If a ruler regards his subjects as brothers, they will follow him as elder siblings. If he views them as sons, he will reign over the four seas. If he views them as fathers, he will rectify the world.

If a ruler regards his subjects as brothers, he will not hesitate to die for them. If subjects serve their ruler as elder siblings, they will not hesitate to perish for him. Hence, when the bonds are those of father and son or brother and brother, such conflicts cannot be fought.

Therefore, a just ruler refines governance within and accumulates virtue/power within the ancestral temple, while repelling evil without to demonstrate his might. He observes their labor and rest to know their hunger and fullness. When battle is certain, he treats death as returning home—such is the depth of his benevolence.

There is a Way to govern the realm—why resort to arms at all? Only when there is no alternative. The soldiers of a strong state die because they are compelled by just cause. Then, would a ruler of the Way tolerate a strong state that places its people in the path of death?

If a ruler regards his subjects as sons, and they serve him as fathers, then a just ruler who refines governance and accumulates virtue/power will see his state grow strong naturally—such examples exist in the world.

*(End of Chapter Eleven of the *True Classic of the Mysterious Dao*, compiled by the Southern Valley Master Du Daojian.)*

---
**Chapter Twelve: The Way of the Realm** *(Compiled by the Southern Valley Master Du Daojian.)*

**Chapter Twelve: The Way of the Realm**
*(The True Classic of the Mysterious Dao, compiled by the Southern Valley Master Du Daojian)*

---

**Section on Supreme Rites**

Laozi said:
In the age of the ancient True Ones, they harmonized the breath of yin and yang, and all living beings looked up to their virtue/power in harmony. At that time, all things spontaneously aligned, their essence remained hidden and pure, and the world thrived in great ease.

When the age declined to the time of Fu Xi, men grew dim and laborious, all seeking to abandon their childlike innocence and awaken to the world. Between heaven and earth, virtue became fragmented and inconsistent. When Shennong and the Yellow Emperor took leadership over the world, they established the framework of the four seasons, harmonized yin and yang, and thus all people stood in awe, straining their senses to listen and obey. Yet governance remained unbalanced.

By the time of the Xia and Shang dynasties, desires grew excessive, and wisdom was seduced by external things, so that nature and life lost their true essence. When this spread to the Zhou, refinement scattered simplicity, and they abandoned the Dao for artifice, perverting virtue into conduct. Cleverness and cunning emerged, and men mimicked the sages with deceitful learning, flaunting empty rhetoric to coerce the masses, polishing poetry and books to trade for fame. Each sought to impose their own intelligence, masking themselves in falsehoods to fit the world, thus losing the root of the great tradition.

Thus, the world has long suffered the decay of nature and life.

Therefore, the study of the True Ones seeks to restore nature through stillness, to wander the heart in emptiness. The study of the common world, however, flaunts virtue/power and exhausts nature, agitating the five viscera, recklessly acting beyond wisdom, and clamoring for fame in the world—these are the things the True Ones do not do. To exhaust nature is to sever life.

The True Ones are fixed in the intent of life and death, penetrating the logic of honor and shame. If the world praises them, they are not further encouraged; if the world condemns them, they are not further discouraged. This is the essence of attaining the Dao.

Is the "supreme rite" the five rites—auspicious, mourning, military, guest, and festive?

The ancient True Ones were the sovereigns of the primal age. At that time, all living beings were pure and simple, and the world thrived in great ease. From Fu Xi to Shennong and the Yellow Emperor, and extending to the Three Sovereigns, governance varied, and rites adapted accordingly, reaching their peak in the Warring States era. But the root was already lost.

Laozi said:
In ancient times, the sage-kings wore loose hair without collars and ruled the world. Their virtue/power gave life without taking it, gave without seizing, and the world did not reject their ways. All shared in their virtue/power. At that time, yin and yang were in harmony, and all things flourished. Birds’ nests could be reached by bending down, and wild beasts could be tethered and led.

But as the age declined, birds, beasts, insects, and serpents all became men’s adversaries. Thus, iron was cast and blades forged to repel these dangers. When people faced hardship, they sought convenience; when they encountered calamity, they devised defenses. Each used their intelligence to avoid harm and pursue benefit. Customs were not fixed, and tools were not permanent. Thus, the laws of the ancient kings were subject to change.

Therefore, it is said: *"The named is the named, but not the unchanging name."* The Five Emperors followed different Ways, yet their virtue/power covered the world. The Three Kings pursued distinct affairs, yet their names endured in posterity. This was adapting to the times.

It is like the blind musician Zhi Kuang tuning the five tones—his adjustments had no fixed measure, yet none were out of harmony. Thus, those attuned to the essence of music could compose melodies. Those who grasped the fundamental principles of compasses, squares, hooks, and cords could govern men.

Therefore, the ancient kings’ institutions, if no longer fitting, were abandoned; the customs of later ages, if beneficial, were adopted. Thus, the sages who established rites and music were not bound by rites and music, those who governed things were not bound by things, and those who made laws were not bound by laws. Hence it is said: *"The Dao that can be spoken is not the eternal Dao."*

The Way of Heaven has no constancy, and the world grows ever more degenerate. In ancient times, the sovereigns wore loose hair without collars, and the world did not reject their attire. The people and creatures flourished, all sharing in their virtue/power. But as the age declined, birds, beasts, insects, and serpents all became men’s adversaries. Laws and tools adapted to the times, and thus warfare arose.

Laozi said:
In ancient times, the sage-kings looked upward to the patterns of Heaven, downward to the measures of Earth, and inward to the laws of humanity. They regulated the qi of yin and yang, harmonized the rhythms of the four seasons, and observed the suitability of hills, plains, waters, and fertile or barren lands to establish affairs and generate wealth, removing the sufferings of hunger and cold, averting the calamities of sickness and misfortune.

Within, they received the affairs of humanity to establish rites and music, practicing the Way of benevolence and righteousness to govern human relationships. They arranged the natures of metal, wood, water, fire, and earth to establish filial piety and form the family. They listened to the pure and impure tones of the five notes and the generative sequences of the six pitches to establish the obligations of ruler and subject and form the state. They observed the order of early, middle, and late in the four seasons to establish the hierarchy of elders and juniors and form the bureaucracy. They divided the lands into regions and assigned duties to govern them. They established the Grand Academy to teach—these were the fundamental principles of governance.

When the Way is attained, it is upheld; when the Way is lost, it is abandoned. Nothing in the world remains stretched without relaxation, completed without ruin—only the sage can achieve completion without decay.

Is the principle of Heaven’s unity the same as the principle of a man’s life? To know the beginning of life is to know the origin of all things, for the time before life is the time before all things.

When a man is young and then grows strong, it is like the convergence and movement of the primal unity. When he grows old, it is like the passage of an age. To know that youth transforms into strength, strength into old age, and old age into death is to understand what comes after the origin of all things.

As for those who transform without being transformed—are they not the sages alone?

When the sages first composed music, it was to return the spirit and suppress licentiousness, restoring the heart to its natural state. But as it declined, it became excessive and unreturning, indulging in sensuality without regard for proper law, spreading to later ages until it led to the fall of kingdoms.

As for their writings, they were meant to guide all affairs—so that the foolish would not forget, and the wise would record events.

When it declined, they resorted to deceit and falsehood to exonerate the guilty and condemn the innocent, putting the innocent to death.

As for their parks and preserves, they were originally established to provide for the offerings of ancestral temples and to mobilize soldiers for unexpected emergencies. But when they declined, they indulged in reckless hunting and hawking, seizing the people’s time and exhausting their strength.

As for their promotion of the worthy, it was meant to harmonize teaching and governance, to adjudicate lawsuits fairly, to place the worthy in office, and to assign the capable to their duties. Benevolence spread below, and the common people cherished virtue. But when it declined, factions and cliques formed, each advancing their own allies, abandoning the public for private gain, and elevating outsiders and insiders alike. The wicked took office, while the worthy withdrew in obscurity.

The writings of **Wénzǐ** serve as a mirror for ten thousand generations.

The original intent of the sages in establishing these institutions was:
Music, to return the spirit and suppress licentiousness;
Writings, to guide all affairs;
Parks and preserves, to provide for ancestral temple offerings;
Promotion of the worthy, to harmonize teaching and governance, and to adjudicate lawsuits fairly.

But when they declined:
Music became an indulgence in sensuality,
Writings became deceitful,
Parks and preserves became grounds for hunting,
Promotion of the worthy became factionalism,
And the wicked took office while the worthy hid away—this was inevitable.

The **Dao** of Heaven and Earth is such that extremes reverse, and excess leads to loss.
Thus, the sages addressed decay by reforming institutions, and when one method reached its end, they introduced another. Their excellence lay in harmony, their failure in expedience.

The **Dao** of the sages states:
Without cultivating ritual and righteousness, propriety and shame cannot stand. If the people lack propriety and shame, they cannot be governed. Without knowing ritual and righteousness, laws cannot be properly enforced. Without upholding the good and abandoning the base, people will not turn toward ritual and righteousness. Without laws, governance is impossible. Without knowing ritual and righteousness, laws cannot be implemented.

Laws may kill the unfilial, but they cannot make people filial. They may punish thieves, but they cannot make people virtuous.

When the sage-kings ruled above, they made their preferences and disapprovals clear to the people, using praise and blame to guide them. They honored the worthy and advanced them, while they degraded the unworthy and removed them. They enforced punishments but did not rely on them; they cultivated ritual and righteousness and entrusted governance to the virtuous.

Thus, the highest in the land were the **Three Dukes**, the highest in a state were the **Nine Ministers**, the highest in a country were the **Twenty-Seven Great Officers**, and the highest in a village were the **Eighty-One Elemental Worthies**.

The vastness of Heaven and Earth cannot stand without humanity. The exalted status of the emperor cannot be sustained without the people. The multitude of the four quarters cannot be governed without ritual, righteousness, and shame.

Therefore, the sages reformed decayed institutions and established new ones, using ritual, righteousness, and shame as the four cardinal supports. When the worthy were in their posts, ritual and righteousness flourished, and punishments were rarely used.

To surpass ten thousand people in wisdom was called **Yīng** (英),
To surpass a thousand was called **Zhì** (智),
To surpass a hundred was called **Jié** (杰),
To surpass ten was called **Háo** (豪).

One who understood the **Dao** of Heaven and Earth, and grasped the principles of human nature,
Whose breadth could embrace the multitude, whose benevolence could win the distant,
Whose wisdom could discern expedience—such a person was **Yīng** (英);

One whose virtue could teach and transform, whose conduct could uphold righteousness,
Whose trustworthiness could win the people, whose clarity could illuminate below—such a person was **Zhì** (智);

One whose conduct could serve as a model, whose wisdom could resolve doubts,
Whose trustworthiness could keep agreements, whose integrity could govern the distribution of wealth,
Whose actions were lawful, whose words were proper—such a person was **Jié** (杰);

One who fulfilled their duties without neglect, who adhered to righteousness without forming factions,
Who did not evade difficulties and did not seek unjust gain—such a person was **Háo** (豪).

The **Yīng, Zhì, Jié,** and **Háo** were placed in their respective positions according to their talents—from root to branch, from heavy to light—with the ruler leading and the people responding in harmony. Within the four seas, all hearts turned in unison, abandoning greed and vulgarity, turning toward benevolence and righteousness. In transforming the people, their influence was like the wind sweeping through the grass.

Now, if the unworthy rule over the worthy, even severe punishments cannot restrain their deceit.

The small cannot control the great, the weak cannot command the strong—this is the nature of Heaven and Earth. Thus, the sages selected the worthy to establish merit, while unworthy rulers elevated their like-minded allies. By observing whom they promote, one can discern whether a state is orderly or chaotic. By examining their factions, one can judge whether they are worthy or unworthy.

In ancient times, the method of selecting officials ranked **moral cultivation and virtue** highest, followed by **benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and music**, then **writing, arithmetic, and laws**. Only then did they consider **wisdom**. Was this not the method of the **Warring States**?

The principles of governance are such that the small cannot control the great, and the weak cannot control the strong. If one relies on **balancing scales and sealed debates**, even severe punishments cannot restrain deceit.

**Lǎozǐ** said:
Those who establish ritual carve and polish human nature, forcing and restraining its spontaneity. Though the eyes desire something, ritual restrains its measure. Though the heart takes pleasure, ritual restricts it with propriety. Moving, circling, bowing, and scraping—limbs stiffen though they do not eat, wine settles though they do not drink. Externally, the body is bound; internally, the mind is troubled. The harmony of yin and yang is suppressed, and the essence of life is strained. Thus, one spends their life as a sorrowful being.

Why? Because they do not address the root of desire but only restrain its expression. They do not explore the source of joy but only prevent its indulgence. This is like caging a wild beast without blocking its path, or damning a river’s flow with one’s hands. Therefore, it is said:
*"Open the mouth of the flood, complete the task—yet the life will never be saved."*

Ritual does not eliminate desire but restrains it.
Music does not eliminate joy but regulates it.

Ritual is the standard for self-discipline and the tool to prevent vice—it is the universal path of the world.

As **Yánzǐ**’s gaze, hearing, speech, and movement adhered to ritual, keeping his heart in harmony with it, then the improper could not enter.

The proper use of ritual values **harmony** above all.
When a gentleman’s heart is full, ritual is sincere within and expressed without, naturally harmonizing with Heaven and Earth. Human relationships become harmonious, yin and yang are balanced, and all things flourish.

The ruler who makes all under Heaven fear punishment so that none dare steal—
how much better it would be to eliminate the very desire to steal!

Thus, when the use of something is known, even the greedy will decline it;
when its uselessness is unknown, even the virtuous cannot relinquish it.

The reason states perish, rulers die by others’ hands, and they become the laughingstock of the world—
it is never because of anything other than desire.

If one knows that a fan in winter and a fur in summer serve no purpose,
then all things would be reduced to dust and filth.

Thus, to stop boiling by skimming the foam only makes it worse—
one must remove the fire at its source.

The difficulty of governing theft has long persisted.
Steal a hook and you are executed; steal a kingdom and you become a feudal lord.
Thus, theft lies with the ruler, not the commoner.

If, like Yao, one’s thatched hut is left untrimmed and unhewn timber uncarved,
even rewards would not tempt theft.

To topple palaces of jade and gold, to ruin the halls of the tyrants—
the fate of Jie and Zhou—
their bodies torn apart by others, alas!

Laozi said:
*"To act in accordance with one’s nature is called the Dao;
to attain one’s innate nature is called virtue.*

When nature is lost, then benevolence is prized;
when the Dao is lost, then righteousness is prized.
Once benevolence and righteousness are established, virtue and the Dao decay.
When purity and simplicity scatter, ritual and music become ornate;
when distinctions of right and wrong take form, the people are bewildered;
when jade and pearls are treasured, the world falls into contention.

Ritual serves to distinguish the noble from the base, the high from the low;
righteousness harmonizes the relationships between ruler and minister, father and son,
brothers, husband and wife—the proper order of human affairs.

In the decadent age, ritual becomes mere formality, and acts of courtesy are called ‘righteousness’
for the sake of gain, while lords and ministers denounce one another,
and kin harbor grudges.

Thus, stagnant water breeds cannibalistic creatures,
decaying earth spawns self-devouring beasts—
ornate ritual and music give rise to deceit and falsehood.

No thing, in its use, endures without eventually decaying.
Though the Dao may be followed and virtue attained,
if the time is not right, it cannot be practiced.

Even when lords and ministers uphold righteousness,
they cannot escape mutual reproach;
when parents and children rely on affection,
they may still breed resentment.

Is this not the fixed nature of human hearts?

In the decadent age, governance does not accumulate the means of sustenance,
but instead corrupts the purity of the people, scatters their simplicity,
confuses the masses, and inverts clarity for filth.
Life and essence scatter in chaos, all for the sake of greed.
Sincerity and trust decay into arrogance and negligence,
and people lose their true nature.
Law and righteousness diverge, conduct and self-interest oppose one another,
while the rich and poor scheme against each other—
even the ruler and his servants are beyond comparison.

When one has excess, one should yield;
when one lacks, one contends.
Yielding gives rise to ritual and righteousness;
contention spawns violence and disorder.
Thus, excessive desire leads to unchecked actions,
and insatiable greed fuels endless strife.

Therefore, in an age of order, the petty man adheres to propriety
and cannot be enticed by profit;
in an age of chaos, the noble resorts to deceit
and cannot be restrained by law.

Food is essential to life—
one day without it brings hunger,
three days brings sickness,
seven days brings death.

In ancient times, states stored ten years’ worth of grain,
and thus could endure nine years of flood and seven years of drought
without the people starving.

But in the decadent age, governance does not accumulate the means of sustenance—
for rulers, consumed by desire, cannot curb their actions.
When the ruler lacks provision, he must take from below;
when the people lack sustenance, they must contend for survival.
How grave is this hunger!

Laozi said:
*"In a declining age, the ruler drills through mountains for ore,
plucks gold and jade like fruit,
harvests pearls and shells,
melts copper and iron—
yet all things wither and fail to flourish.

He flays the pregnant, burns the sacrificial grounds,
destroys nests and ruins states—
the phoenix will not alight, the qilin will not roam.
He builds wooden towers, burns forests for hunting,
drains ponds for fishing, piles earth to make mounds,
dug wells to drink from, dredges rivers to make pools,
fortifies cities as strongholds, imprisons beasts for sport—
and thus yin and yang are disrupted, the four seasons fall out of order.
Thunder and lightning are shattered, hail and frost bring ruin,
ten thousand things wither and die prematurely.
Half the crops fail, grasses and trees wither in summer,
the three great rivers run dry and cease to flow.

He demarcates mountains and rivers, setting boundaries,
counts the people, assigning quotas,
devises traps and ambushes as defenses,
regulates clothing and colors, ranks the noble and base,
distinguishes the worthy from the unworthy,
and enforces rewards and punishments—
and thus war arises, and resentment spreads.
The innocent are slaughtered, the guilty go unpunished—
this is how calamity begins.

Those who are full yet know not to stop
are condemned by Heaven, Earth, and the spirits.

When yin and yang are disrupted, the four seasons fall out of order,
thunder and lightning are shattered, hail and frost bring ruin,
ten thousand things wither and die prematurely,
and the rivers run dry—
these are the consequences of excess, the transformation of excess,
and the harm of excess.

If one cannot correct these errors,
then human misfortune from excess follows.

This is indeed something to fear.

Laozi said:
*'When the world is about to lose its nature and essence,
it is like the rise of yin energy—
the ruler is dim-witted and unclear,
the Dao is abandoned and not practiced,
virtue is extinguished and not upheld.
Actions defy Heaven, decrees contradict the seasons.
Spring and autumn shrink from harmony,
Heaven and Earth withdraw their blessings,
the ruler sits uneasily in his position,
the ministers withdraw in seclusion and remain silent,
the officials follow the ruler’s whims and abandon tradition,
they alienate their kin while flattering themselves,
flatterers scheme in secret, mimicking the ruler’s chaos,
and the people complete their ruin.
Thus, the ruler and minister grow estranged and untrusting,
kin become distant and unfaithful,
fields yield no grain, roads are deserted,
treasures lose their luster, jade becomes worthless,
tortoiseshells have no inner markings, divination fails daily,
and though the world is one in name, it is not truly united.

The feudal lords enact laws that differ in every state,
customs become perverse,
roots are uprooted, foundations abandoned,
the Five Punishments are sharpened into cruelty,
they quarrel over trifles while slaughtering half the people,
raise armies for war, assault cities, and massacre indiscriminately,
overturning the high to secure the low, deploying massive chariots,
building towering ramparts, clearing battlefields,
forcing soldiers into death traps,
clashing with stern enemies time and again—
their fame may swell, their conquests vast,
but the dead pile up by the tens of thousands,
and the old and weak perish in hunger and cold beyond count.'"

From this point onward, the world has never once known peace in life and death,
nor has it found joy in its customs.

The Yang gives birth while the Yin kills—
the two *qi* shift in turn.
The rise and fall of states are indeed fated.

Yet when the six *qi* cycles complete and one Yin begins anew,
the *flood* of *Gonggong* serves as a warning,
and for nine years, calamity ensues.
Unless a sage like *Emperor Yao*, *Emperor Shun*, or *Emperor Yu* arises,
the lineage of such rulers will be extinguished.

Thus, rise and fall are fated,
order and chaos depend on human action.

When worthy sages emerge and uphold *the Dao/Way of virtue/power*,
reinforced by the Seven *Rites of Virtue*,
they advance the near with wisdom,
win the distant with their virtue,
uniting the world into one,
with descendants succeeding in governance,
rejecting the origins of slanderous flatterers,
silencing the specious debates of petty sophists,
abolishing the harsh *Five Punishments*,
removing burdensome and oppressive affairs,
banishing the traces of idle gossip,
sealing the gates of factional cliques,
dulling sharp wit,
following great constancy,
ignoring bodily refinements,
discarding cleverness,
all things return to their roots in grand harmony.

Sages do not create the times—
they act when the times arrive without faltering,
and thus the course is never broken.

Ancient rulers established their teachings,
with the *Three Exalted Ministers* expounding the *Dao/Way*,
harmonizing *Yin-Yang* principles,
preserving what was lost,
restoring what was disordered.

When sages arise, they uphold *the Dao/Way of virtue/power*,
reinforced by the Seven *Rites of Virtue*,
dismissing treacherous flatterers,
removing burdensome and oppressive affairs,
banishing the traces of idle gossip,
sealing the gates of factional cliques,
uniting the world into one household,
with descendants succeeding in governance.

The *Laozi* said:
*"The depth of the *Gonggong flood*,
ten fathoms deep yet untainted by dust,
gold and jade within its heart manifest on the surface—
it is not shallow nor unclean.
Yet fish, turtles, serpents, and dragons cannot dwell there.
No grains grow on barren stone,
no deer roam the treeless hills—
there is no shelter nor refuge.
Thus, governance marked by severity is deemed discerning,
by ruthlessness as clarity,
by harshness as loyalty,
by scheming as merit—
such rule is like spreading wild grass,
a path to utter ruin and collapse.*

When its governance is stifling, its people are pure;
when its governance is probing, its people are depleted.

Water too clear, fish and turtles dare not enter,
for fear of the nets and traps that harm them.
Barren mountains, deer and elk refuse to dwell,
losing the shelter and protection that brings them peace.
Stone yields no grains, for it lacks the earth to root them.

In the *末世 (last age)*, rulers who govern with severity deemed as discernment,
who probe as clarity,
who oppress as loyalty,
and who scheme as merit—
such rulers are not chosen by enlightened *明主 (sages)*.

The *Laozi* said:
*"Govern with rectitude (*正*), wage war with the unexpected (*奇*).
First establish a governance that cannot be overcome,
then seek victory over enemies.
To attack a land not yet governed is like responding to fire with fire,
or water with water—
they cannot harmonize to resolve each other.

Thus, what is different is deemed the unexpected (*奇*).
The unexpected that is still is called restless (*躁*),
the unexpected in governance is called chaos (*乱*),
the unexpected in abundance is called hunger (*饥*),
the unexpected in ease is called toil (*劳*),
and the unexpected in rectitude, when met with its opposite,
is like water, fire, metal, and wood clashing in destruction—
where shall they go without being overcome?

Hence, when *德 (virtue/power)* is balanced, the many overcome the few,
when strength is matched, the wise subdue the foolish,
and when wisdom is equal, those with strategy capture the countless.

The *Book of Wenzi* begins with the *皇 (August Sovereign)* and ends with the *霸 (Hegemon)*—
its teachings on sovereign rule, kingship, and hegemony.

To govern with rectitude and wage war with the unexpected—
this is the universal principle of antiquity and the present.

Yet the *Hegemon* does otherwise:
he wages war with the unexpected,
and governs with the unexpected as well—
thus his governance becomes the unexpected itself,
and his benevolence turns to *妖 (bewitching/chaotic)*.

Alas, those who govern without rectitude,
yet make the unexpected their sole concern—
what use have they for many schemes?

The *Twelfth Chapter of the *通玄真经 (Scripture on the Perfection of the Dao)** concludes.
